# Rechargeable Batteries for Grid Scale Energy Storage

Zhengxin Zhu, $^{\nabla}$  Taoli Jiang, $^{\nabla}$  Mohsin Ali, $^{\nabla}$  Yahan Meng, Yang Jin, Yi Cui, and Wei Chen*

![](images/537b590597916853fcaad81337f0ff978b8a40566600dd513e8f5f811eb766f7.jpg)

Cite This: Chem. Rev. 2022, 122, 16610-16751

![](images/4745c56b1611ff444796c95870eef6525438f0404d1c811410171cb6a57558b7.jpg)

Article Recommendations

ABSTRACT: Ever-increasing global energy consumption has driven the development of renewable energy technologies to reduce greenhouse gas emissions and air pollution. Battery energy storage systems (BESS) with high electrochemical performance are critical for enabling renewable yet intermittent sources of energy such as solar and wind. In recent years, numerous new battery technologies have been achieved and showed great potential for grid scale energy storage (GSES) applications. However, their practical applications have been greatly impeded due to the gap between the breakthroughs achieved in research laboratories and the industrial applications. In addition, various complex applications call for different battery performances. Matching of diverse batteries to various applications is required to promote practical energy storage research achievement. This review provides in-

depth discussion and comprehensive consideration in the battery research field for GSES. The overall requirements of battery technologies for practical applications with key parameters are systematically analyzed by generating standards and measures for GSES. We also discuss recent progress and existing challenges for some representative battery technologies with great promise for GSES, including metal-ion batteries, lead-acid batteries, molten-salt batteries, alkaline batteries, redox-flow batteries, metal-air batteries, and hydrogen-gas batteries. Moreover, we emphasize the importance of bringing emerging battery technologies from academia to industry. Our perspectives on the future development of batteries for GSES applications are provided.

![](images/d8156a41454aaa76a05ed13a3263b8ea9357eccf1270cbfdb540357907c5d2db.jpg)

# 1. INTRODUCTION

# 1.1. Current Situation and Future Development Trends of Global Electricity

The rampant demand for electricity has been a consequence of increased industrial development, arising from the escalating worldwide utilization of electronic appliances, electric vehicles, air conditioners, and so on. According to the International Energy Agency (IEA) and Enerdata, the consumption of global electricity has accelerated at a whopping rate of  $\sim 3.5\%$  in the past decade, reaching above 22 000 TWh in 2018, and its generation capacity has surpassed 25 000 TWh. Currently, coal is the largest non-renewable energy source contributing up to  $38\%$  of electricity generation, followed by natural gas  $(23\%)$  and oil  $(3\%)$ . In contrast, renewable energy sources account for considerably small percentages for hydropower  $(17\%)$ , wind energy  $(5\%)$ , and solar energy  $(2\%)$  (Figure 1a). Apropos nonrenewable sources, the calculation of energy reserves-to-production  $(\mathrm{R / P})$  ratio has shown that the global oil reserves in 2018 accounted for 50 years of current electricity production, with the highest and lowest R/P ratios for South and Central America (136 years) and Europe (11 years), respectively. Similarly, gas reserves in 2018 amounted to 50.9 years of present production, with leading R/P ratios for Middle East (109.9 years) and Commonwealth of Independent States (CIS, 75.6 years). As for coal reserves, they amounted to 132 years of existing energy production capacity with North America (342 years) and CIS (329 years) having the highest R/P ratios.

The uneven geographical distribution of fossil fuels is one of the main obstacles hindering sustainable future power generation.[5,6] Another major concern arising from fossil fuel-based energy generation is the exponential rise in carbon footprint, which is a consequence of harmful byproducts that are released into the atmosphere during fossil fuel consumption.[7] For instance, sulfur dioxide, nitrogen oxides, carbon dioxide  $(\mathrm{CO}_{2})$ , and a variety of heavy metal particulates can lead to water, air, and soil pollution as well as global warming.[8,9] It is imperative to point out that human activities alone are speculated to have contributed to about  $1.0~^\circ \mathrm{C}$  more global warming compared to the preindustrial levels. If continued at the current rate, the global temperature is estimated to increase up to  $1.5~^\circ \mathrm{C}$  between 2030 and 2052.[10,11] The United Nations Climate Change Conference in Paris (2015) has set the framework for a rapid global shift to a renewable energy system, ensuring a realistic solution to achieve sustainable development and avoid catastrophic climate change. In this regard, solar and wind are sought as clean, abundant, and widely distributed renewable energy resources.[12] The widespread installation of large-scale and high-capacity renewable energy generation units is projected to provide  $86\%$  of the global electricity supply by 2030.

emphasis has been laid on phasing out coal-fired power generation altogether, thereby eliminating the largest root cause of  $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$  emissions.

# 1.2. Developing Renewable Sources for the Electrical Grid

The market share of renewable energy sources in the global power generation marketplace has grown dramatically since 2011, which is ascribed to the recent development and dissemination of wind and solar PV technologies, as supported by set climate policies. $^{13}$  For instance, the globally installed solar PV generation capacity has jumped from 72 GW in 2011 to 585 GW in 2021 (Figure 2a). $^{14}$  Similarly, the worldwide onshore and offshore wind generation capacities have experienced an upward spike from 216 GW in 2010 to 769 GW in 2021, as shown in Figure 2b. $^{15}$  Therefore, the convenience of scaling up and increasingly competitive supply chains have rendered solar and

wind power generation technologies inexpensive and hence economical in the past decade. For example, the cost of electricity from utility-scale solar PV has declined by  $13\%$  in 2019 based on year-on-year development, reaching  $\$0.068$ kWh $^{-1}$  in the United States. $^{15}$  Furthermore, for projects commissioned in 2019, the overall weighted-average levelized cost of electricity computed for both onshore and offshore wind power generation technologies has declined by  $9\%$  on a year-on-year basis, reaching  $\$0.053$ kWh $^{-1}$  and  $\$0.115$ kWh $^{-1}$ , respectively. $^{15}$

However, solar and wind are not constant and hence are unreliable power sources owing to their intermittent nature, which is at odds with continuous demands. The voltage and frequency of wind power are heavily dependent on instantaneous transitions in weather and seasons. Similarly, solar power is generated only during the daytime and also varies with the

Table 1. Characteristics of Various Energy Storage Devices  

<table><tr><td>technology</td><td>total project cost (USD kWh-1)</td><td>lifetime (years/cycles)</td><td>EE (%)</td><td>self-discharge</td><td>MW deployed (global)</td></tr><tr><td>PSH</td><td>100–200</td><td>30–60 years</td><td>65–80</td><td>very low</td><td>169557</td></tr><tr><td>CAES</td><td>100–250</td><td>20–40 years</td><td>40–70</td><td>high</td><td>407</td></tr><tr><td>flywheels</td><td>4000–12000</td><td>15–20 years</td><td>80–90</td><td>very high</td><td>931</td></tr><tr><td>redox-flow battery</td><td>500–1200</td><td>12000–14000 cycles</td><td>60–85</td><td>low</td><td>72</td></tr><tr><td>Li-ion battery</td><td>200–500</td><td>1000–10000 cycles</td><td>85–95</td><td>low</td><td>1629</td></tr><tr><td>Na-S battery</td><td>500–1200</td><td>2000–4500 cycles</td><td>75–90</td><td>very low</td><td>189</td></tr><tr><td>lead-acid battery</td><td>100–600</td><td>200–2500 cycles</td><td>70–80</td><td>medium</td><td>75</td></tr></table>

intensity of solar radiation and seasons. Figure 2c,d illustrates the weekly and monthly distribution of solar and wind energy, respectively, in United Kingdom,[16] showing their inconsistencies with electricity demands. Furthermore, it is evident that solar energy production achieves higher values in the summer, whereas the wind generation reaches its summit during the winter. For example, wind sources are concentrated in the midwest regions, while the solar radiations are dominant in southwest regions in the United States.[17,18]

The traditional complex power distribution grid system requires a series of auxiliary services to ensure its stable and reliable operation. Simultaneously, numerous energy storage systems are also needed to balance electricity generation and demand in real time.[19] In addition, due to uncertain fluctuations in electricity demand, the flexibility of such systems and services, which enables grid operators to respond to unexpected rise in demand or loss in supply, is of paramount importance.[20] In modern power systems, existing solar and wind power plants still have limited impact on grid systems. However, with the exponential growth of renewable sources in the next 30 years, a grid with insufficient energy storage would cause sharp fluctuations ranging from seconds to hours or even longer durations, which in turn may damage the grid and consequently interrupt the continuous power supply and consumption. Therefore, substantial efforts are being devoted to the development of effective electrical energy storage systems (EESSs) to compensate for the inflexible features of the grids.[21]

# 1.3. Development Status of EESSs for the Electrical Grid

Since the invention of the lead-acid battery (LAB) in 1859 followed by its integration into electrical grids in the 1880s, various types of EESSs for grid scale energy storage (GSES) have been continuously deployed.[22,23] Figure 3a displays a timeline of key stages in the development of various GSES systems. In addition to pumped storage hydropower (PSH), most energy storage technologies still have many limited applications. Although several technologies have been studied for a long time, they are still far from meeting the requirements of GSES. The characteristics of major deployed energy storage technologies are further summarized in Table 1, including PSH,[24] compressed air energy storage (CAES),[25] flywheels,[26] hydrogen energy,[27] thermal energy storage (TES),[28] rechargeable batteries,[29] and so forth. According to the requirements of energy storage scenarios for installing capacity and operation frequency, it can be generally divided into uninterruptible power supply (UPS), seasonal storage, frequency regulation, integration of renewable energy, household energy storage, and electric energy time shift. Figure 3b shows the most cost-efficient current technologies for energy storage scenarios based on their respective characteristics.[30] It is evident that rechargeable batteries with highly flexible and wide energy storage ranges have utility for short-, medium- and long-term storage applications.[21]

As the primary grid-scale storage technology, PSH has the potential to deploy energy storage with a massive power capacity approaching up to 170 GW, which accounts for  $\sim 96\%$  of worldwide energy storage. Such a system stores energy by pumping water between two reservoirs located at varying heights. The stored gravitational potential energy depends on the water volume stored in the upper reservoir and the height difference between reservoirs. Previously, PSH has been widely utilized for high power rating ( $>10\mathrm{MW}$ ) in medium- and long-term storage, exhibiting complete discharge times from several hours to a few days at a low rate of  $\sim 2\%$  per day. The currently well-established PSH plants are more technically mature for providing extensive operational experience. In general, the energy efficiencies (EEs) of PSH range between  $65\%$  and  $80\%$ , and they have an extensive lifetime spanning over a period of 50 years, which upon major refurbishment can be further extended. Regardless, the PSH systems offer potential downsides in terms of the geographic and hydrological restrictions, low energy density, long construction period, and high initial capital expense. Consequently, traditional PSH energy storage systems are gradually being replaced with the increased penetration of variable renewable energy (VRE) in small- and medium-scale wind and solar PV based electricity distribution facilities.

Apropos CAES systems, the energy is stored in the form of compressed air (potential elastic energy) in a large-volume reservoir while power is directed toward an electric motor that drives a series of compressors for storing energy to charge the CAES system.[32] Upon retaining heat from the air pressure, this system can achieve up to  $70\%$  EE, which otherwise falls within the range of  $42 - 55\%$ .[33] Despite their use for high power rating with medium- and long-term energy storage and widespread attention, only a handful number of large-scale plants have been successfully integrated into the grid. In this regard, the stringent geographical requirements, high self-discharge, and low EE are to blame.[34]

In contrast to PSH and CAES systems, flywheels constitute electromechanical energy storage for a short duration of only a few minutes, rendering them suitable for short-term storage applications. Concerning flywheels, a variable frequency motor is harnessed for electrical to kinetic energy conversion. The biggest advantage of flywheels is their ability to offer superior cycling life and fast response times, which are highly sought after in certain critical applications.

A TES system mainly gains thermal energy from renewables and waste heat, which can reduce the dependence from heat and cold production to the grid, release its capacity and improve the flexibility.[35] In addition, TES can reduce the volatility from renewable energy to the power grid through the conversion between electricity and thermal energy.[36] However, currently, the high cost and stability of storage materials are the major challenges to grid application.[37]

![](images/21a2dbde6ad5dc80caa5ba13a3b496202761da04306a74266aa860e2d7c41e04.jpg)

Hydrogen energy includes hydrogen generation through electrocatalytic water splitting, hydrogen storage and transport, and hydrogen conversion into electricity through fuel cells and is also important to the future grid.[38] Although hydrogen is a green and carbon-free energy, its production, storage, and utilization costs should be greatly reduced for grid applications.[39]

# 1.4. Significance of Rechargeable Battery Technologies for the Electrical Grid

For over a century, PSH has been the dominant energy storage technology in the power sector. As shown in Figure 4a, the United States had significantly more operating PSH systems, most of which were installed in the 1970s and early 1980s, than other energy storage systems.[40] In real-life applications, however, limitations of using a single energy storage technology have been gradually recognized. In many instances, the diverse

critical requirements such as suitable construction period and location, fast response time, and wide range of power levels and durations hold precedence over the performance limits of EESSs. $^{41}$  Therefore, there is considerable global demand for hybrid energy storage technologies to optimize cost and comprehensive performance for the electrical grid. In this regard, many battery technologies are expected to play key roles. $^{42}$

Unlike PSH and CAES, rechargeable batteries provide indispensable advantages of short construction periods and compact size, rendering them well-suited for utility at various distributed locations. The BESSs typically include Li-ion batteries (LIBs), $^{43,44}$  Na-S batteries, $^{45}$  lead-acid batteries (LABs), $^{46}$  and redox-flow batteries (RFBs). $^{47}$  In addition, there are a number of battery concepts awaiting widespread

![](images/56166d854a2676b5af1a11252a4bd90bfdc006764624885866334a47a4bf74ed.jpg)  
a Outstanding performance advantages in some aspects but with obvious shortcoming

![](images/b39e3b1c837035ff97291d0ea14170fdcc701e41d7ef7b62222505e604597dec.jpg)  
b Potential safety hazards in battery

![](images/c6ed2f17a043b9b6b8f19e8fa6a8878fef70fe00c18cbbb3b2391904faa0cddd.jpg)

![](images/4d2683cc4acbea0740cd4164791f66ea5f9235ab90883b647aa17eeecef74b63.jpg)  
Figure 6. Gap between academia and industry in batteries for GSES. (a) Schematic of the Cannikin law in battery industry. (b) Potential battery safety issues. (c) Potential battery cost issues. (d) Potential preparation process issues. (e) Energy density gap between academia and industry in batteries. (f) Potential electrochemical performance deterioration after energy density of prototype batteries reaches industrial standards.

![](images/44edf9c42c350b4ef5c6365927e962acee07697412ff80b206abec2de7cd8e70.jpg)

![](images/43e3cf607af78852e03beef7f09655eb6199b3f8854246b71e102219f83aae75.jpg)

commercialization like metal-ion, metal-air, liquid-metal, and hydrogen gas batteries, with immense inherent potential for further development. During the last two decades, the deployment of battery energy storage has grown explosively. In the United States, the battery delivered energy capacity has been continuously on the rise since 2011, reaching up to 1000 MWh in 2018,  $91\%$  of which comes from LIBs (Figure 4b).<sup>31,40</sup> At present, most of the GSES market is still dominated by the relatively mature LIBs; nevertheless they are insufficient to cater to all kinds of energy storage needs. The publications of academia are the source of inspiration for industry. In recent years, numerous new battery technologies have been achieved and have shown great potential for large scale energy storage (LSES) applications.<sup>48-51</sup> However, their practical applications have been greatly impeded due to the gap between academic research and industrial practices within the domain of LSES.<sup>52-54</sup> During their development, practical manufacturing aspects, such as safety, cost, service life, scalable production, self

discharge, EE, maintenance, and recyclability are generally overlooked owing to the lack of measurement standards, not to mention that sophisticated energy storage applications call for different battery performances. Therefore, apt and diverse battery designs are required for further promoting practical energy storage research. In this regard, a comprehensive and timely guideline in the battery research field for GSES is urgently needed.

# 2. GENERAL GUIDELINES FOR BATTERY TECHNOLOGIES FOR GRID SCALE ENERGY STORAGE

# 2.1. Key Requirements of Battery Technologies for GSES

Comprehensive requirements for battery technologies need to be fully met before they can be transferred to industrial production, which is the biggest difference between academia and industry. These requirements include cost, safety, service

life, EE, energy density, self-discharge performance, operating temperature range, recyclability, and so on (Figure 5). Although the projected cost promises widespread adoption of BESSs, it is not easy to quantify the total investment cost of a BESS. Generally, the total investment includes the cost of chemicals, the power conversion system (PCS), plant balance, construction, and commissioning, among others.[40,55] In particular, the chemical cost is an approximate measure required for each battery technology. Currently, the energy storage cost target proposed by the U.S. Department of Energy (DoE) is  $100 \mathrm{kWh}^{-1}$ .[56-58] The total investment cost of existing BESSs is unable to meet the adoption requirement. The total lifetime cost for an energy storage system divided by its cumulative delivered electricity is a more appropriate measure. Such a measure for BESS cost assessment is called levelized cost of storage (LCOS), as shown below, which is described as the total lifetime cost divided by the discharged electricity during the operational period. The total lifetime cost includes investment cost, operation and maintenance (O&M) cost, charging, and end-of-life costs. The equation assumes all initial investment costs and sums ongoing costs in each year ( $n$ ) up to the lifetime ( $N$ ) of the BESS and is discounted by the discount rate ( $r$ ). Therefore, the cycle/calendar life of BESSs should be more than 5000 cycles or 10 years for high annualized return.[30,59]

$$
\begin{array}{l} \text {L C O S} [ \mathbb {S} / \text {M W h} ] = \left[ \text {i n v e s t m e n t c o s t} + \sum_ {n} ^ {N} \frac {\mathrm {O} \& \mathrm {M} \text {c o s t}}{(1 + r) ^ {n}} \right. \\ \left. + \sum_ {n} ^ {N} \frac {\text {c h a r g i n g c o s t}}{(1 + r) ^ {n}} + \sum_ {n} ^ {N} \frac {\text {e n d - o f - l i f e c o s t}}{(1 + r) ^ {N + 1}} \right] \\ \left/ \left[ \sum_ {n} ^ {N} \frac {\text {e l e c d i s c h a r g e d c o s t}}{(1 + r) ^ {n}} \right]\left. \right. \tag {1} \\ \end{array}
$$

With ongoing proliferation of projects, industries are prioritizing safety features of batteries in GSES. Such realization has come from the past incidents, including a sodium-sulfur battery fire in Japan in September 2011, a LIB blaze in Arizona in April 2019, and nearly 30 BESS fire occurrences reported in South Korea until 2020.[60-62] This shows that safety is indispensable during the entire period of installation, commissioning, operation, maintenance, and decommissioning of BESS and holds precedence over its widespread adoption. Concerning electrochemical performance, the round-trip efficiency (RTE) of a BESS must exceed  $75\%$  to surpass that of PSH while avoiding energy loss. It is imperative to mention that the low energy density of BESSs tends to occupy more cell volume and adds to its manufacturing and installation costs. However, unlike mobile devices (e.g., electric vehicles) with stringent design and performance requirements, an energy density over  $50 \mathrm{Wh} \mathrm{kg}^{-1}$  is sufficient for BESS stationary applications.[63] Similar to energy density, self-discharge is also one of the primary indexes to determine the cost-efficiency of BESS and, ideally it should not exceed  $1\%$  per day.[42] Furthermore, batteries with wide operating temperature range, from  $-30$  to  $60^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$ , and minimal maintenance needs conveniently qualify for widespread commercialization.[64] Last but not least, substantial room for the recyclability of constituent raw materials of end-of-life batteries is also conducive for deploying the related energy storage technology. We compared the four current primary energy storage technologies, including sodium sulfur batteries (NSBs), vanadium RFBs (VRFBs), LIBs and LABs, in terms of

key performance parameters. Obviously, each technology has its own advantages and disadvantages, for example, LIBs have excellent comprehensive performance but limited lithium resources and insufficient safety, while LABs have low cost and high safety but short service life.

# 2.2. Gap between Academia and Industry in Batteries for GSES

Despite academic emphasis on laboratory research and industry emphasis on transforming technologies into economical consumer products, the common mission ought to work together for achieving a clean energy blueprint. For commercializing and meeting battery performance standards, it is relatively more important for a BESS to exhibit a well-balanced combination of all electrochemical performance parameters than to demonstrate superior performance in one parameter only (Figure 6a). However, it has been observed that the performance values reported in most of the research studies on novel battery chemistries are somewhat overestimated due to the lack of standards and measures.[54,65] Such misrepresentation therefore leads to the obvious gap between academic research and industrial attempts to commercialize BESS technologies with mass production.[53,66] Additionally, the contradictory objectives of research practitioners and industry experts also hamper the possibility for widespread commercial manufacturing of BESSs.

In fact, it is necessary to determine whether the research object is intrinsically safe or potential safety hazards can be solved by appropriate solutions before the start of practical battery research (Figure 6b). Reliable safety is the presumption for the battery technology to be used for GSES. In addition, in many academic investigations, only impeccable electrochemical performance is sought, whereas the battery cost and preparation process issues are mostly disregarded. The ignored costs often include the use of expensive chemicals in battery components (active materials, separator, electrolytes, solvents, additives, and so on; Figure 6c). For example, a battery system may involve expensive metals (such as rare earth elements, precious metal elements, or other metal elements with limited reserves) or expensive battery components with high manufacturing cost (such as ion-exchange membranes (IEM) or micro-nanomaterials). Preparation process issues are also a huge obstacle to industrial application. These issues include processes involving many hazardous chemicals or expensive manufacturing equipment, processes that are hard to scale up, poor manufacturing consistency, time-consuming processes, low yield of design materials, and so on (Figure 6d).

In addition, the preparation processes for materials and battery stacks are also a huge obstacle to industrial application. Lab-level fabrication of batteries has typically paid less attention to the cost and energy consumption of electrode materials synthesis and their compatibility with industrial production lines, which are crucial for practical large-scale applications.[67]

Syntheses with complex technologies that operate under harsh conditions with rare ingredients and rigorous circumstances should be largely avoided in the mass production of batteries for GSES. Instead, facile, large-scale, energy-saving, pollution-free, and low-cost methodologies should be encouragingly implemented throughout the entire battery fabrication process.[68] The internal situations of commercial-level batteries exhibit discrepancies with the lab-level ones and the major issues in the former are generally different from that in the latter. The existing problems will be enlarged, and elaborate optimizations at lab

level may not work well in practical applications.[69] Therefore, effective ex situ and in situ battery diagnostic techniques should be applied to the battery stack and container for large-scale applications.[70,71] In addition, battery management is an absolute necessity in GSES applications. Different from lab-level technologies, the battery management systems for practical applications with high safety must be sensitive enough to compensate for nonuniform aging in thermal gradients or production mismatch during battery stack balancing.[69] Meanwhile, the guidelines and measures should be applied to the battery scale-up process.

After making it clear that the above conditions meet the requirements, we still need to eliminate the electrochemical performance gap between academia and industry. First, the unmentioned active materials of laboratory synthesized battery prototypes pose substantial risk for creating misunderstanding pertaining to energy density evaluation. In order to obtain high specific capacity, low polarization, and stable electrode structure, the emphasis is placed on designing batteries with low mass loading; unreasonable negative/positive (N/P) ratio, high concentrations of additive content and electrolyte amount, and excess inactive materials, not to mention that the mass of industrial battery modules, has not been considered here (Figure 6e,f). For example, Betz et al.[72] employed a six-step process to demonstrate the effect of relative mass distribution of an operating Li-S battery component on its specific energy and energy density calculations. After carrying out a series of critical analyses of realistic issues and inactive components, the practical energy density of a 18650 Li-S cell was computed to be nearly 10 times lower than its theoretical value. Similarly, Liu and coworkers[73] investigated the influence of electrolyte amount and mass loading of electrodes for determining the cycle stability of NMC622Li coin cells. Upon cycling, it was revealed that much electrolyte and high mass loading of electrodes compromised the cycling stability of the cell. Therefore, the electrochemical performance of batteries meeting the requirements of industrial energy density is more representative. The key electrochemical performance parameters include life, EE, operating current density, and so on. From these examples, it behooves battery research to mention individual parameters (Table 2), including active material ratio (wt %), mass loading

Table 2. Recommendations for Key Reporting Parameters in Applied Battery Science  

<table><tr><td>chemical material cost ($ kg-1)</td><td>material recycling cost ($ kg-1)</td></tr><tr><td>estimated preparation cost ($ PCS-1)</td><td>tracked calendar life (years)</td></tr><tr><td>active material ratio (wt %)</td><td>mass loading (mg cm-2)</td></tr><tr><td>electrolyte amount (mL Ah-1)</td><td>areal capacity (mAh cm-2)</td></tr><tr><td>N/P ratio (%)</td><td>voltage efficiency (%)</td></tr><tr><td>electrode porosity (%)</td><td>average discharge voltage (V)</td></tr><tr><td>electrode density (g cm-3)</td><td>hazardous gases or chemical materials (yes/no)</td></tr><tr><td>current density (mA cm-2)</td><td>operating temperature range (K)</td></tr><tr><td>separator thickness (mm)</td><td>self-discharge (% day-1)</td></tr></table>

$(\mathrm{mg~cm}^{-2})$ , and areal capacity  $(\mathrm{mAh~cm}^{-2})$  of the electrodes, electrolyte concentration  $(\mathrm{mL~Ah}^{-1})$ , electrode density  $(\mathrm{g~cm}^{-3})$ , electrode porosity  $(\%)$ , N/P ratio  $(\%)$ , separator thickness  $(\mu \mathrm{m})$ , and average discharge voltage (V). Moreover, EE losses in BESSs are attributed to losses in Coulombic efficiency  $(\mathrm{CE},\%)$ , voltage efficiency  $(\mathrm{VE},\%)$ , and auxiliary loads  $(\%)$  such as pumps for RFBs. Therefore, losses in VE and

auxiliary loads also need to be reported for comprehensive evaluation of EE (Figure 6). Lastly, the considerations of cell self-discharge performance, operating conditions, and maintenance requirements ought to be incorporated as well.

# 2.3. Scenarios for Batteries in GSES

A deep understanding of energy storage applications is expected to allow design of more targeted BESS technologies with varying characteristics for different applications. Figure 7 illustrates the future grid structure from energy generation to consumption. The electrical grid is a complex network of electrical generators (i.e., power plants) and transmission and distribution (T&D) lines that dynamically respond to shifts in electrical supply and demand to make sure electricity is always supplied reliably. The BESS has practical and flexible applications in renewable farms, power plants, high voltage transmission lines, distribution stations, microgrids, factories, homes, and so on. However, the performance requirements of battery energy storage technologies are different for various utility examples. In addition to cost, the various performance requirements include reliability (including safety), response time, EE, self-discharge, energy density, cycle life, calendar life, and so on. The main GSES scenarios and their important storage characteristics for different battery technologies are summarized in Table 3. In the future, different technologies may serve the power grid in different application scenarios. Despite these properties, great attention should be devoted to the integration of renewable energy for catering to our current and future energy needs. A battery technology that has no obvious shortcoming is deeply expected for GSES.

# 2.4. Promising Battery Technologies for GSES

Notwithstanding their supreme electrochemical performance, the four main battery energy storage technologies (LIBs, NSBs, LABs, and VRFBs) still face respective shortcomings.[74-76] To mitigate corresponding challenges for these BESSs, innumerable studies have been carried out on introducing novel battery designs and compositions.[49,50,75-77] Figure 8 represents noteworthy and groundbreaking developments in the field of BESSs for GSES applications. Recently, it can be seen that the low-cost hydrogen gas battery (HGB) and organic aqueous RFB (ARFB) technologies have emerged to be very competitive contenders owing to their efficacy in power grid energy storage.[49,78] However, the assessment of feasibility of such technologies through in-depth analysis is urgently needed.

In order to help the research community obtain a better understanding of stationary emerging energy systems and accelerate their development, we present a comprehensive review of recently developed, inexpensive, high performance, and inherently safe energy storage technologies. This topical review includes accounts of the operating principles, chemistries, material challenges, and current development status of mature and emerging battery technologies. With no intention to cover all potentially known battery chemistries, this review exclusively focuses on delineating frontier research and up-to-date advancements of metal-ion batteries (MIBs), lead-acid batteries (LABs), molten-salt batteries (MSBs), alkaline batteries (ABs), redox flow batteries (RFBs), metal-air batteries (MABs), and hydrogen-gas batteries (HGBs). In recent years, many novel battery designs have been derived to make up for the shortcomings of battery technologies. The practicability, potential, new challenges, and current development status need an accurate evaluation and tracking. Therefore, this review summarizes a large number of representative battery tech

# 3. METAL-ION BATTERIES

MIBs have experienced extensive development among smart storage devices. However, they still confront many difficulties, such as high manufacturing costs and limited number of reasonable electrodes and electrolytes for GSES. Figure 9 displays a schematic for a MIB composed of an anode, a cathode, an electrolyte, a separator, and current collectors. The electrodes are capable of extracting and inserting metal ions. In the battery charge state, the metal ions through the electrolyte flow from the cathode to the anode where they are stored. In the battery

discharge state, metal ions transport from the anode to the cathode and release the energy. Here, the characteristics of different MIBs (mainly  $\mathrm{Li^{+}}$ ,  $\mathrm{Na^{+}}$ ,  $\mathrm{K^{+}}$ ,  $\mathrm{Zn^{2+}}$ ,  $\mathrm{Mg^{2+}}$ , and  $\mathrm{Al^{3+}}$ ) are summarized in Table 4. According to the comparison data in Table 4, sodium ions and potassium ions, contributing to batteries with a single-electron transfer, show higher crustal abundance compared to lithium for commercial LIBs. In addition, in comparison with commercial LIBs, battery systems using multivalent ions (e.g.,  $\mathrm{Zn^{2+}}$ ,  $\mathrm{Mg^{2+}}$ , and  $\mathrm{Al^{3+}}$ ) can achieve higher volumetric capacity and lower cost due to multiple electron transfer redox reactions and abundant resources.[79] Furthermore, these nontoxic multivalent metals are highly stable and inactive when exposed to air compared with metallic Li, Na, and K, which contributes to their higher safety and easier handling for practical applications.

![](images/6a8919f3e897afe4cf704f870b57839ae0edd1f93658e4b016f8b23f70552716.jpg)  
Figure 8. Directions in development of advanced BESS in terms of different technologies, including metal-ion batteries, lead-acid batteries, molten-salt batteries, alkaline batteries, redox-flow batteries, metal-air batteries, and hydrogen gas batteries.

![](images/ad16db108b84bbcbeee6c5d9f25a478031541fff5b89b83b59b7ffe5c25b6897.jpg)  
Figure 9. Schematic of MIBs. A = Li, Na, K, Zn, Mg, or Al;  $x = 1$ , 2, or 3.

# 3.1. Current Lithium-Ion Batteries in GSES

Since their development by Sony Corporation in the early 1990s, rechargeable LIBs have rapidly become one of the most important technologies for mobile consumer electronics.[80] LIBs

exchange  $\mathrm{Li^{+}}$  between the anode and the cathode. They usually have a cathode made of a lithium metal oxide or phosphate (e.g.,  $\mathrm{LiCoO_2}$  (LCO),  $\mathrm{LiMn_2O_4}$  (LMO),  $\mathrm{LiNi_{1 / 3}Co_{1 / 3}Mn_{1 / 3}}$ , and  $\mathrm{LiFePO_4}$  (LFP)), while the anode is often made of graphite or lithium titanate. All LIB chemistries have the advantage of yielding high energy density, above  $250\mathrm{Whkg^{-1}}$ , relative to other battery technologies.[31] They also exhibit high rate, excellent RTE, relatively long lifetime, and low self-discharge rate. The issues of thermal stability and safety of LIBs relate to chemical reactions that release oxygen when the cathodes overheat. The so-called "thermal runaway" may cause leaks, smoke venting, and even cell fire. These inherent risks of LIBs are typically triggered by external influences (not related to design), such as external heating conditions, overcharging, or high-current fast charging. Therefore, much effort is being directed toward the improvement of safety of LIBs through integrated thermal management and monitoring processes.[31]

The battery cost is an essential factor for developing advanced energy storage systems.[81] The overall battery cost mainly consists of modules, containers, power components, and thermal management systems, which also depend on the size and complexity of a specific project. The current global market for grid-scale battery storage is still dominated by lithium-ion chemistries.[82] Due to the technological innovations and improved manufacturing capacity, lithium-ion chemistries have experienced a steep price decline of over  $45\%$  from 2014 to 2017, with a further decrease from 2017 to 2020 (Figure 10a). The cathode cost makes up to  $31 - 39\%$  of the total material cost in LIBs, which amounts to  $10 - 23\%$  of the total battery energy storage system cost.[82] It is worth noting that as energy density improves, the share of material cost in the total cost falls, representing an important cost reduction avenue, which may outweigh raw material cost reductions from manufacturing process improvements. Much research, therefore, prioritizes increasing the overall cell kilowatt-hour output, either through the increased efficiencies or chemistry innovation.[83] For instance, the LCO cathode has become a standardized material, which is used to a large extent for consumer portable electronic

![](images/8139bbfc7af97e16d6cdd6e890fa102986310a604f8651eafa2cc6e465dc97d5.jpg)  
a

![](images/0f851be26aa4b3c01f0f92fed3706c57b2fc73a30aaebba57fc4a27cba716bf8.jpg)  
b

![](images/f8b2bedbc206c45335af5608f47572942046978aef9e57132c01dec4d0b58fd4.jpg)  
C  
Figure 10. (a) Lowest projected battery price in different types of cells for utility-scale applications. (b) Lowest cell price of LIB chemistries for utility-scale applications. (c) LIB pack-level costs based on the observed and projected LIB demands. Data from International Renewable Energy Agency.

devices and electric vehicles. Figure 10b suggests a cost of about  $200\mathrm{kWh}^{-1}$  for lithium-cobalt chemistries and a fast decrease for other developed cathodes. This acceleration in GSES system deployments has been enabled by the dramatic decrease in the cost and fast increase in the demand for LIB storage systems over the past decade (Figure 10c).<sup>84</sup> Consequently, energy storage is becoming increasingly cost-competitive with traditional grid assets (such as fossil-fueled power plants) for utility companies to fulfill various needs including reserve capacity, frequency regulation, and transmission investment deferral.<sup>63</sup>

LIBs are expected to experience further cost reductions, which may lead them to become increasingly cost-effective for a variety of grid applications, ensuring further growth in deployment of grid-connected LIB systems. Global manufacturing for LIBs has ramped up considerably and plans for further expanding capacities continue. The annual manufacturing capacity for LIBs today, for all chemistry types, exceeds  $\sim 100$  GWh.[85] There are several examples of BESS deployments for LSES applications. Hornsdale Power Reserve has installed a 100 MW/129 MWh LIB storage facility, which was one of the largest LIB energy storage systems in the world and has been in operation in South Australia since December 2017.[82] The battery energy storage system can bid 30 MW and 119 MWh of its capacity directly into the market. In addition, the new Contego project, in southeast England, assembled 28 Tesla megapack batteries for storing energy from renewable sources to be used at times of peak consumption. This 34 MW plant will be one of the United Kingdom's largest energy storage plants. In addition, the United Kingdom has committed to delivering a huge offshore wind program by 2030. Integrating 40 GW of

intermittent wind generation calls for a boom in battery storage to help harness excess energy and transmit electricity when needed.

# 3.2. Future Lithium Battery Chemistry for GSES

The next generation LIB technologies with potentially huge cost and performance benefits could be the answer to reaching the cost and energy density targets for GSES.[83] In this regard, systems employing Li- and Mn-rich (LMR) cathodes,  $5\mathrm{V}$  cathodes, Li metal, and silicon anodes have been extensively explored.[86] In particular, the  $5\mathrm{V}$  cathode materials are considered as potential candidates for next-generation LIBs. This class of materials includes spinel  $\mathrm{LiMn}_{1.5}\mathrm{Ni}_{0.5}\mathrm{O}_4$ , inverse spinel  $\mathrm{LiNiVO}_4$ , and olivine  $\mathrm{LiCoPO}_4$  among others, all possessing a high discharge voltage of  $>4.5\mathrm{~V}$  vs.  $\mathrm{Li} / \mathrm{Li}^+$ .[87] As the energy density is a product of capacity and discharge voltage, this class of materials is envisioned to achieve energy densities well above  $400\mathrm{Whkg}^{-1}$ . One of the main problems associated with such high voltage cathodes is the oxidation of the electrolyte. Traditional carbonate-based electrolytes cannot withstand potentials higher than  $4.3\mathrm{~V}$  vs.  $\mathrm{Li} / \mathrm{Li}^+$  and are normally oxidized when used with  $5\mathrm{~V}$  cathodes. Alternatively, researchers have employed more stable electrolytes such as ionic liquids (ILs), sulfones, nitrile, carbonate derivatives, and carbonates with additives.[82] However,  $5\mathrm{~V}$  cathodes still face similar challenges to lithium metal oxide or phosphate cathodes. For example, similar to the spinel LMO,  $\mathrm{Mn}^{2+}$  dissolution is a concern for the spinel  $\mathrm{LiMn}_{1.5}\mathrm{Ni}_{0.5}\mathrm{O}_4$  cathode especially at high temperatures, while the  $\mathrm{LiCoPO}_4$  olivine cathode suffers from poor electrical conductivity much like its olivine LFP counter

![](images/c14abb7e4520c123364440807dccb5899aa6a0a8211bcd0922506eb14a93007f.jpg)

![](images/9caaf8383595f18d5fc6ba74d597c900114571bde0fbef3f8faf01317b0bfa1d.jpg)

![](images/e03b18703e96152acdefd294cd5d8571ed81c7793577c3c593c46d786da43198.jpg)

Reproduced with permission from ref 115. Copyright 2014 Royal Society of Chemistry. (c, d) SEM images of 3D hierarchical LCO nanorod arrays. (e) Cycling stability of the LCO/LiTi $_2$ PO $_4$ ) $_3@C$  cell. Reproduced with permission from ref 117. Copyright 2019 Elsevier.

![](images/2e2de0779a1fc90f04c0c1c146c098055707c05dd35b20341e5fd4e1f4ca02ad.jpg)

![](images/d9b15f414e3cd251ef7658153202198f564c3b6064f579bacde11ba01648d39c.jpg)

part. $^{87}$  Although the nature of the problems and solutions are similar to their parental archetypes, the  $5\mathrm{V}$  cathode materials have yet to reach the energy market. Another class of materials that employs high voltages is the LMR cathode, which is also known as layer-layered/layered-spinel materials. $^{88}$  This cathode material is remarkable in producing high specific capacity at high voltage with excellent capacity-based cycle stability. However, the charge and discharge voltages and thus energy density decrease substantially over cycling, owing to its well-known voltage fade phenomenon. $^{89}$  The activation process for LMR includes large portions of irreversible capacity, which make it problematic for pairing electrodes in a full cell. $^{89}$  The ultimate problem of the LMR cathode stems from its relatively poor conductivity, which results in rate performances inferior to that of commercial cathodes (NMC, NCA, etc.).

The addition of a small amount of silicon particles into the graphite anode of LIBs can boost the achievable cell energy density, but at the expense of increased mechanical stress during charging as the active material expands.[90] This needs to be overcome before its widespread practicality, as the electrode volume expansion is approximately  $400\%$  during lithiation, reducing cycle life to as few as several hundred cycles. Overcoming low lifetimes of electrodes through improved silicon nanoparticles and electrode designs and enhanced fabrication processes is therefore the current focus of research in this area.[91,92] Strategies of adopting nanowire structures and nanoparticles have resulted in enhanced utilization and stability of Si anodes.[93] The commonly used nanoparticles in literature most likely pose severe challenges in achieving appropriate volumetric energy densities.[94-96] A pioneering company, Amprius Inc., in Silicon Valley California is committed to transforming a  $100\%$  silicon nanowire anode at the laboratory level into practical battery systems for mass production and applications.

Post-Li-ion technologies exploring new approaches toward lithium metal anodes for energy storage have been explored in the past few decades.[97-99] In this respect, one of the most

promising material pathways for increasing energy densities includes lithium-sulfur batteries, using cost-effective and abundant sulfur as an active material for yielding very high energy densities of up to  $400\mathrm{Whkg^{-1}}$ . In addition, lithium-air batteries[101] make use of oxygen (derived from ambient air) as an active cathode material, featuring the highest potential energy and power density of all battery storage systems.[82] Furthermore, the use of only one active material increases the battery's inherent safety and promises improved environmental compatibility. However, three fundamental problems hinder the implementation of lithium metal anodes: (1) dendrite formation leading to short circuits of the batteries, (2) low CE, and (3) unavoidable continuous reactions of the Li metal with the electrolyte solutions in practical cells, which dry out the batteries ultimately.[102,103] Different approaches to limit or completely suppress dendrite growth have been studied and can be broadly divided into SEI related approaches (modifying the Li surface chemistry) and mechanical blocking of dendrite growth.[104] The development of Li-S batteries is still in infancy as these cells must overcome a number of challenges including a high self-discharge rate, low internal conductivity, and low cycle lifetimes before they can be considered for commercialization.[105] So far, there is no ultimate approach to fully solve the above-described problems of Li-S batteries; thus only combined approaches are promising to further this battery technology. In this respect, practical Li-S batteries were produced by Sion Power, and the cell yields an energy density of  $350\mathrm{Whkg^{-1}}$ .[82] Several other commercialization ventures for Li-S batteries by established companies are on the way. For Li-air batteries, many practical problems persist to date such as low storage capacities and high vulnerability to environmental influences, especially humidity.[106] Due to the existing challenges, large-scale commercialization of the Li-air battery is not expected within the next few years.

Since the conception of the aqueous lithium-ion battery (ALIB) by Dahn's group in 1994, the use of aqueous electrolytes has

(d) CV of WIBS and WIS electrolytes on stainless steel WE at a scanning rate of 10 mV s<sup>-1</sup>, and C-TiO<sub>2</sub> anode and LMO cathode at a scanning rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>-1</sup> in the same electrolyte. (e) First charge/discharge profiles of LMO/C-TiO<sub>2</sub> cell in WIS (black) and WIBS (blue); therein, the CEs are labeled in the graph. (f) Cycling stability for varying combinations of TiO<sub>2</sub> anode with electrolytes. Reproduced with permission from ref 123. Copyright 2016 Wiley-VCH. (g) Discharge capacities and CEs of these full cells during cycling. Reproduced with permission from ref 124. Copyright 2019 Springer Nature.

![](images/2d3b83bd85e4db242a03f98447bcaed50d66c067cc351286640e51a6fc18e288.jpg)

![](images/3d78f3aca59b917728b75faeb5a39cba3ec311dcfe05e11a09de6b0fc4b41b7b.jpg)

been recommended as a promising approach to replace the organic electrolytes due to their low cost and high safety. The resulting battery system showed an average operating voltage of  $1.5\mathrm{V}$  with an energy density of  $75\mathrm{Whkg}^{-1}$  while suffering from poor cycling performance. $^{107}$  Since then, a number of ALIB systems using various types of novel electrode materials and electrolytes have been developed to achieve better electrochemical performance. $^{108-110}$

3.3.1. Electrode Materials for ALIBs. For the electrode materials of ALIBs, the cathode usually comprises traditional LFP, LCO, or LMO, and the anodes are often  $\mathrm{LiTi_2(PO_4)_3}$ ,  $\mathrm{VO}_2$ , and  $\mathrm{TiP_2O_7}$ . In general, to restrain the water decomposition, part of the cathode needs to be coated with carbon to eliminate oxygen evolution. Dahn's group reported the first ALIB, which was composed of a  $\mathrm{VO}_2$  anode and the LMO cathode. Following this work, a series of ALIB systems

have been reported, such as  $\mathrm{LiV}_3\mathrm{O}_8 / \mathrm{LCO}$ ,  $\mathrm{TiP}_2\mathrm{O}_7 / \mathrm{LMO}$ , and  $\mathrm{LiTi}_2(\mathrm{PO}_4)_3 / \mathrm{LMO}$ . Nevertheless, the same problem of poor cycling performance has not been completely solved. The difficulty of capacity fading upon cycling has only made limited progress during the past five decades.

Much effort has been devoted to modifying the structure of electrodes to enhance the stability of the battery. For instance, Zheng and co-workers reported a  $\mathrm{Fe_2O_3}$ -decorated polyaniline nanotube as anode of ALIBs, as shown in the scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images of Figure 11a. $^{115}$  The full cell with the LMO cathode and  $\mathrm{Fe_2O_3}$ -decorated polyaniline anode showed superior capacity retention of  $73.3\%$  after over 1000 cycles (Figure 11b). Similarly, Wang and co-workers prepared N-doped carbon coated  $\mathrm{LiTi_2(PO_4)_3}$  composite nanoparticles as an anode for ALIBs by sol-gel and HT pyrolysis methods. $^{116}$  An ALIB using this N-doped carbon coated  $\mathrm{LiTi_2(PO_4)_3}$  anode and

![](images/a045f5339a971a06d77e6425c0dfb9a082cd37b4a2b268d2b963f8f4f396e1a1.jpg)

![](images/0957e51e50b89d8093a137852b317ee691fbf2152d4f434e1e18359c61d83026.jpg)

![](images/3d5b834c2f2fb016376f64297a34a7f064f7a32f7f7661b6f1e86f17da9908ca.jpg)

![](images/48840c763db7b630aaa1c062b522a76f65c46b8d23b2a00e8658803284b1d8fd.jpg)

![](images/cb1c881367c5c445a2b1fb6af0ec327a61f5a51cd8ec5f6e2a05335224a5b0bf.jpg)  
Figure 13. Low-cost and nontoxic nonfluorinated electrolytes for enhancing the range of working voltage of ALIBs. (a) CV curves at  $0.2\mathrm{mV}s^{-1}$  for acetate solutions using Pt foil, Ti foil, and glassy carbon as current collectors. (b) CV curves at  $0.2\mathrm{mV}s^{-1}$  in  $32m$  KOAc-8m LiOAc for  $\mathsf{c - T_iO_2}$  (pink),  $\mathrm{Mo}_6\mathrm{S}_8$  (teal), LFP (blue), and LMO (brown) with Ti current collector and  $\mathsf{c - Li_4Ti_5O_{12}}$  on glassy carbon current collector. (c) Charge/discharge profiles and (d) rate capability of  $\mathsf{c - TiO_2}$ /LMO full cell. Reproduced with permission from ref 126. Copyright 2018 Royal Society of Chemistry. (e) Electrochemical stability windows of the 50 wt % LiPAA electrolyte, as well as CV curves of  $\mathrm{TiO_2}$ ,  $\mathrm{LiTi_2(PO_4)_3}$ , LMO, and  $\mathrm{LiNi_{0.5}Mn_{1.5}O_4}$  on various current collectors at  $0.1\mathrm{mV}s^{-1}$ . (f) Comparison of electrochemical stability windows of ALIBs with various salts. (g) Cycling performance of a  $\mathrm{LiTi_2(PO_4)_3}$ /LMO battery at  $0.5\mathrm{C}$ . Inset: Voltage profiles in different cycles. Reproduced with permission from ref 127. Copyright 2018 Springer Nature.

![](images/651d79a62e8b1f0a42d38a3658e0096986a87012f86c6f0c39f67c08c962f7e8.jpg)

![](images/9cbe9a95f41abf7261827fa794fb960d4f473681310f8cbcb87d74118f945a11.jpg)

a LMO cathode delivered a high capacity of  $95.3\mathrm{mAhg}^{-1}$  at a rate of  $20~\mathrm{C}$  and excellent capacity retention up to  $82.1\%$  of the initial discharge capacity after 1000 cycles. Similarly, the decoration with carbon and  $\mathrm{Fe_2O_3}$  for anode materials has proved effective in improving the cycling performance of ALIBs.

In addition, it is well-known that three-dimensional (3D) frameworks to decorate the electrode structure with freestanding features can improve the rate and cycling performance for LIBs. Therefore, Xia and co-workers synthesized 3D hierarchical LCO nanorod arrays by hydrothermal treatment methods for developing advanced cathodes for ALIBs.[117] As shown in Figure 11c,d, the as-prepared 3D hierarchical LCO nanorod arrays possess a superior structure. When subjected to electrochemical cycling, the aqueous full cell based on the 3D

hierarchical LCO nanorod array cathode and  $\mathrm{LiTi}_2(\mathrm{PO}_4)_3@C$  anode presented good cycle performance with  $86.1\%$  capacity retention after 2000 cycles at 1 C (Figure 11e). The 3D structural frameworks can also accelerate ionic transport and stabilize the whole electrode operation during reversible cycling. Notwithstanding numerous research efforts toward improving the electrode performance in laboratory based ALIBs, there is still a lack of evaluation standards for the battery technology. For example, ALIBs utilizing structurally modified electrode materials show high specific capacity only based on the mass of cathode materials, while the practical energy density is still low when taking into account the mass of the whole battery. Therefore, these challenges call for immediate attention and more research needs to be conducted in this regard.

![](images/de6435ea15a4dbbf447f44d406f41a059f8f6d2de7f9c0ffdf98ba52e0e9b24e.jpg)

![](images/d87b9818f2e040c7209f91af0dd22a803a6cdb0825e27ead2bc9c2609095421c.jpg)

![](images/11f78f30c8e18c6ad050ceac0bd85c008f173e77496e90af47de188ddaa40312.jpg)

![](images/78364958575f4d414187f278c96db8d3c0542508ebc045ea8ef49ff5feac6c33.jpg)

![](images/6ee292d0cab7b2f066e9a839a22f46b6b88e00206056306d16f25515a3516ff8.jpg)  
Figure 14. Electrochemical behavior and OEMS of  $2m$  LiTFSI-94% PEG-6%  $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$  for enhancing the range of working voltage of ALIBs. (a) Electrochemical stability windows for  $2m$  LiTFSI-  $x\mathrm{PEG} - (1 - x)\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$  ( $x = 0,71,80,90,94\%$ ) determined by LSV tests at a scan rate of  $0.2\mathrm{mV}s^{-1}$ . (b) CV curves in  $2m$  LiTFSI-94% PEG-6%  $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$  at  $0.2\mathrm{mVs^{-1}}$  for LMO and L-LTO electrodes. OEMS results of the L-LTO/LMO full cell conducted in LiTFSI-94% PEG-6%  $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$  during the first (c) and tenth (d) cycles at 1 C ( $0.104\mathrm{A g^{-1}}$  for LMO). (e) Voltage profiles of the 1st, 200th, and 300th cycles and (f) cycling stability with CE of the L-LTO/LMO cell in  $2m$  LiTFSI-94% PEG-6%  $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$  at 1 C. Reproduced with permission from ref 128. Copyright 2020 Springer Nature.

![](images/574898e5c064957899537919ce17969ac15ed3ac9060eb6d413bccea41e5d7fe.jpg)

3.3.2. Electrolytes for ALIBs. In order to enhance the stability and enlarge the aqueous battery systems, extensive effort has been devoted to the development of novel electrolytes. In general,  $\mathrm{Li_2SO_4}$  or  $\mathrm{LiNO}_3$  water solution is the most commonly used electrolyte for ALIBs in neutral pH. $^{110}$  Ramanujapuram and Yushin explored the battery performance of the water electrolytes based on three low-cost inorganic salts

$\left(\mathrm{LiNO}_{3}, \mathrm{Li}_{2} \mathrm{SO}_{4}, \text { and } \mathrm{LiCl}\right)$  at low temperatures. $^{118}$  Their results demonstrated that the ALIBs were able to charge and discharge at much higher rates at lower temperatures due to the low charge transfer resistances. However, in conventional aqueous electrolytes, the ALIBs are typically limited to low voltage and energy density due to the narrow window of water decomposition (1.23 V). $^{111}$  Considering this, researchers have tried to employ high-

concentration electrolytes to expand the narrow voltage window. In 2015, for instance, Wang and co-workers pioneered a water-in-salt (WIS) electrolyte in an ALIB by dissolving  $>20m$ $(\mathrm{mol~kg}_{\mathrm{solvent}}^{-1})$  lithium bis(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide (LiTFSI) in water. $^{119}$  According to the schematic diagram of salt-in-water and WIS in Figure 12a, a low-concentration salt solution expresses salt-in-water structure, which leads to preferential decomposition of water. However, upon increasing LiTFSI concentrations over  $20m$ , the high-concentration TFSI leads to an expanded electrochemical stability window of  $3.0\mathrm{V}$ , which is wider than those of previous inorganic salt based electrolytes (Figure 12b). $^{119}$  When electrochemically cycled, ALIBs assembled with a LMO anode and a  $\mathrm{Mo}_6\mathrm{S}_8$  cathode exhibited nearly  $100\%$  CE for up to 1000 cycles at a current rate of  $4.5\mathrm{C}$  (Figure 12c). Similarly, WIS electrolytes have also been reported for other ALIB systems, including  $\mathrm{Mo}_6\mathrm{S}_8 / \mathrm{LCO}$ , $^{120}$ $\mathrm{LiTi}_2(\mathrm{PO}_4)_3 / \mathrm{Li}_3\mathrm{V}_2(\mathrm{PO}_4)_3$ , $^{121}$  and  $\mathrm{VO}_2 / \mathrm{LiVOPO}_4$ . $^{122}$  Furthermore, Wang and co-workers proposed a new class of electrolyte termed as water-in-bisalt (WIBS) electrolyte with a wider electrochemical stability window than WIS of about  $3.1\mathrm{V}$ , as shown in Figure 12d. $^{123}$  The WIBS electrolyte consisted of  $21m$  LiTFSI and  $7m$  lithium trifluoromethanesulfonate. When assembled with a LMO cathode and a C-TiO $_2$  anode to a full ALIB, it delivered a high average discharge voltage of  $2.1\mathrm{V}$  with energy density of  $100\mathrm{Whkg}^{-1}$  (Figure 12e). As shown in Figure 12f, the LMO/C-TiO $_2$  based ALIB exhibited excellent cycling stability with a low capacity decay rate of  $0.22\%$  per cycle after 100 cycles. The WIBS electrolyte can also be used in conjunction with LiCl/LiBr as cathode and graphite as anode. $^{124}$  The resulting battery system achieved initial capacity retention of  $74\%$  over 150 cycles at an average CE of  $99.8\%$  (Figure 12g). In addition, Wang and co-workers have further reported a novel  $63m$  aqueous electrolyte ( $42m$  LiTFSI +  $21m$ $\mathrm{Me}_3\mathrm{EtN}\cdot \mathrm{TFSI}$ ), offering a wide potential window of  $3.25\mathrm{V}$  for developing a  $2.5\mathrm{V}$  ALIB ( $\mathrm{LMO} / \mathrm{Li}_4\mathrm{Ti}_5\mathrm{O}_{12}$ ), which can deliver high energy density of  $145\mathrm{Whkg}^{-1}$  over 150 cycles. $^{125}$

Despite the remarkable improvement in the voltage window for current WIS electrolyte systems, economic and environmental concerns remain, hindering their further applications. In particular, the LiTFSI salt has limited large-scale availability, whereas other hydrate melt components, such as LiBETI, are only available in limited quantities. Hence, the actual concentration of lithium salt used to make the electrolyte is an important parameter to consider. Bao and co-workers proposed a new WIS electrolyte based on potassium acetate and lithium acetate salts that can provide the same voltage window as LiTFSI-based electrolytes.[126] The electrochemical stability windows of  $27\mathrm{m}$  KOAc and  $32\mathrm{m}$  KOAc-8 m LiOAc electrolyte solutions in combination with different current collectors were evaluated using CV at a scan rate of  $0.2\mathrm{mV}s^{-1}$  and compared with the CV data obtained from 1 m KOAc electrolyte (Figure 13a). Evident is  $\sim 1.15\mathrm{V}$  extension of the electrochemical stability window upon increase in KOAc salt concentration from 1 to  $27\mathrm{m}$ . The increased salt molality in  $32\mathrm{m}$  KOAc-8 m LiOAc solution does not lead to any additional voltage window extension. On the other hand, definite extension in the negative potential range was observed in  $32\mathrm{m}$  KOAc-8 m LiOAc with a glassy carbon current collector. To evaluate whether a mixed cation strategy would allow us to use the already-developed Li-ion electrode systems, CV characterization of various typical anode and cathode materials was tested in the  $32\mathrm{m}$  KOAc-8 m LiOAc electrolyte. Consequently, the results demonstrated a set of reversible Li-ion intercalation peaks (Figure 13b). As a result,

the  $c\text{-TiO}_2/$  LMO cell delivered an operation voltage window up to  $2.5\mathrm{V}$  (Figure 13c) and stable cycling performance with capacities of  $\sim 45$  mAh g $^{-1}$  (Figure 13d).

In addition, a new type of electrolyte, water-in-ionomer, has been reported, which comprised nonfluorinated and nontoxic lithiated poly(acrylic acid) (LiPAA) aqueous gel electrolyte and exhibited a comparable electrochemical stability window to those of WIS electrolytes. [127] As illustrated in Figure 13e, a typical Al current collector for LIBs allows for an extended electrochemical stability window compared with Pt or stainless steel, not to mention that this electrolyte allowed reversible  $\mathrm{Li^{+}}$  insertion and extraction for both  $\mathrm{TiO_2}$  and  $\mathrm{LiTi_2(PO_4)_3}$  anodes. For cathode, LMO delithiation was reversible, but for  $\mathrm{LiNi_{0.5}Mn_{1.5}O_4}$ , only partial delithiation occurred on the reverse scan. The enlargement of the electrochemical stability window for water-in-ionomer electrolyte is mainly due to the increase of the cathodic stability, which has been the main challenge for aqueous electrolytes (Figure 13f). As shown in Figure 13g, the  $\mathrm{LiTi_2(PO_4)_3 / LMO}$  cell achieves stable cycling for 100 cycles with an initial energy density of  $77\mathrm{Whkg^{-1}}$  and a remarkable capacity retention of  $77\%$  over 100 cycles with CE of  $98\%$ .

However, owing to the absence of a reliable SEI in  $32m$  KOAc-8 m LiOAc and the high water content needed  $(>30\%)$  in LiPAA gel to avoid electrode-electrolyte contact issues, the water stability window is narrow in these nonfluorinated electrolytes, leading to lower energy density and shorter cycle life as compared with the fluorinated electrolytes.[126,127] Lu and co-workers successfully created low-cost, ecofriendly, and high-voltage "molecular crowding" aqueous electrolytes via confining water molecules in a crowding agent, poly(ethylene glycol) (PEG), network through hydrogen bonding.[128] With the expanded stable voltage window with a potential range of  $1.3 - 4.5\mathrm{V}$  vs.  $\mathrm{Li} / \mathrm{Li}^{+}$  as provided by the crowding agent PEG (Figure 14a), the electrochemical behavior of the full cell was examined with a LMO cathode, a spinel  $\mathrm{Li}_4\mathrm{Ti}_5\mathrm{O}_{12}$  anode with 3 wt  $\%$ $\mathrm{Li}_{1.3}\mathrm{Al}_{0.3}\mathrm{Ti}_{1.7}(\mathrm{PO}_4)_3$  coating (L-LTO), and  $2m$  LiTFSI-  $94\% \mathrm{PEG} - 6\% \mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$  electrolyte. The reversible electrochemical behavior of the L-LTO and LMO electrodes in the  $2m$  LiTFSI-  $94\% \mathrm{PEG} - 6\% \mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$  electrolyte was confirmed by CV measurements (Figure 14b). Online electrochemical mass spectroscopy (OEMS) was used to monitor gas evolution during the charge and discharge process of the L-LTO/LMO full cell at  $1C$  in  $2m$  LiTFSI-  $94\% \mathrm{PEG} - 6\% \mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ . Clearly, no visible  $\mathrm{H}_2,\mathrm{O}_2,\mathrm{CO} / \mathrm{N}_2,$  or  $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$  signal was detected upon cycling (Figure 14c,d), indicating that water decomposition was virtually eliminated in the L-LTO/LMO cell with the molecular crowding aqueous electrolyte. In addition, an initial specific energy of  $110\mathrm{Whkg^{-1}}$  was achieved at  $1C,$  and  $75\mathrm{Whkg^{-1}}$  was maintained after 300 cycles with a high average CE of  $\sim 99\%$  (Figure 14e,f). The polymer-salt solution provided a new way to broaden the electrochemical window of electrolytes, but one significant barrier is its low conductivity and high viscosity.[129,130]

Therefore, resolving these barriers becomes the focus of future work in the development of aqueous electrolytes. For example, introducing acetonitrile (ACN) or sulfolane as a cosolvent minimizes the presence of interfacial water at the negatively charged electrode surface.[131-133] However, these gel polymers and organic solvents are still flammable, which compromises the merits of aqueous electrolytes.[134] To design better dilute aqueous electrolytes beyond WIS electrolytes, Wang and coworkers have developed a nonflammable ternary eutectic electrolyte  $\mathrm{(LiTFSI - KOH - CO(NH_2)_2 - H_2O)}$  by using  $\mathrm{CO}(\mathrm{NH}_2)_2$  as a model diluent.[134] The aqueous electrolyte

expanded the electrochemical stability window to  $3.3\mathrm{V}$  and enabled  $\mathrm{Li}_4\mathrm{Ti}_5\mathrm{O}_{12} / \mathrm{LMO}$  pouch cells to achieve a high CE of  $99.96\%$  and capacity retention of  $92\%$  after 470 cycles, which demonstrates a promising approach toward safe, low-cost, and high-energy ALIBs for GSES applications. The electrochemical performance of high-concentration salt electrolytes in ALIBs has been summarized in Table 5. Although tremendous improvements in electrolytes have been reported for laboratory-based ALIBs, their development is still impeded with further challenges that need to be overcome. Furthermore, the data reported in the literature to date has often been acquired under ideal testing conditions, such as with high N/P ratio, excessive electrolyte with salt, and low active material loading. While these achievements appear to be significant, they are only of limited value for industrial production, where much stricter conditions are required.

# 3.4. Sodium-lon Batteries

In the Earth's crust, sodium (2.83%) is much more abundant than lithium (0.01%) and hence has a lower price ( $150 \, \text{ton}^{-1}$ ). The cost aspect is extremely critical for the development of new battery systems, especially considering LSES.[135,136] Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) were initially studied in the 1970s and 1980s, but this battery system was largely abandoned due to the rapid development in the commercial applications of LIBs.[137,138] Moreover, sodium is difficult to deal with because it is more active than lithium.[139] Nonetheless, the indispensable cost effectiveness, acceptable energy and power densities make SIBs an attractive alternative for LSES systems.[135,136,139,140] The similarities to LIBs in battery principle, energy storage mechanisms, and electrolyte composition have enabled more convenient and rapid development of SIBs.[140] However, unlike LIBs, the development of proper anode and cathode materials for SIBs has been challenging, especially for their commercialization.[135,141] Hence, we present the recent progress in enhancing the electrochemical properties as well as discovering new electrode materials for SIBs in order to promote the development of this battery system.

3.4.1. Anode Materials for SIBs. Graphite as a commercial anode material has been successfully applied in first generation LIBs due to its low price, easy fabrication, and excellent lithium-ion insertion capability.[80] However, most attempts to apply graphite anodes in SIBs have failed with low Na storage capacities owing to its larger ion radius than  $\mathrm{Li^{+}}$ .[136,142] Therefore, non-graphitic carbonaceous materials are considered to be the "first-generation" anodes for SIB systems due to their good  $\mathrm{Na^{+}}$ insertion capacity and high specific capacity.[140] However, their cycling performance is still not good enough for commercial utilization in SIBs.[136] It is therefore imperative to fabricate large interlayer distance graphite or novel carbon materials to improve the performance of the anode for SIBs.

As demonstrated by theoretical calculations, the interlayer distance of graphite ( $\sim 0.34 \mathrm{~nm}$ ) is too small to accommodate the large  $\mathrm{Na}^{+}$ , and therefore a minimum interlayer distance of  $0.37 \mathrm{~nm}$  is required for  $\mathrm{Na}^{+}$ insertion.[143] Wang and co-workers reported expanded graphite (EG), which is a graphite-derived material formed by a two-step oxidation-reduction process with a large interlayer distance of  $40.34 \mathrm{~nm}$  (Figure 15a), as a superior SIB anode material.[144] These features provide favorable conditions for electrochemical intercalation of  $\mathrm{Na}^{+}$ . The pristine graphite (PG) displays well-defined layers of long-range order stacking parallel to each other (Figure 15b). After oxidation, such long-range order is disrupted by massive intercalation of oxygen in the graphite interlayer, forming a more disordered pattern with only localized short-range ordering (Figure 15c). However, after partial reduction, the material recovers toward a well-organized morphology, while preserving expanded interlayer distances and long-range channels suitable for  $\mathrm{Na}^{+}$ transport (Figure 15d,e). In addition, the average interlayer spacings are measured to be  $\sim 0.34 \mathrm{~nm}$  for PG,  $0.61 \mathrm{~nm}$  for GO,  $0.43 \mathrm{~nm}$  for EG-1 h and  $0.37 \mathrm{~nm}$  for EG-5 h. It is obvious that the distance between PG layers is expanded after the oxidation reactions because of the insertion of oxygen-containing groups and that such spacing remains larger than PG after reduction treatments. Therefore, PG has a negligible sodium storage capacity of  $\sim 13 \mathrm{mAh} \mathrm{g}^{-1}$ , and GO shows monotonic voltage

![](images/ce2e88cfd0b87c1c2c3f964e353f2943b9cf59eed963fd93d7de49c24b6a4e8f.jpg)

![](images/07f3b34de86eba7f1c25124f3a97512f3e737dfef0edb8006f52256f5c8c0ea7.jpg)

![](images/3849f59ad815ecb76bdca423db357bcba8f33112ef4059fa91f71ee0a0a70733.jpg)

![](images/5d941bd7e6df76ee1ecd240ae734b7a2171195b899e082d317866f4f9fea1bea.jpg)

![](images/e458781704413a11d9041822dbe41b99cb8c9f1f66b2a5a5341c2d0b4d474f6b.jpg)

![](images/c4d610204129e042c4d61da30faca802fd4d38e98c4265ab332a5f9294d91168.jpg)

![](images/4ac4888e1021b16218912df85c0295202572bb16a541a90aa2cea2536b80e33c.jpg)

![](images/bfc26a629c0a79656a128efd9f55bbf1ab6f512d1181371b90aca68770dbb751.jpg)

![](images/74757a3f01af1a42716637c1dbc2bc389e8d1da2853409ff3df4445e238abe85.jpg)

![](images/ce80acad8160c95fa12de7b126323be34ecf3264eb6076f8206f1ab4e7900838.jpg)

![](images/905c08f40265e28169f797cb7f841236b677dcbf2e0db1dc13a3ac628412e21f.jpg)

![](images/c9d0d7ccac7d9e5c7c6430d165c586440b754c694294491f3eaa293f8a50db79.jpg)

High-resolution TEM images of (b) PG, (c) GO, (d) EG-1 h, and (e) EG-5 h. Scale bars,  $2\mathrm{nm}$ . Contrast profiles along the arrows indicate interlayer spacing of corresponding samples. (f) Charge/discharge curves for the second cycles of PG, GO, EG-1 h, and EG-5 h at a current density of  $20\mathrm{mA}\mathrm{g}^{-1}$ . (g) Short-term cycling stability test for PG, GO, EG-1 h, and EG-5 h at a current density of  $20\mathrm{mA}\mathrm{g}^{-1}$ . (h) Long-term cycling stability at a current density of  $100\mathrm{mA}\mathrm{g}^{-1}$ , and (i) rate capability of EG-1 h. Reproduced with permission from ref 144. Copyright 2014 Springer Nature.

![](images/c82a2bf1ac6abbc0ef5d56ac71f4299ad941c5084803bc16cc2eaa58dbcb9140.jpg)

![](images/1026b6326048ab27361a6c2e68e3706951af75bb58548f86b4c4e19aadb0edf0.jpg)

![](images/bc6ce62395e7d18bda12b9a29d6a94bd4fa2e9db7a8c5c16fd0931ca491d2648.jpg)

![](images/5c17699ffd7f225fa6e51f1eac95bf7c709f4d5179bf5fada92135706d09f463.jpg)  
Figure 16. Typical examples of anode materials of SIBs for improving the cycling performance. (a) Schematic illustration of the synthetic process of CNFs. (b) SEM image and (c) cycling stability of CNFs at  $10\mathrm{A}\mathrm{g}^{-1}$ . Reproduced with permission from ref 147. Copyright 2019 Elsevier. (d) Structural evolution of the sandwiched phosphorene-graphene structure during sodiation. (e) Reversible desodiation capacities of various phosphorene-graphene electrodes with different carbon-phosphorus mole ratios at a current density of  $0.05\mathrm{A}\mathrm{g}^{-1}$ . Reproduced with permission from ref 150. Copyright 2015 Springer Nature. (f) Capacity comparison of different anode materials of SIBs. (g) Cycling stability at  $150\mathrm{mA}\mathrm{g}^{-1}$  of  $\mathrm{SnP}_3 / \mathrm{C}$  electrode. Reproduced with permission from ref 151. Copyright 2015 Wiley-VCH.

![](images/73397c8b51ede30c052fb2e6d603ac4738524d08228cc7cda485648a6a3d001e.jpg)

curves during the whole sodiation/desodiaton process with an increased capacity of  $\sim 156\mathrm{mAhg^{-1}}$  (Figure 15f). After  $1\textrm{h}$  of thermal reduction, it can deliver a highly reversible sodium conversion capacity of  $\sim 300\mathrm{mAhg^{-1}}$  , which is significantly higher than PG, GO, and EG-5 h  $(\sim 300\mathrm{mAhg^{-1}})$  , implying that the interlayer distance predominantly controls the  $\mathrm{Na^{+}}$  storage capacity. In addition, all materials show stable cycling performances in the first 30 cycles except for the initial few cycles, with EG-1 h exhibiting the highest sodium storage capacity (Figure 15g). For cycling performance, the EG demonstrates excellent cycling stability for 2000 cycles with a low capacity decay rate of  $\sim 0.013\%$  per cycle from the 11th cycle to the 2000th cycle (Figure 15h). In the rate capability tests, EG exhibits a capacity of  $284\mathrm{mAhg^{-1}}$  at a low current of  $20\mathrm{mA g^{-1}}$  and a high capacity of  $91\mathrm{mAhg^{-1}}$  at a high current of  $200\mathrm{mA g^{-1}}$  , demonstrating the high tolerance of EG under a wide range of charge/discharge conditions (Figure 15i).

Recently, various nanostructured carbonaceous materials have been reported as anodes for SIBs, and it has been found that the sodium storage properties, including the cycling stability and rate capability, are highly dependent on the carbon structures. Dou and co-workers prepared a novel hierarchical 3D porous carbon material (C-600) as the anode for SIBs through an in situ template carbonization process.[145] This anode delivered high rate capacity  $(126\mathrm{mAhg}^{-1}$  at  $20\mathrm{A g}^{-1})$  and long-term cycling stability (15000 cycles at  $10\mathrm{A g}^{-1}$ ). Similarly,

Zhang and co-workers reported 3D porous graphene grids (PGGs) consisting of several layered graphene structures with ultrahigh surface area and hierarchical connected structure. $^{146}$  The PGGs showed high specific capacity of  $160\mathrm{mAhg^{-1}}$  at a current density of  $50\mathrm{mA g^{-1}}$  and long cycling performance of  $112\mathrm{mAh g^{-1}}$  at  $1\mathrm{A g^{-1}}$  after 1000 cycles. In addition, Yu and coworkers investigated a highly porous and ultrafine interconnected carbon nanofiber (CNF) electrode prepared by simply carbonization of a bacterial cellulose film, as shown in Figure 16a. $^{147}$  Figure 16b presents the SEM image of the CNF morphology with a high aspect ratio and diameter of 20-200 nm. The CNF anode also showed long cycling stability at a high current rate of  $10\mathrm{A g^{-1}}$  after 10000 cycles (Figure 16c). These carbonaceous electrode materials with large surface areas are able to demonstrate high rate and cycling performance, because sodium ions have a relatively larger radius compared to lithium ions.

Besides the carbon-based anodes, many other novel anode materials can be synthesized by alloying, conversion, or combination of both to achieve high capacities. Regarding alloying, group IVA and VA elements such as Sn, Sb, and P showed high sodium storage capacity in their alloying reaction.[136] However, the main challenge of alloy-based anodes in SIBs is the pulverization and aggregation of materials due to the harsh volume variation and large radius of sodium ions.[148] Another critical issue for alloy-based anodes, mainly for P and Si

![](images/45159251dc5abc949ee8ef085e2b8d4074711187a74895c9a99ad94dcbd42833.jpg)

![](images/6ccc5fa709cc6c358b839f192228ef99c6e7fe114c131ba355d70e13d7c2e11d.jpg)

![](images/5a46863f3c6adab499cc6d2ba50cba3fbaaa6a0b0d90f3ebe394a1abc69ebc17.jpg)

![](images/be29232e03af1bd767d70331a120ea83d61335fc0b3d7ee2b723da2f99f985a4.jpg)

![](images/02ffde7e85582d34c85c6890b272409c02dc1a34878955e6d0e459679c75e544.jpg)

![](images/87436fffb3e94a5ef4f6aa9350aa48deaa395c3c714e0e971d20fa6cf2e6cea8.jpg)
