(a) Schematic diagram of the Al/graphite cell during discharge in the optimal composition of the  $\mathrm{AlCl}_3 / [\mathrm{EMIm}]\mathrm{Cl}$  IL electrolyte. (b) Galvanostatic charge and discharge curves of an Al/pyrolytic graphite Swagelok cell at a current density of  $66\mathrm{mA}$ $\mathbf{g}^{-1}$ . (c) Stability test of an Al/pyrolytic graphite cell at a current density of  $66\mathrm{mA}\mathrm{g}^{-1}$ . Reproduced with permission from ref 278. Copyright 2015 Springer Nature.

(Figure 30d,e). These results therefore provide a new pathway for other oxide-based materials as insertion cathodes for MgIBs.

# 3.8. Aluminum-Ion Batteries

In the last several years, rechargeable aluminum-ion batteries (AlBs) have attracted much attention, fundamentally owing to the crustal abundance of Al metal. The ability of  $\mathrm{Al}^{3+}$  to exchange three electrons during redox reaction is also notable for delivering a high volumetric capacity of  $8046\mathrm{mAhcm}^{-3}$ , which is superior to single electron reaction such as Li, Na, and K.[274] In addition, the inertia of Al metal and easy operation in the ambient environment suggest commendable safety of AIB systems.[275,276] In short, AIBs offer bright prospects for GSES applications. However, for practical applications, some problems exist in relation to AIBs, for instance the lack of AIB cathode materials, low energy density, and poor cycling stability.[274] Energy density, as determined by battery working voltage and capacity, is one of the most critical parameters for gauging the electrochemical performance of AIBs. The low reversibility of the Al metal anode also limits the energy density of AIBs. Archer and co-workers reported that specific interfacial metal-substrate bonding can be used to achieve a fine control of Al metal electrodeposition morphology with high reversibility.[277] In general, the AIB system is composed of an Al anode with different cathodes and electrolytes. With regards to AIB development, the studies have concentrated on investigating novel electrolytes and cathode materials.

3.8.1. Electrolytes for AIBs. In 2015, Dai and co-workers reported an IL electrolyte made from vacuum-dried  $\mathrm{AlCl}_3 / 1$ -ethyl-3-methylimidazolium chloride  $(\mathrm{AlCl}_3 / [\mathrm{EMIm}]\mathrm{Cl})$  for AIB.[278] The AIB is constructed by  $\mathrm{AlCl}_3 / [\mathrm{EMIm}]\mathrm{Cl}$  electrolyte, graphite cathode, and aluminum anode (Figure 31a). During discharging, Al metal and  $\mathrm{AlCl}_4^-$  at the anode are transformed

into  $\mathrm{Al}_{2}\mathrm{Cl}_{7}^{-}$  and the predominant  $\mathrm{AlCl}_4^-$  is de-intercalated from the cathode graphite layers. The reverse reactions take place during battery recharging. The cell shows an excellent discharge working voltage of  $\sim 2\mathrm{V}$  and over 200 cycles with an average CE of  $98\%$  at room temperature (Figure 31b,c). However, the high cost of  $\mathrm{AlCl}_3 / [\mathrm{EMIm}] \mathrm{Cl}$  ILs limits the furtherance of these AIBs in large-scale applications. Recently, the utility of urea as an additive for partial or full replacement of [EMIm]Cl electrolyte in AIBs has been proposed in order to achieve excellent electrochemical performance.[279,280] In another study, Gao and co-workers reported a low-cost  $\mathrm{AlCl}_3 / \mathrm{Et}_3\mathrm{NHCl}$  IL as electrolyte for an Al/graphene battery, which shows good capacity retention of  $97.3\%$  after 30000 cycles.[281] The emergence of solid-state electrolytes also provides great stability and safety for AIBs, which is promising for their widespread employability in energy storage systems. Fang and co-workers have used the gel-polymer electrolytes between graphite cathodes and Al anodes, thereby pioneering a novel approach toward developing high-performance AIBs.[282] In this regard, novel approaches to improving performance and reducing cost of AIBs through the development of new electrolytes are worth exploring to pave the way for their GSES applications.

3.8.2. Cathode Materials for AIBs. As discussed earlier, traditional cathode materials such as graphite for AIBs usually show low capacity  $(< 100\mathrm{mAhg^{-1}})$ , hindering their prospects for GSES application.[278] Therefore, the construction of high-capacity cathode materials is the key to the development of commercial AIBs. In this respect, the vanadium and manganese oxides have been explored in AIBs, similarly to other MIBs, due to their good ionic channels.[283,284] Although the batteries based on these cathode materials deliver a higher capacity than graphite cathodes, short-term cycling still hinders their

![](images/0a86781791cdb26601cda57799afbe91db17e366e48607d22c71de819f37a92b.jpg)

![](images/5419e32a7534d25796afa35e5a4b4914de8692d65b37d78f574e276bc17472fb.jpg)

![](images/e287d2e198a783ab09c7d728fda5ad8bacd63c10af7bc1a9f53d63bf81887cb3.jpg)

![](images/589290cb74e450907b45542d3625c095453a9d35c32484842ad7997d9acf7b7b.jpg)

![](images/48df60c9f2163ee43110fbb14057ff2d17030e3c9852e59b6337b82019a1633c.jpg)

![](images/5d43db37aa1386df477d7a8958eb8fa6b4f5f067a1b94faf169f8f4358f3a316.jpg)

![](images/1fb1c20362bc1be607b659eff30d254d2ff352e4bf4843d60fa8d5f69acbc4b3.jpg)

![](images/95ad7720c81c988b708cef2c9de64db29dbd132c0acd0d8b0daa339bf9966cff.jpg)  
Figure 32. Typical examples of cathode materials for AIBs. (a-c) FE-SEM images of 3D hierarchical nanostructured CuS microspheres at different magnifications. (d) Initial discharge/charge curves and (e) cycling performance with CE at  $20\mathrm{mA}\mathrm{g}^{-1}$  of  $\mathrm{CuS@C}$  microsphere cathode. Reproduced with permission from ref 287. Copyright 2017 American Chemical Society. (f) FE-SEM image of the  $\mathrm{Co_9S_8@CNT - CNF}$ . (g) First, fifth, and fiftieth charge/discharge curves at a current density of  $100\mathrm{mA}\mathrm{g}^{-1}$ , and (h) cycling performance with CE at  $1\mathrm{A}\mathrm{g}^{-1}$  of the  $\mathrm{Co_9S_8@CNT - CNF}$  electrode. Inset exhibits the flexible  $\mathrm{Co_9S_8@CNT - CNF}$  AIBs using soft-package assembly. Reproduced with permission from ref 288. Copyright 2018 Wiley-VCH.

application. In contrast to the metal oxides, sulfides have been proven to be more beneficial for  $\mathrm{Al}^{3+}$  intercalation/deintercalation due to their lower electronegativity and larger ionic radius.[285] Combining carbon with the higher-capacity sulfides has been established as a superior approach to designing high-performance AIB cathodes. In 2016, for example, Jiao and coworkers reported  $\mathrm{Ni}_3\mathrm{S}_2/$  graphene microflakes as a cathode material with Al foil anode and  $\mathrm{AlCl}_3/$  [EMIm]Cl IL electrolyte

for AIBs, which can deliver a high initial discharge capacity of  $350\mathrm{mAhg^{-1}}$  286 Further, a novel 3D hierarchical  $\mathrm{CuS@C}$  microsphere as the cathode for AIBs has also been demonstrated (Figure 32a-c).287 When tested with an Al anode and an  $\mathrm{AlCl}_3/$  [EMIm]Cl ILs electrolyte, this novel cathode material exhibits a high initial discharge capacity of about  $240\mathrm{mAhg^{-1}}$  and retains a discharge capacity of about  $90\mathrm{mAhg^{-1}}$  after 100 cycles (Figure 32d,e). Another potential advantage of using this novel

and inexpensive cathode material is its ability to direct unprecedented redox reaction pathways in AIBs. In contrast, the  $\mathrm{CuS@C}$  microsphere material shows a low CE and fast fade capacity in the initial cycles of AIBs. Later, Wang and co-workers reported a cobalt sulfide encapsulated in CNTs coated on carbon nanofibers  $(\mathrm{Co}_{9}\mathrm{S}_{8}@\mathrm{CNT - CNF})$  as a novel cathode for AIBs, as shown in Figure 32f.[288] The structure of this cathode material tends to deliver a high initial capacity of  $315\mathrm{mAhg^{-1}}$  with a CE approaching  $100\%$  and stable cycling performance with a capacity of  $87\mathrm{mAhg^{-1}}$  after 6000 cycles, as shown in Figure 32g,h. In addition, some other novel materials, such as CoSe and  $\mathrm{Cu_4P}$ , have been investigated as cathode alternatives for AIBs.[289,290] However, the electrochemical performance of these materials has not met the demand of AIBs for GSES. Therefore, the development of AIB cathode materials needs momentous improvement in order to ensure their practical large-scale applications.

# 3.9. Summary and Future Perspective for MIBs

The sustainable development of our society requires advanced battery systems for GSES. Every type of MIB has attracted considerable attention and therefore has been developed rapidly in recent years due to their respective advantages. This section provides a summary on some of the most extensively investigated MIBs mainly from the standpoints of their recent development and performance as well as numerous improvement strategies. Generally, six main groups of MIB systems, including LIBs, SIBs, PIBs, ZIBs, MgIBs, and AIBs, have been summarized on the basis of their different metal-ion transport mechanisms.

Notwithstanding extensive research, it is still desirable to further improve the electrochemical performance and reduce the cost of MIBs for meeting the demands of the GSES market. It is critical to develop outstanding structures and novel materials for cathode and anode as well as stable electrolytes. The optimization of one performance metric usually comes at the cost of other parameters, and the assessment itself is based on experimental conditions that are hard to scale up for practical utility. For example, with cost-effective and environmentally benign  $\mathrm{MnO}_2$  as the cathode and Zn metal as the anode in acidic electrolyte, the as-prepared electrolytic  $\mathrm{Zn - MnO_2}$  batteries deliver reasonable energy density and long-term stability at relatively low areal capacities. However, most of the reported values in the literature are solely based on the cathode materials and do not take into account the weight of the whole battery or the vast Zn metal loss in acidic electrolyte.

With the low-cost and abundant Na, K, Zn, Mg, or Al, metal-based batteries have garnered significant attention in the field of GSES application. Currently, investigations on SIBs, PIBs, ZIBs, MgIBs, and AIBs are mostly confined to the initial laboratory scaled academic research stage. The recent explorations and proposed approaches have attempted to establish insightful strategies, which can promote the practical advancements for new battery systems. However, more investigations on electrodes, electrolytes, and full cells are in order before these novel chemistries can be adopted into commercial applications. Furthermore, the data reported in the laboratory experiments are often acquired under ideal testing conditions, for instance, with excess Al or Mg metal anode, abundant electrolyte, and low active material loading on the cathode. As efficient as these laboratory practices are, they are of limited significance in industrial production requiring more stringent conditions. In addition, the technologies employed for materials synthesis and

the data obtained from laboratory based electrochemical characterization cannot always be translated for commercial mass production. In other words, even if the technology seems promising from the perspective of performance, there are many other factors that can immediately exclude its commercial applicability. Therefore, the manufacturing of battery electrodes and materials is crucial for prototyping and eventual commercialization of novel metal-ion battery systems. Besides the seemingly mature LIBs, other MIBs with abundant sources, cost-effectiveness, and fair electrochemical performance are particularly favored for grid-scale applications. However, there is a long way to go for realizing the GSES applications of these battery systems through minimizing the gap between academic research and industrial practices. Having mentioned that, it can be speculated that LIBs may still be one of the mainstream batteries in the GSES field in the upcoming 1-2 decades given their market share and impressive performance.

# 4. LEAD-ACID BATTERIES

Invented in 1859 by Gaston Plante, the LAB has been one of the earliest developed and is still one of the most widely utilized battery systems.[291] It is primarily deployed in various energy storage applications, ranging from lights to electric automobiles to uninterruptible power supplies (UPSs). Such extensive utility of LABs is predominantly attributed to their low storage costs per kilowatt-hour, convenient accessibility, and safe water-based electrolyte.[292] For more than a decade, LABs have been commercially available in LSES markets owing to the accumulated power and energy up to  $10\mathrm{MW}$  and  $40\mathrm{MWh}$ , respectively,[293] thereby showcasing their potential in serving LSES applications.

A LAB based BESS was installed at the Alt Daber solar power plant in Germany by the Belectric and Vattenfall companies. This energy storage facility was designed with the intention of putting the solar energy facility on even footing with conventional power plants. The energy storage facility based on LABs is used to provide primary operating reserve to the high-voltage transmission network, which makes it possible to feed primary operating reserve power directly into the  $110\mathrm{kV}$  high-voltage distribution network through which the solar power plant is connected to low- or medium-voltage networks. The soluble Pb-acid flow battery (SLAFB) remains as one of the most cost-effective and environmentally safe ways to store energy. Because of this, many of them are being used in advanced LSES applications to power businesses and residences with solar farms across the United States. For example, the Public Services Company in New Mexico serves more than 500000 residents and utilizes solar farms with flooded LAB storage systems that can deliver  $500\mathrm{kW}$  of power. The battery system, which is rack-mounted in eight freight containers adjacent to solar panels, allows for stabilized power output during high demand. In addition, the University of New Mexico and Sandia National Laboratories helped on the project and have successfully demonstrated peak shaving, smoothing of photovoltaic ramp rates, and minimizing of voltage fluctuations to the satisfaction of Public Services Company in New Mexico. This section summarizes the underlying dynamics of LABs to better understand their recent development trends and to promote their potential for catering to our future GSES needs.

# 4.1. Brief History of LABs

Since their emergence, the primary principles of the LABs have not experienced significant evolution.[294] The key components of

![](images/7eb3ca50100c963eb9292bb24ece694c059535418fe6f62c3e8aa164d7707064.jpg)  
Figure 33. Pictures of LABs in different generations. (a) First generation and (b) current LABs. Reproduced with permission from ref 298. Copyright 2019 Springer Nature.

![](images/f0b5373dbf17067e30d58769fc208ef41f61b60d98bb7fd286d38bcfe1bef438.jpg)

![](images/d51323849b04af0a297221e60c48a2488597ec0459c33cd6d9ffae3e02246777.jpg)  
Figure 34. Timeline of important milestones in the development of LABs.

the LABs remain the Pb metal anode, the  $\mathrm{PbO}_2$  cathode,  $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{SO}_4$  electrolyte, a porous electrically insulated separator, and Pb alloy grids as current collectors for mechanically supporting electrodes. During discharge, the Pb anode and  $\mathrm{PbO}_2$  cathode react simultaneously with  $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{SO}_4$  to form  $\mathrm{PbSO}_4$ , whereas these reactions are reversed during charge. The electrodes and cell reactions of typical LABs are as follows:

Cathode:

$$
\mathrm {P b O} _ {2} + 4 \mathrm {H} ^ {+} + \mathrm {S O} _ {4} ^ {2 -} + 2 \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {P b S O} _ {4} + 2 \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O}
$$

$$
E ^ {\circ} = 1. 6 9 \mathrm {V} \tag {2}
$$

Anode:

$$
\mathrm {P b S O} _ {4} + 2 \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {P b} + \mathrm {S O} _ {4} ^ {2 -} \quad E ^ {\circ} = - 0. 3 6 \mathrm {V} \tag {3}
$$

Overall:

$$
\mathrm {P b O} _ {2} + \mathrm {P b} + 2 \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {S O} _ {4} \leftrightarrow 2 \mathrm {P b S O} _ {4} + 2 \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O}
$$

$$
E ^ {\circ} = 2. 0 5 \mathrm {V} \tag {4}
$$

It can be seen that the single cell voltage of a LAB can reach up to  $2.05\mathrm{V}$ . Previously, LABs generally adopted the flooded design, containing extra electrolytes, which undergo decomposition during battery overcharge, as shown in Figure 33a.[292] In this regard, in the first half of the twentieth century, the high overpotential grid alloys, such as lead-antimony and lead-calcium grids, were developed for reducing hydrogen gas evolution onto the anode. Later, in the early second half of twentieth century, a valve regulated LAB (VRLAB) cell design (Figure 33b), which not only allowed the venting of hydrogen

and oxygen gases but also prevented the entrance of air into the cells, thereby improving its overall safety, was introduced.[295,296] Hitherto, there have been two important developments pertaining to VRLABs: the absorbed glass mat (AGM) for holding the electrolyte within its pores, and the undivided gel battery based on silica to create a highly viscous gel-like electrolyte.[297] These design developments have reduced the recharge time along with the self-discharge rate and consequently have improved the cycle life and specific power of LABs.

One of the major concerns in relation to VRLAB design has been the formation of large crystals of  $\mathrm{PbSO_4}$  on the anode surface, which detrimentally influence its cycle performance under the high-rate partial-state-of-charge (HRPSoC) mode.[296] To address this issue, the introduction of carbon into the anode has proven effective for reducing the accumulation of  $\mathrm{PbSO_4}$ . This new practice pertaining to LABs has been adopted in designing other energy storage systems as well, primarily owing to its low manufacturing cost and stable performance under HRPSoC conditions.[299] In addition to the promising conventional LABs, a flow battery based on the  $\mathrm{Pb^{2+}/Pb}$  and  $\mathrm{PbO_2/}$ $\mathrm{Pb^{2+}}$  coupled with methanesulfonic acid has also been investigated to show the high solubility of  $\mathrm{Pb(II)}$  along with no stringent requirement of the membrane.[297] Figure 34 summarizes the key developmental history of LABs over the years.

# 4.2. Lead-Carbon Batteries

Although LABs are known for their applications requiring instant ignition, especially in the automobile industry, they have short lifespan mainly due to anode sulfation in HRPSoC

![](images/1a36ed618b71457369793a015f61ba725e0afee2a5bcf65129af9999d0f34de9.jpg)  
Carbon additive

![](images/38bf98ae3ac0b61d834187eae57e31e1881ba019eba4d985720e35599e4eb370.jpg)  
Carbon current collector

![](images/612615f9cff7bf504b708990da48d7c8f11b44c11580378f12fa5fbfd01500f5.jpg)  
Ultrabattery

![](images/8465d597cb67ee6e83c0c8436816f7248fe7e626f5cd9a8749a98f2534c41901.jpg)  
Carbon electrode  
Figure 35. Various designs of lead-carbon battery systems and the cross-sectional views of their anodes. (a) Carbon as additive. (b) Carbon as current collector. (c) Half lead plate and half carbon supercapacitor plate (ultrabattery). (d) All-carbon anode (Axion Power).

mode. $^{300}$  Recently, it has been established that partial or full replacement of the LAB anode with carbon can improve its cycle life and reduce cost. $^{301}$  The different variants of carbon-based LAB systems are illustrated in Figure 35, according to which these are further classified into carbon as additives (Figure 35a), carbon as current collectors (Figure 35b), supercapacitor/battery hybrids (ultrabattery) (Figure 35c), and carbon as anode (Figure 35d).

4.2.1. Carbon as Additives. The addition of carbon at concentrations ranging from  $0.2\mathrm{wt}\%$  to  $2.0\mathrm{wt}\%$  can enhance the conductivity of the anode and restrict  $\mathrm{PbSO_4}$  crystal growth in HRPSoC conditions. $^{46}$  To date, many types of carbon additives have been explored as the anode for LABs, including activated carbon (AC), CB, and acetylene black. $^{302-304}$  In addition, other types of carbon having large surface area are relatively more effective in inhibiting the formation of  $\mathrm{PbSO_4}$  and improving the cycle life of LABs. For example, owing to its high conductivity, graphene has attracted many studies pertaining to LABs for enhancing cycling performance in HRPSoC conditions. $^{162,305}$  In this regard, Ma and co-workers employed a 3D-RGO additive in the LAB anode. $^{306}$  As evident from the SEM micrographs (Figure 36a), the 3D-RGO is composed of a continuous porous structure with pore sizes of  $1-4\mu \mathrm{m}$ , which facilitates the migration ions into the anode. Such a web-like structure of 3D-RGO is also responsible for producing longer cycle life as compared to the conventional carbon additives, such as AC and acetylene black (Figure 36b). Moreover, the abundant porosity of the 3D-RGO along with excellent conductivity assists in creating a synergistic effect by facilitating the charging and discharging of the anode. In addition to graphene, CNTs and CNFs have been investigated for their efficacy as anode additives in LABs. $^{307-310}$  However, there has been a lack of consensus when it comes to the selection of optimal carbon additive for LABs, not to mention that the

underlying mechanisms of LABs are still unclear and need further clarification.

In contrast to the accelerates for carbon additives, these materials pose some serious concerns in terms of augmented hydrogen evolution at the anode. To address this issue, studies have proposed and carefully examined the modification of carbon materials with metal oxides, such as  $\mathrm{Bi}_2\mathrm{O}_3$ ,  $\mathrm{Ga}_2\mathrm{O}_3$ , and  $\mathrm{In}_2\mathrm{O}_3$ . Such modifications of carbon additives have the ability to effectively hamper the hydrogen gas evolution rate and enhance the cycling stability under HRPSoC conditions. In this regard, an AC composite with  $\mathrm{Bi}_2\mathrm{O}_2\mathrm{CO}_3$  has been reported as an effective choice for suppressing hydrogen evolution reaction (HER) by  $93.1\%$  and improving the cycle lifetime of LABs. Similarly, Martha and co-workers have reported the solvothermal synthesis of a  $\mathrm{TiO}_2$ -RGO hybrid as an anode additive in LABs. As it can be seen in Figure 36c, the layered structure of graphite consists of graphene layers with a thickness of  $\sim 10$  nm. The morphology of GO exhibits the multilayer graphene structures due to the presence of oxygen containing functional groups (Figure 36d). In addition, Figure 36e shows spherical  $\mathrm{TiO}_2$  particles of sizes ranging between 25 and  $50~\mathrm{nm}$ . Figure 36f further demonstrates that such oxide particles are well grown on the surface of RGO. The electrochemical testing results show that the presence of the  $\mathrm{TiO}_2$ -RGO hybrid additive not only improved interfacial stability and cycling performance of the LAB as compared to its individual constituents ( $\mathrm{TiO}_2$  and RGO) but also suppressed sulfation on the anode, as shown in Figure 36g,h. Similarly, Naresh and Martha have examined the efficiency of a synthesized carbon coating on  $\mathrm{SnO}_2$  in LABs. Concerning this, the results reveal that carbon coating onto  $\mathrm{SnO}_2$  favors high conductivity, inhibits sulfation, and prolongs cycling life in contrast to the conventional carbon additives.

4.2.2. Carbon as Current Collectors. One of the shortcomings of conventional LABs is their low specific capacity, which is limited by the high mass of  $\mathrm{Pb}$  and its ineffective

![](images/15b754dba5e7ee85807a69df25d508cfcb75c12d16904e7b8a0dfecc291f5f06.jpg)

![](images/7796b98ca4ac2586ddfdef4eb1c0362900c6ff4af55ef2c6f22d4035842fc4e6.jpg)

![](images/1f63151320377573a6a30b240497f7eeb5f87e714dd03d33a8e002895f60da15.jpg)

![](images/a439625f2d1f102996e83a3b638417cae8ce907fbc3649e0bd427c7bb9c68279.jpg)

![](images/112c8ca99b9a9d88f22f2a11b6bcc7ef44db21a4e6d8f8410541406c96a60c1d.jpg)

Figure 36. Morphology and electrochemical performance of LABs with different carbon additives. (a) SEM images of the prepared 3D-RGO. (b) Cycling performance for end of discharge voltage of LABs with different carbon additives (3D-RGO, AC, ACET (acetylene black)) under HRPSoC conditions. Reproduced with permission from ref 306. Copyright 2017 Elsevier. SEM images of (c) graphite, (d) GO, (e)  $\mathrm{TiO_2}$ , and (f)  $\mathrm{TiO_2}$ -RGO hybrid (3:1). (g) Cycling performance of conventional cell and cells with anode additives of  $\mathrm{TiO_2}$ , RGO and  $\mathrm{TiO_2}$ -RGO hybrid (different ratios of 100:1, 10:1, and 3:1). (h) Bar chart showing the cycle life under HRPSoC conditions of the conventional and LABs with different anode additives. Reproduced with permission from ref 311. Copyright 2018 Elsevier.

![](images/c701ee5fe27fbd6cf7101061e6d5b6f31e499c3a9f81625db72ce5306bcc409b.jpg)

utilization. $^{298}$  The high weight of LABs is attributed to both of its electrodes, in which Pb alloys serve as current collectors. In this regard, low-weight carbon-based current collectors have been considered as the optimal choice to replace the heavy Pb alloys. In 1995, Czerwinski reported that reticulated vitreous carbon (RVC), a porous glassy carbon made from a polyurethane precursor, when used as a current collector for both cathode and anode plates of LABs can significantly decrease the mass of electrodes and augment energy density. $^{298}$  Having similarity to the conventional electrode plates with the exception of slightly higher thickness  $(5\mathrm{mm})$ , $^{316}$  RVC also exhibits excellent properties in terms of high surface area and superior electrical conductivity. Figure 37a is a digital photograph of the RVC/Pb current collector (RVC electroplated with Pb onto its surface) showing its high surface area and its suitability for immense reactivity. It can be noticed that the Pb metal coating on RVC further protects it from oxidation in  $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{SO}_4$  solution. $^{317}$

Figure 37b presents an SEM micrograph of the electrodeposited Pb on RVC, showing its 3D reticular structure. In addition,

Figure 37c presents a cross-section of a RVC/Pb filament, revealing the thickness of the uniformly distributed Pb coating up to  $10\mu \mathrm{m}$ . Furthermore, it is evident from the schematic of a four-plate 2 V cell with RVC/Pb-based electrodes (Figure 37d) that these LABs are placed in a standard polypropylene casing, which is linked with electrical connectors, and sealed with a lid used typically for flooded batteries. During cyclic operation, a good discharge voltage plateau of 2 V and high capacity (117% of the calculated nominal capacity) were obtained at a current rate as low as 0.05 C, as shown in Figure 37e. Figure 37f further shows the cycle life of the 2 V RVC/Pb-based LAB with a nominal capacity of 3.4 Ah, revealing its high initial capacity (130%), exceeding that of standard LABs. However, the capacity drops rapidly below 10% after 200 cycles, which is most likely due to the corrosion of the Pb layer on the carbon collectors in the cathode. Nevertheless, such performance confirms the superior charge/discharge performance of the RVC/Pb-based LABs as compared to the standard LABs along with much space to further improve their cycling stability. In a similar study, a 12

![](images/48194f9f1b9aee774ad2e930bf9492b21171959801b9ea714644b8abf3d0f72a.jpg)

![](images/95e70f83e1639fb0bf70598f6f7e5785f39663296d5b8d2b0fbc7d8c2264f72d.jpg)

![](images/3481461b88816fbd17debe86533c0672c37a6f0def06ba4b81bbdd39d69d4835.jpg)

![](images/e854579d898f4659e39eb12174f6bbb7eef28774825d16c6a426370e9793b4d8.jpg)

![](images/62574a42fbd819e7c33dc205299f15294d6bfbc092facf75c2e1df89a0d1a5b0.jpg)

(b) SEM image and (c) filament cross section of RVC/Pb current collector. (d) Preparation of a 2 V RVC/Pb-based cell. (e) Discharge performance of LABs with RVC-based current collectors at different rates. (f) Cycle life of a small 2 V RVC/Pb-based cell with a nominal capacity of 3.4 Ah. ![](images/c70ab01e3acfb10b7811b65118ce6790eabd2e86dcc30d0342869231be730074.jpg)

V RVC/Pb-based LAB accomplished 300 charge/discharge cycles, outperforming its commercial counterparts and justifying the efficacy of RVC/Pb as current collectors for LABs (Figure 37g).<sup>318</sup>

In order to further bring down the weight of LABs, numerous studies have investigated novel carbon materials as reticulated current collectors for both anode and cathode. A conductive porous carbon made from a polyurethane precursor with a proper pore size has immense potential to be used as reticulated current collectors and achieved high rate capacities due to its higher conductivity.[298] For instance, Chen and co-workers have employed carbon foam as negative current collector in LABs and compared its electrochemical performance with that of the Pb sheet.[319] Upon electrochemical testing, the former can improve the cycling stability of LABs, mainly attributed to its high specific

surface area and 3D interconnected carbon framework. This study further explored the electrochemical stability of carbon foam as a cathode current collector, which revealed that the potential of oxygen evolution reaction (OER) was lower than the charge acceptance potential during battery charging.[320] That is why the carbon foam is considered to be more suitable as an anode current collector than its cathode counterpart.

In addition to carbon foam, graphite foam as a 3D structured carbon has also been acknowledged as a promising current collector alternative for the development of high energy density LABs. Such acknowledgment comes from its high electrical and thermal conductivity, competitive mechanical strength, and low mass. In this regard, Dudney and co-workers synthesized and evaluated the effect of graphite foams. After a series of electrochemical tests, the graphite foam reveals comparable

![](images/bc6663cc2cdd60f60ae92cbcc3d78473fe9c3a9844977972365a5f63d5a76187.jpg)

![](images/5a178a7f90206c23fef9a3eea6b42efeb0b418d7afa3c8b9cee41a32f76e30c8.jpg)

![](images/fe10fdf676dad9b526cfe24c8fcb7f749f31205df30e1b6697da58ebc1a86146.jpg)  
Figure 38. Electrochemical performance of lead-acid ultrabattery. (a) Operational potential of a lead-acid anode plate and a carbon-based supercapacitor electrode during discharge and charge. (b) HER rates on the LAB anode and the carbon electrodes with or without additives. (c) Power performance of the control battery and lead-acid ultrabattery. (d) Cycling performance of the control batteries and lead-acid ultrabattery. Reproduced with permission from ref 327. Copyright 2006 Elsevier.

![](images/45ad9ecb76ba5b9af43176ebc7923bb938505a35b213f4c2536fa772a84d6e6a.jpg)

performance to that of the carbon foam in terms of its stability for anodic current collector. $^{321}$  Conversely, graphite foam is unstable as the cathode current collector, because of the intercalation of  $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{SO}_4$  into the graphite. In addition to the aforementioned current collector materials, other forms of carbon such as thin graphite foils, composite honeycomb structures, and polymer-graphite composites have also been investigated as current collectors for higher energy density LABs. $^{322-325}$  However, the utility of these materials is limited by the cathode plates due to the anodic corrosion of the entire structure attacking the carbon/carbon composite interface. To address this concern, Dai and co-workers have reported a cathode current collector based on Pb nanocoating on a Cu foam substrate, which helps in improving the electrochemical reactions and hence the stability during LAB operation. $^{326}$  To recapitulate, the lighter carbon materials still need further optimization toward their enhanced stability as cathode current collectors.

4.2.3. Lead-Acid Ultrabatteries. Australia's Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organization along with Furukawa pioneered the ultrabattery, which is a hybrid of an asymmetrical supercapacitor with a LAB.[301] Such a battery is composed of a  $\mathrm{PbO}_2$  cathode and a spongy Pb anode, while the asymmetrical supercapacitor is composed of a  $\mathrm{PbO}_2$  cathode and a carbon-based anode. During operation, the supercapacitor electrode acts as a buffer to share the discharge and charge currents with the Pb anode, thus preventing the system from being discharged and charged at high rates. Compared with a

Pb-based anode, the carbon-based supercapacitor anode provides little battery capacity during early stages of discharging.[327] In addition, when the combined anode potential exceeds  $-0.5\mathrm{V}$ , more current tends to flow from the carbon-based capacitor electrode through charge neutralization (Figure 38a). During battery charging, the current starts to flow to the supercapacitor electrode and then passes onto the Pb-based anode. Consequently, the carbon electrode conveniently causes severe HER by the end of charging, thereby eventually leading to the dry-out of the electrolyte. It is also evident from Figure 38b that the HER current increases with the decrease of potential of the LAB anode and carbon electrodes. Under certain potentials, the HER current of the carbon electrode is greater than that of the Pb anode. However, upon doping of the carbon electrode with additives, the HER rate can be significantly reduced to levels close to that of the Pb anode. This phenomenon indicates that carbon and Pb electrodes can be connected in parallel to prevent substantial electrolyte dry-out under HRPSoC conditions. Therefore, the enhancement of power and cycle-life of the ultrabattery is ensured with the employment of the supercapacitor carbon electrode. Moreover, at any given depth-of-discharge, the discharge and charge power of the ultrabattery are greater than those of control cells without supercapacitor and are improved by  $\sim 50\%$  and even  $\sim 60\%$ , respectively (Figure 38c). This proves the ability of the ultrabattery to operate at a wider depth-of-discharge window and to yield power levels similar to that of conventional VRLABs. Interestingly, the ultrabattery exhibits excellent

![](images/0ba05e745a14d0699f61579e69dd88a9050e50acd1216480eb3e23267c980fa6.jpg)

![](images/df5de459a56317f6191f01c3ddcd69b85d3e3581a697a8e74e1e0086333ff760.jpg)

![](images/21ce02ec61baad01e32108bacb3316d3a614413d1229916de1139b5c313f3c94.jpg)

![](images/fa51eda2677d40cb994db3d4e51d5af79559fc67d6f507af948ea263e32d953b.jpg)

4.2.4. Carbon as Anodes. For the lead-acid ultrabattery, another way to enhance rapid charge acceptance and cycling stability is by coating a carbon layer on the surface of the  $\mathrm{Pb}$

The distribution of  $\mathrm{Pb}$  nanoparticles is further demonstrated (Figure 39b–d) by the EDS mapping analysis. Among the different

types of coatings on the anode,  $30\%$  Pb content generated the highest discharge capacity. (Figure 39e) To validate the HER mechanism, linear sweep voltammetry (LSV) analysis (Figure 39f) showed that the HER starts at approximately  $-1.01\mathrm{~V}$  and  $-1.134\mathrm{~V}$  for AC and AC-Pb anodes, respectively, showing higher HER for AC than that observed for AC-Pb under similar operating conditions. It has also been confirmed that the introduction of Pb can significantly hamper HER and improve

the battery stability. At each current state, the discharge capacity of the AC-Pb anode-based battery is comparatively higher than that with the AC anode (Figure 39g). Furthermore, the cycling stability of the conventional LAB has been found to be significantly lower than that of the AC-Pb coated anode, which demonstrates remarkable cycle stability up to 29250 cycles (Figure 39h). Figure 39i displays the schematic of the

reaction process to understand the effect of active material coatings on conventional Pb electrode surfaces. It is evident that the anode of a LAB usually suffers substantial crystallization of  $\mathrm{PbSO}_4$ , which detrimentally affects the battery performance. In contrast, the carbon layer with nanosized Pb on the anode surface greatly limits the formation of  $\mathrm{PbSO}_4$  crystals during the discharge process, serving as a key benefit for such devices. To

recapitulate, the AC-Pb coated anode has been established as a promising active material to improve the electrochemical performance of LABs.

# 4.3. Soluble Lead-Acid Flow Batteries

Traditional LABs use  $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{SO}_4$  electrolyte, in which  $\mathrm{Pb}$  compounds are sparingly soluble. For that reason, an insoluble paste containing active materials is generally applied to the electrodes of such systems. It has been widely recognized that traditional LABs are not well suited for LSES purposes owing to their limited shelf life and practical difficulty in manufacturing large batteries. Therefore, new forms of LABs with convenient manufacturing and long cycle life are necessarily required. In 2004, Pletcher and Wills introduced a SLAFB and conducted numerous studies on its intrinsic reaction kinetics and electrochemical performance.[329] Such a storage system is comprised of electrodes prepared by electrochemical plating of active materials onto the current collectors. Its working principle is based on the electrode reactions of  $\mathrm{Pb(II)}$  in methanesulfonic acid. The SLAFB undergoes the following reactions during charge and discharge:

Cathode:

$$
\mathrm {P b O} _ {2} + 4 \mathrm {H} ^ {+} + 2 \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {P b} ^ {2 +} + 2 \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} \quad E ^ {\circ} = 1. 4 7 \mathrm {V} \tag {5}
$$

Anode:

$$
\mathrm {P b} ^ {2 +} + 2 \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {P b} \quad E ^ {\circ} = - 0. 1 3 \mathrm {V} \tag {6}
$$

Overall:

$$
\mathrm {P b O} _ {2} + \mathrm {P b} + 4 \mathrm {H} ^ {+} \leftrightarrow 2 \mathrm {P b} ^ {2 +} + 2 \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} \quad E ^ {\circ} = 1. 6 \mathrm {V} \tag {7}
$$

During charging, Pb is deposited at the anode and  $\mathrm{PbO}_2$  is deposited at the cathode, while both  $\mathrm{Pb}$  and  $\mathrm{PbO}_2$  are dissolved into the electrolyte solution, releasing soluble  $\mathrm{Pb}^{2+}$  during battery discharge.[330] It is evident from the structural schematic of an SLAFB (Figure 40a) that an interelectrode gap of  $4\mathrm{mm}$  is maintained using elastomeric gaskets in order to form the flow channel. The cell, operating either as a two-electrode (parallel plate) reactor or as a four-electrode (two-cell) bipolar stack, uses three flow-chamber configurations (Figure 40b), in which the  $10\mathrm{cm} \times 10\mathrm{cm}$  electrodes are attached in the center of the frames, denoted as A, B, and C. Zhang and co-workers conducted a comparative study for summarizing the difference between static LABs and SLAFBs.[331] Figure 40c,d shows the charge characteristics of two such batteries at different currents. The charge curves reveal complex variations when the SLAFB is charged at a constant current within the range of  $1-6\mathrm{A}$  until its capacity reaches  $4\mathrm{Ah}$  (Figure 40c). To avoid OER and HER, the static LAB is first charged at a constant current until its charge voltage reaches the maximum value, at which time the battery charging is then continued until the current drops to  $0.3\mathrm{A}$ . Consequently, the charge voltage keeps on increasing during constant current charging owing to the gradual increment in the  $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{SO}_4$  concentration (Figure 40c). It has been indicated that the SLAFB shows relatively lower charge potentials than that of the static LAB due to the better reaction kinetics of the former. Figure 40e,f displays the discharge characteristics at various current densities for the SLAFB and static LAB, respectively. It is evident that at low current densities, the SLAFB maintains a relatively flatter voltage curve than the static LAB. In addition, the SLAFB voltage remains almost constant until one of its active materials is fully consumed. Although the overpotential in

both batteries expectedly augments at the same current density, it is higher for the SLAFB than the static LAB. However, this difference may be reduced through optimizing cell design and operating conditions.

The major distinction between the SLAFB and static LAB is that the former exhibits larger voltage polarization than the latter at the same current rate. $^{330}$  It is attributed to the dendrite growth of  $\mathrm{Pb}$  at anode and the partial irreversibility of  $\mathrm{PbO}_2$  at cathode. Despite the proven efficacy of the leveling agents against  $\mathrm{Pb}$  dendrite formation, the overpotential of the cathode remains a key challenge and can be addressed with optimization of the  $\mathrm{Pb}^{2+}/\mathrm{PbO}_2$  couple at the cathode. $^{332}$  Regarding this, Meng and co-workers have reported significant improvement in the EE and the cycle life of SLAFBs in terms of their charging. $^{333}$  The charge/discharge curves in various cycles (Figure 41a) demonstrate that the voltage profile in the 80th cycle is fairly representative of the previous cycles. It is also evident that upon approaching the reduction of CE in the 90th cycle, the flat voltage curve becomes shorter and the sloping region becomes longer. Regardless, a reduced flat voltage still appears in the 95th cycle. The decline in the flat voltage is ascribed to the morphological changes in  $\mathrm{PbO}_2$ , which in addition to being detrimental are not easily reversible. In order to further elucidate the morphological and phase characteristics associated with the different voltage regimes, batteries are potentiostatically charged at 1.8, 1.9, and  $2.0\mathrm{V}$  for  $1\mathrm{h}$  (Figure 41b) for demonstrating various current curves. In addition, the times required for reaching the maximum current and for the nuclei to completely cover the electrode surface have been studied. It has been revealed that the latter phenomenon occurs at  $\sim2\mathrm{min}$  of charging at  $2.0\mathrm{V}$  and  $\sim9\mathrm{min}$  of charging at  $1.9\mathrm{V}$  and never happens during  $1\mathrm{h}$  of charging at  $1.8\mathrm{V}$ . This signifies that a large number of nuclei quickly form and coalesce to create layers of nanoparticles at  $2.0\mathrm{V}$ . In other words, fewer nuclei are formed as the new ones continue to deposit on the electrode surface at low voltages. The observed trend has been further validated by various SEM micrographs (Figure 41c-e). Furthermore, the XRD results (Figure 41f) revealed that a mixture of  $\alpha-$  and  $\beta-\mathrm{PbO}_2$  was always produced upon charging at 1.8, 1.9, and  $2.0\mathrm{V}$ . The Rietveld refinement results determined the ratio of  $\alpha-$  to  $\beta-\mathrm{PbO}_2$  deposited at  $1.8\mathrm{V}$  to be 41:59. At a relatively higher current density during charging at  $1.9\mathrm{V}$ , this ratio was 97:3. However, at  $2.0\mathrm{V}$  charging, XRD peaks became significantly broadened, indicating that the voltage plateau at  $2.0\mathrm{V}$  is critical in relation to the deposition of  $\mathrm{PbO}_2$  nanoparticles. Concerning the electrochemical performance, the battery exhibits superior discharge capacity during charging at  $2.0\mathrm{V}$  as compared to that at  $1.8\mathrm{V}$  (Figure 41g). The loss in capacity stems from the incomplete dissolution of  $\mathrm{PbO}_2$  particles into solvated  $\mathrm{Pb}^{2+}$  (the inset of Figure 41g). Moreover, the long cycling stability of the SLAFB is greater than 2000 cycles with EE of  $\sim79\%$  (Figure 41h). Overall, the SLAFB is capable of delivering good electrochemical performance upon structural optimization and rational measurements, indicating promising characteristics as LSES devices.

# 4.4. Summary and Future Perspectives for LABs

LABs mainly include two types of batteries in which the flooded battery requires much electrolyte and the VRLAB requires inspection. First, there has been substantial progress in enhancing the energy density for LABs, predominantly by employing carbon materials as current collectors to the electrodes. However, there is still ample room for further

![](images/1292da0915516eb536c8bca71c155e302472c6969d347dd379774871f9dca0a2.jpg)  
a

![](images/193cad5fab499ff0baa44b0554c4a3e4a98f7fa2e2babfa24cca69bc9947b4d7.jpg)  
b  
Figure 42. (a) Development history of NBBs with major milestones. (b) Phase diagram of Na-S system at high, intermediate, and room temperature. Reproduced with permission from ref 340. Copyright 1972 The Electrochemical Society.

development of carbon materials and metal oxides as anode additives to improve the electrochemical performance in HRPSoC conditions. Furthermore, the carbon additives and the  $\mathrm{PbO}_2$  structure need intensive investigation to address the problem of anodic sulfation of the active materials. In other words, the functionality of the additives in terms of increased electrochemical reactivity of the active mass and restriction of the growth of nonreversible  $\mathrm{PbSO}_4$  crystals should be enhanced. It is imperative to point out that carbon has huge potential in bringing about the upcoming breakthrough in LAB technology. Advanced LAB designs based on harnessing carbon-related materials could render its development economical and practical. Owing to their extensive commercialization to date, LABs are potentially considered for LSES in the future, providing further improvements in their energy densities and overall cycling performances.

In contrast to conventional LABs, SLAFBs offer preferential advantages, primarily because methanesulfonic acid is less aggressive toward battery components than  $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{SO}_4$ . Typically, the undivided SLAFB requires an electrolyte containing  $\mathrm{Pb}^{2+}$  to reduce the need for equipment to store and pump the electrolyte, thereby leading to significant cost reduction. The ability of SLAFBs to operate without an expensive Nafion membrane further brings down the battery cost. Regardless, the major challenges related to the phase changes at both electrodes

remain. For future work, the morphology of the  $\mathrm{Pb}$  metal must be controlled to avoid uneven dendrite deposition, despite the rapid kinetics of the anodic reaction. As for the cathode, its reaction kinetics are much slower than those of the anode. Moreover, owing to the poor adhesion of  $\mathrm{PbO}_2$  particles onto the electrode and the resulting damage on the surface, there is a dire need to enhance the performance of the SLAFB system by optimizing the morphology of  $\mathrm{PbO}_2$  particles through balancing their compactness and porosity.

# 5. MOLTEN SALT BATTERIES

# 5.1. Introduction

MSBs are exclusively known for their electrochemical energy storage capacity using molten salts as electrodes and electrolytes. $^{334,335}$  These batteries offer a multitude of potential advantages, such as nonvolatility, nonflammability, and high conductivity of molten salts, high energy, and impressive power densities for stationary applications. $^{335-337}$  In order to keep the electrodes and electrolytes in molten states, the MSBs are generally operated under relatively high temperatures. To date, several MSB systems have been successfully developed, including traditional high-temperature (HT) and intermediate-temperature (IT) Na-S batteries, ZEBRA batteries, and the most recently developed liquid-metal batteries (LMBs) and

![](images/d8836cc4e6ddac2bc881d7aa7506ebfaef9e7bb5703c4b85937c7186a3f0a09e.jpg)

![](images/8f3ec8033701bb962e17fc1defde513007803b20c14793c24df475b7e874c0a8.jpg)

![](images/f672e96cb278606086a0db5ce36ed5b082427e3456ab7c8c5a5e0c44275e3657.jpg)  
Figure 43. (a) Diagram of schematic structure (left) and operating principle (right) of Na-S battery. (b) Voltage curves of a Na-S battery at different states. Reproduced with permission from ref 351. Copyright 2013 Elsevier. (c) Voltage-cycle number curve of a 650 Ah single Na-S battery. (d) Cycling stability of a 30 Ah Na-S cell. Reproduced with permission from ref 345. Copyright 2008 Elsevier.

![](images/aced7ccd313b17c7464902c9dabd1a23be210d7c1993581ef9054f5009c94c88.jpg)

solid electrolyte-based liquid Li (SELL) batteries. In this section, we discuss MSB systems by reviewing their developmental history and current status. In addition, the potential for their practical applications toward GSES and safety considerations are also highlighted.

# 5.2. Traditional Na-S Batteries

Compared with conventional battery technologies, energy storage systems employing Na anodes have considerable advantages in stationary energy storage applications, such as utility-based load leveling (LL) and peak shaving in smart grids, and emergency and uninterruptible power supplies.[335,338] Currently, two basic battery systems that use a molten Na anode are representative of HT energy storage devices including Na-S batteries and Na-metal chloride batteries, the developmental history of which is shown in Figure 42a. The Na-S battery technology was originally developed in 1966 by Ford Motors and later was transferred to Japanese NGK INSULATORS.[339] Although the aforementioned battery systems are fundamentally different, their basic configurations and operations are similar. Therefore, such electrochemical batteries are usually called sodium/  $\beta$  batteries (NBBs). Numerous studies have been reported toward their significant advancements. As is evident in Figure 42b, Na-S batteries are further categorized into HT ( $\sim 300 - 350^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ), IT ( $\sim 130 - 180^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ), and room-temperature (RT) ( $\sim 25 - 35^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ) Na-S batteries based on their

operating temperatures. $^{340}$  Since the operating temperature range of the RT Na-S battery is relatively low and the melting point of the electrodes cannot be reached, it is normally not recognized as a MSB. In contrast, the operating temperature range of IT Na-S batteries is slightly higher than the melting points of S  $(115.21^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$  and Na  $(98^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$ , therefore qualifying it to be a MSB system. $^{341}$  Thus, sticking with the scope of this section, we mainly discuss HT and IT Na-S batteries.

For grid energy storage applications, the Na-S battery has been offering bright prospects since the 1980s due to its high theoretical energy density ( $\sim 760$  Wh  $\mathrm{kg}^{-1}$ ), high CE (approaching  $100\%$ ), long operational life ( $>15$  years), and negligible self-discharge.[45,139,342-346] In 1984, NGK INSULATORS and Tokyo Electric Power Company jointly launched a Na-S battery development program accomplishing great achievements in the past two decades.[339] Later, in 2003, Na-S batteries were commercialized for megawatt-scale load leveling. In the subsequent year, DoE and NYSERDA initiated a three-year-long project in which the power storage ability of a 1.2 MW/7.2 MWh Na-S battery system developed by NGK INSULATORS was demonstrated, promising bright prospects for its commercialization. In 2006, China also began to develop these batteries vigorously at the system level, with the goal of commercializing Na-S batteries for GSES. Later, in 2007, a 165 MWh Na-S battery was designed and employed successfully to

stabilize the power grids. $^{342}$  In 2010, the production capacity of NGK INSULATORS was increased to  $150000\mathrm{kW}$  every year. To date, besides Japan, other countries such as France and United States also have passed grid storage tests of Na-S battery technology. $^{347}$

The traditional  $\mathrm{Na - S}$  battery employs a  $\mathrm{Na}\left(T_{\mathrm{m}} = 98^{\circ}\mathrm{C}\right)$  anode, a  $S\left(T_{\mathrm{m}} = 115^{\circ}\mathrm{C}\right)$  cathode, and  $\mathrm{Na}$  ion conductive  $\beta$  alumina ceramic as the electrolyte and separator.[344,345] The most important advantage of the tubular structure of the  $\mathrm{Na - S}$  battery is its ample room to compensate the volumetric change of anodes and cathodes during battery cycling, thereby minimizing the sealing area and rendering it a practical battery design. Apart from tubular structure, planar designs of  $\mathrm{Na - S}$  batteries have also been reported.[348,349] In order to overcome the resistance posed by the flow of  $\mathrm{Na}^{+}$  through the  $\beta$  -alumina solid electrolyte (BASE),  $\mathrm{Na - S}$  batteries are operated at about  $300 - 350^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  for keeping the electrodes in molten state.[350] The principle of the  $\mathrm{Na - S}$  battery is predominantly predicated on the electrochemical reaction between Na and S forming sodium polysulfide  $(\mathrm{Na}_{2}\mathrm{S}_{x})$ .[42] In general, the electrochemical reactions of a typical HT  $\mathrm{Na - S}$  battery are as follows:[342]

Anode:

$$
\mathrm {N a} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {N a} ^ {+} + \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \tag {8}
$$

Cathode:

$$
x S + 2 e ^ {-} + 2 N a ^ {+} \leftrightarrow N a _ {2} S _ {x} \tag {9}
$$

Overall:

$$
2 \mathrm {N a} + x \mathrm {S} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {N a} _ {2} \mathrm {S} _ {x} \quad (x = 3 - 5)
$$

$$
E ^ {\circ} = 1. 7 8 - 2. 0 8 \mathrm {V} \text {a t} 3 5 0 ^ {\circ} \mathrm {C} \tag {10}
$$

Figure 43a illustrates the tubular structure of  $\mathrm{Na-S}$  battery having a central Na anode, which results in higher volumetric energy density and helps in maintaining battery operating temperature. As shown in Figure 43a, the Na at the anode is oxidized to  $\mathrm{Na^{+}}$  ions during discharging, releasing electrons to the external circuit, while  $\mathrm{Na^{+}}$  passes through the BASE toward the cathode and reacts with S to form  $\mathrm{Na_2S_x}$  polysulfide. $^{345}$  Upon charging, however, this reaction is reversed: sodium polysulfide splits back into Na and S separately for returning to the anode and cathode, respectively. Since both electrodes are in the liquid state, only  $\mathrm{Na_2S_x}$  species with low melting points are allowed to be formed during battery discharge. Upon continuous discharge, when  $x$  approaches 2, a solid phase with a high melting point is produced, for instance solid  $\mathrm{Na_2S}$  is undesired as it increases the battery impedance to hinder further discharge. $^{342}$  The electrochemical performance of traditional Na-S batteries is also shown in Figure 43b-d. Figure 43b shows charge and discharge curves for the Na-S couple vs.  $\mathrm{Na_2S}$  utilization rate. Based on the utilization rate, composition of the final reaction products during charging was close to  $\mathrm{Na_2S_4}$  while the composition during discharging was close to  $\mathrm{Na_2S}$ . When the current increases from 10 to 60 A, the discharge voltage can remain over 1.5 V. Particularly, Figure 43d illustrates that the Na-S cell with a capacity of 30 Ah demonstrates impressive cycle stability even after 1200 cycles. $^{345}$

With regards to the solid electrolyte in a Na-S battery, the ideal materials should have characteristics of high  $\mathrm{Na^{+}}$  conductivity, chemical and thermal stability, mechanical strength, volumetric density with minimum porosity, inertness toward alkali metals (Na anode), and low electronic

conductivity. Among commercial electrolytes, the tubular shaped  $\beta\text{-Al}_2\mathrm{O}_3$  is a critical component, whose ionic conductivity is comparable to that of aqueous  $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{SO}_4$  electrolyte, indicating that the former can be used as a solid electrolyte in HT electrochemical cells. $^{42}$  The  $\beta\text{-Al}_2\mathrm{O}_3$  is a nonstoichiometric compound of  $\mathrm{Al}_2\mathrm{O}_3$  and  $\mathrm{Na}_2\mathrm{O}$  with typical crystalline structure and chemical formula denoted by  $\mathrm{NaAl}_{(5-11)}\mathrm{O}_{17}$ . It has two distinct crystal structures: a double helix shaft-based  $\beta\text{-Al}_2\mathrm{O}_3$  ( $\mathrm{Na}_2\mathrm{O} \cdot (9-11)\mathrm{Al}_2\mathrm{O}_3$ ; hexagonal,  $P6_3/mmc$ ;  $\alpha_{\mathrm{o}} = 0.559~\mathrm{nm}$ ;  $c_{\mathrm{o}} = 2.261~\mathrm{nm}$ ), $^{352}$  and triple helix shaft-based  $\beta''\text{-Al}_2\mathrm{O}_3$  ( $\mathrm{Na}_2\mathrm{O} \cdot (5-7)\mathrm{Al}_2\mathrm{O}_3$ ; rhombohedral,  $R3_m$ ;  $\alpha_{\mathrm{o}} = 0.560~\mathrm{nm}$ ;  $c_{\mathrm{o}} = 3.395~\mathrm{nm}$ ). $^{353}$

Compared with  $\beta\text{-Al}_2\mathrm{O}_3$ , the  $\beta''\text{-Al}_2\mathrm{O}_3$  has higher  $\mathrm{Na}^+$  ionic conductivity with higher  $\mathrm{Na}^+$  ratio. However, during the preparation process, the  $\beta''\text{-Al}_2\mathrm{O}_3$  crystal is unstable owing to the decomposition of the  $\beta''$  phase near the sintering temperature. Therefore, in order to stabilize  $\beta''\text{-Al}_2\mathrm{O}_3$  crystals, metallic ions (such as  $\mathrm{Li}^+$  and  $\mathrm{Mg}^{2+}$ ) are generally used to replace  $\mathrm{Al}^{3+}$  in their crystalline phase. In practical Na-S cells, the electrolyte is comprised of a mixture of  $\beta-$  and  $\beta''\text{-Al}_2\mathrm{O}_3$  particles.[45] Following the discovery of  $\beta\text{-Al}_2\mathrm{O}_3$ , many studies have reported new electrolytes with higher ionic conductivity and lower cost for Na-S batteries. Among such electrolytes, NASICON is known for its large number of interconnected holes that can be occupied by additional  $\mathrm{Na}^+$ .[354] However, the electrochemical performance of Na-S batteries using this electrolyte does not exceed that containing  $\beta\text{-Al}_2\mathrm{O}_3$  electrolyte.

To date, the Na-S battery is considered to be one of the most practical choices for stationary energy storage applications owing to the following advantages:342,345,355 high theoretical energy density (at  $350^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , the theoretical specific energy density of the battery is 760 Wh  $\mathrm{kg}^{-1}$ ), high CE (approaching  $100\%$  along with low self-discharge), high cycle life (the number of working cycles for Na-S battery is about four times more than high-quality commercially available LABs), no intermediate reaction and over  $85\%$  average conversion efficiency, environmental friendliness (the batteries are completely sealed, and more than  $99\%$  of battery materials are recyclable; however, the Na component must be handled with utmost care as it can act as hazardous substance), low material cost (Na and S are both naturally abundant), and simple field maintenance (since the Na-S battery need no pumps, valves, or exchangers, field maintenance requirements are limited to regular inspections and cleaning only).345

In spite of all these upsides, Na-S batteries face some major technical challenges that have not yet been completely addressed.[356] The detrimental corrosion of  $\mathrm{Na}_2\mathrm{S}_5$  and S species is dominant in Na-S cells, caused by their high operating temperatures and high reactivity of the molten electrodes. In addition, macroscopic cracking of the solid electrolyte is caused by internal stress inside the ceramic or by externally applied stress. Furthermore, in case of microcracks or other defects on the electrolyte surface, Na dendrites begin to form, which gradually propagate into the ceramic. Upon complete penetration of dendrites into electrolyte, the battery experiences short circuiting. In other words, upon damage to  $\beta\text{-Al}_2\mathrm{O}_3$ , the molten S is in direct contact with liquid Na, resulting in a violent reaction, which in turn may cause a fire hazard or even an explosion. In short, in order to ensure safety, the selection of materials for containers and other cell components is a stringent and expensive requirement. To further address the safety concerns in Na-S batteries, it is important to point out that the development of high capacity and low-cost Na-S batteries that operate at lower temperatures (intermediate or even room

![](images/88e2d9d630ddba6e73fd2c55977deb6273782b9b200c706db0404c8afe40934e.jpg)  
a  
Battery Cell

![](images/22f64f8873212860a76632ab799393e98cdb97e29f588bd49e1c58dcc0c4ba2b.jpg)  
Battery Module

![](images/402c5391a3cb1438b94dc2eac7c540a95a28d1b846a9f6f011d577d9e7e8e233.jpg)  
Battery Container

![](images/4e110bc903ce765c3fb88276731cda0aa389e0505dd7714dcd6ca46bad6a563f.jpg)

![](images/b375e72c7b041d7bfc0b43a808a93446fe97ef2fc5e36321ce171b1bf9fa7a9a.jpg)

![](images/0ced0ef05ac80b3f55c68082100f8150571784fe09a9e00ee4565eae2cc95614.jpg)

![](images/0dd31f963fa5ede26a5dfaf76f17e3ebedb08dbb4b516133931b1bea3cf8a241.jpg)  
b  
Figure 44. (a) The structure of NAS battery system (Courtesy of NGK INSULATORS www.ngk-insulators.com). (b) Global distribution of Japanese NGK INSULATORS NAS batteries. (Courtesy of NGK INSULATORS www.ngk-insulators.com).

temperature) is an effective strategy to explore. In this regard, numerous studies have reported RT and IT Na-S batteries, $^{57,338,357-371}$  especially all-solid-state RT Na-S batteries. $^{371,372}$  In addition to the intrinsic safety, all-solid-state

batteries offer benefits comprising of long cycle life, diverse geometric configurations, low cost, and minimal risk of leakage and volatilization. For instance, a bulk-type all-solid-state Na-S battery employing  $\mathrm{Na}_{3}\mathrm{PS}_{4}$  glass-ceramic as a solid electrolyte

yields a capacity of  $1100\mathrm{mAhg}^{-1}$  at RT, which is two times higher than that of a traditional HT Na-S battery. $^{372}$  Similarly, an all-solid Na-S battery using poly(ethylene oxide) (PEO) polymer electrolyte has demonstrated high initial discharge capacity of  $505\mathrm{mAhg}^{-1}$  of S with stable electrochemical performance throughout its repetitive cycling. $^{365}$  In spite of all the aforementioned advantages of RT all-solid-state Na-S batteries, they are still subject to rigorous experimental research owing to serious self-discharge and short cycle life, indicating that the RT Na-S battery technology is yet to mature in order to realize its widespread commercialization, which is therefore not discussed in this review.

Another key concern for the  $\mathrm{Na}-\mathrm{S}$  battery is its capacity limitation up to one-third of that computed theoretically for the complete reduction of S to  $\mathrm{Na}_2\mathrm{S}$ . In other words, the reduction takes place before the formation of  $\mathrm{Na}_2\mathrm{S}_2$ . Further, the battery poses challenges in terms of its ability to achieve temperature-dependent ion transport through the ceramic electrolyte, which is directly related to the extent of wetting of the solid electrolyte through molten Na. This problem is attributed to the formation of a surface oxide film.[350] In addition, a large amount of electrical energy is consumed for maintaining the high temperature during  $\mathrm{Na}-\mathrm{S}$  battery operation, which significantly increases the thermal management costs.

Aiming at LL, UPS, and emergency power supply (EPS), the Na-S battery is mainly applied in the fields of load balancing of substations (including peak shaving and valley filling), renewable energy power generation for suppressing the impact of intermittent energy on power grid stabilization, and auxiliary backup, which is responsible for rotating backup and emergency backup.[42,350,356] Since the Na-S systems are mainly employed in stationary applications, the design of such battery systems holds critical importance.[344,345,352,373] Hitherto, the NAS battery systems universally adopt modular concept, which has been successfully applied by NGK INSULATORS in many projects. The modular concept implies that independent battery-level modules are composed of various batteries electrically connected in series and parallel configurations in a thermal enclosure (Figure 44a). The battery itself provides the required voltage, energy, and power by connecting these modules in series and parallel. The resulting battery is then combined with the power conversion system (PCS) and controller to form an integrated facility that can then be connected to the power system (Figure 44b). For example, a large 50 MW NAS battery energy storage station commissioned in 2016 is in Japan. Currently, the manufacturing of commercial NAS battery stations is on the rise with NGK INSULATORS as the leading manufacturer. Table 7 and Figure 44b delineate the global distribution of Japanese NGK INSULATORS's NAS batteries.

In a nutshell, Na-S batteries have substantial potential to be juxtaposed with currently commercialized Li-ion and redox-flow batteries.[63] Despite the bottlenecks, including poor safety at

Table 7. Global Sites of Japanese NGK INSULATORS NAS Batteries  

<table><tr><td>location</td><td>sites</td><td>energy capacity (MWh)</td></tr><tr><td>Japan</td><td>167</td><td>2600</td></tr><tr><td>Middle East and Africa</td><td>10</td><td>650</td></tr><tr><td>North America</td><td>10</td><td>150</td></tr><tr><td>Europe</td><td>6</td><td>270</td></tr></table>

high temperature and difficult heat management restricting their furtherance, future research pertaining to Na-S battery has pivoted to reducing the operating temperature and costs and improving its overall safety. $^{334,338,363}$  It is worth noting that the efforts to deal with severe self-discharge of low-temperature and IT Na-S batteries along with the fragile sealing of flat-type cells may play an important role in further expanding applications of Na-S batteries.

# 5.3. IT Na-S Batteries

In light of the safety problems of HT Na-S batteries, high operating costs, and limited energy density, new studies have advanced this technology toward intermediate- and low-temperature Na-S systems.[341,362,363,374] IT Na-S batteries have the ability to reduce the cost and safety issues associated with the conventional HT Na-S battery, which makes them attractive for renewable energy integration and other grid-related applications. Therefore, herein we delineate the advances pertaining to IT Na-S batteries.

Lu and co-workers reported an IT  $(150^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$  Na-S battery, in which a dense  $\beta''\text{-Al}_2\mathrm{O}_3$  solid membrane and tetraglyme are employed as the electrolyte separator and the cathode electrolyte solvent, respectively. [362] Figure 45a,b displays a schematic of a single IT Na-S cell and the initial discharge and charge curves, respectively. It can be seen that the initial discharge capacity is about  $473\mathrm{mAhg^{-1}}$  ,which is comparable to that for RT Na-S batteries reported previously, [367,372] along with relatively good rate capacity and cycling stability (Figure 45c). Moreover, in situ Raman measurements during cell operation show that during the discharge process, polysulfides are formed in the order of  $\mathrm{Na_2S_5 + S\leftrightarrow Na_2S_5 + Na_2S_4\leftrightarrow Na_2S_4}$ $+\mathrm{Na}_{2}\mathrm{S}_{2}$  , and the sequence is reversed during the charge process.

In recent years,  $\mathrm{Na}_3\mathrm{Zr}_2\mathrm{Si}_2\mathrm{PO}_{12}$  has been integrated into IT batteries as a solid electrolyte. Park et al. $^{374}$  reported an IT Na-S battery based on  $\mathrm{Na}_3\mathrm{Zr}_2\mathrm{Si}_2\mathrm{PO}_{12}$  electrolyte, the conductivity of which increased when it was doped with Ge at  $150^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . In 2019, Nikiforidis et al. $^{363}$  found that in  $1.5 - 3\mathrm{M}$  solution of sodium pentasulfide dissolved in tetraglyme ether, the stability of an IT Na-S battery showed improvement at its operating temperature of  $150^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . Having predefined the working parameters of the battery, including threshold temperature, solubility of the polysulfide, current density range, and suitability of the carbon-based materials on the cathode side, the battery stiffness and the amplified structure have been investigated. As shown in Figure 45d, the volt-ampere efficiency  $(\eta_{\mathrm{V}})$ , calculated from the average discharge potential during charging, is related to the operating temperature of the cell. Within the tested range of current density, the increase in the temperature yields a higher  $\eta_{\mathrm{V}}$  value. Further investigation reveals the lowest operating temperature of the battery to be  $150^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  with salt-free polysulfide cathode electrolyte and tubular configuration. Furthermore, the electrochemical study of the cathode demonstrates excellent long-term performance in terms of continuous cycling for 42 days. The biggest upside of this battery is the scalable capacity of its volume by 10-fold, reaching a capacity of  $790\mathrm{mAh}$  (Figure 45e). However, the 10-fold increase in volume is not linearly related to its capacity and volumetric density, indicating that mass transport inhibits the capacity improvement during amplification. In short, although the cycling stability of IT Na-S batteries is much higher than that of RT Na-S batteries, there is still a big gap between the former and traditional HT Na-S batteries, limiting the practical applications IT Na-S batteries.

![](images/e13335a62fe3c87cc2a7e822f91bf6826489402e5f0e3ff99ec0ca1f03b2c70c.jpg)

![](images/55ac6c7581dc3641390f73c0375479aef5ea370beb0313dac1747fc80cbf3808.jpg)

![](images/f6cb8e92fa40abe66736e3e7747f1e9a4582388080515c82ad70c4acb5e6d051.jpg)  
Figure 45. (a) Schematic diagram of a single IT Na-S cell. (b) Discharge and charge curves of an IT Na-S battery at  $150^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . (c) Cycling stability of discharge/charge capacities. Reproduced with permission from ref 362. Copyright 2013 Royal Society of Chemistry. (d) Voltaic efficiency  $(\eta_{\mathrm{V}})$  vs. current density  $(j)$  at different temperatures. (e) Under the condition of  $\pm 2.5\mathrm{mAcm}^{-2}$ , change of capacity retention rate of the tubular IT NaS battery in a solution containing  $2.5\mathrm{MNa}_2\mathrm{S}_5$  and TEGDME. Reproduced with permission from ref 363. Copyright 2019 The Electrochemical Society.

![](images/68381fbbd29e8f489859abc01e0b5a8788393ddb5f9e2bf93f442ca4b0bb2459.jpg)

![](images/1442290cb38894df2b6ee92cb705ff8a47e54e17f85074821bac129617eacd8c.jpg)

# 5.4.ZEBRA Batteries

The sodium-nickel chloride  $(\mathrm{Na} / \mathrm{NiCl}_2)$  cell, also known as a ZEBRA battery, is another HT battery based on molten Na anode and  $\beta$ -alumina solid electrolyte (BASE). In the 1980s,  $\mathrm{Na} / \mathrm{NiCl}_2$  batteries were called Zero Emission Battery Research Activity (ZEBRA) batteries, owing to their zero-emission and pollution-free green nature. In addition to the advantages of high energy density, high power density, abundance of employable elements, and low cost, what makes ZEBRA batteries practical is their intrinsic safety, a major concern posed by conventional HT Na-S batteries. Therefore, ZEBRA batteries are considered one of the promising candidates for GSES.

Pertaining to their structural components, ZEBRA batteries consist of a Na anode, a cathode containing mainly  $\mathrm{NiCl}_2 / \mathrm{FeCl}_2$ , and a  $\mathrm{Na}^+$  conducting molten  $\mathrm{NaAlCl}_4$  ( $T_{\mathrm{m}} = 157~^\circ \mathrm{C}$ ) electrolyte. With regards to the cathode materials in ZEBRA cells, various metal halides have been reported to be effective during the battery operation. For instance, chlorides such as  $\mathrm{FeCl}_2^{378,379}$  and  $\mathrm{NiCl}_2^{380,381}$  have been widely used; the former is a more cost-effective alternative than the latter. However,  $\mathrm{Fe}_2\mathrm{O}_3$  is formed during cell overcharge, and  $\mathrm{Fe}^{3+}$  may be transported and penetrate into the alkaline electrolyte, causing electrolytic degradation.[343] Therefore, only a limited amount of  $\mathrm{FeCl}_2$  is added into the ZEBRA cells. Further, Fe is used as a cathode active material additive to provide improved peak power capability under low charge state. The operating temperature of ZEBRA batteries is about  $270 - 300^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , during which the molten  $\mathrm{NaAlCl}_4$  ensures  $\mathrm{Na}^+$  transportation between the alkaline electrolyte and the solid active cathode.[382,383] The electrochemical reactions of  $\mathrm{Na}-\mathrm{NiCl}_2$  cells are described as follows:[313]

Anode:

$$
\mathrm {N a} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {N a} ^ {+} + \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \tag {11}
$$

Cathode:

$$
\mathrm {N i C l} _ {2} + 2 \mathrm {N a} ^ {+} + 2 \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {N i} + 2 \mathrm {N a C l} \tag {12}
$$

Overall:

$$
2 \mathrm {N a} + \mathrm {N i C l} _ {2} \leftrightarrow 2 \mathrm {N a C l} + \mathrm {N i}
$$

$$
E ^ {\circ} = 2. 5 8 \mathrm {V} \text {a t} 3 0 0 ^ {\circ} \mathrm {C} \tag {13}
$$

Figure 46a shows a schematic diagram of the ZEBRA battery. During the discharge process, Na is oxidized to  $\mathrm{Na^{+}}$  and is transported to the cathode through the  $\beta$ -alumina and  $\mathrm{NaAlCl_4}$  electrolyte, where it reacts with  $\mathrm{NiCl_2}$  to form  $\mathrm{NaCl}$  and Ni metal. During the charge process, on the other hand, the reverse process takes place, in which Ni is oxidized and reacts with  $\mathrm{NaCl}$  to form  $\mathrm{NiCl_2}$ . The open-circuit voltage (OCV) of the reaction is  $2.58\mathrm{~V}$  at  $300^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , which is higher than that of the  $\mathrm{Na-S}$  battery.[384] It is worth noting that in addition to facilitating the transfer of  $\mathrm{Na^{+}}$  to the cathode during discharge, another important function of  $\mathrm{NaAlCl_4}$  is to overcome the overcharge of the battery because it serves as a source of  $\mathrm{Na}$  through a reversible reaction  $(2\mathrm{NaAlCl_4} + \mathrm{Ni} \leftrightarrow 2\mathrm{Na} + \mathrm{Ni}(\mathrm{AlCl_4})_2)$ .[383] Upon overcharging, the current collector of Ni reacts with the molten  $\mathrm{NaAlCl_4}$  at a higher voltage of  $3.05\mathrm{~V}$ . Furthermore, the reaction during overdischarging occurs at a lower voltage of  $1.58\mathrm{~V}$ , similar to the one upon battery failure.[343] Figure 46b-d show the electrochemical performance of ZEBRA batteries. Figure 46b shows the discharge curve of a single ZEBRA battery at different cycles. The end-of-charge and -discharge voltages reached cutoff limits of 2.8 and  $1.8\mathrm{~V}$  for the 15th and 30th cycles, respectively. In Figure 46c, the full charge and discharge capacity of  $150\mathrm{mAh}$  was maintained during the initial 15 cycles, and the cell performance started to degrade afterward. Figure 46d shows specific energy of the hybrid battery vs. cycling. The initial charge and discharge energy was 248 and  $230\mathrm{Whkg^{-1}}$

![](images/d7f3a9bd4d1359cd9f04cbe7c9ac712893b8b46b697cbadd4e264dbf5682d664.jpg)

![](images/d9f644c3513ea9366d9066485241bcd6724d0d586a392d6e7040dc0d6ef9e9d2.jpg)

![](images/3831c7ed26f4be9680b868653a4156121f00c4038ba33800b38e794a630fcb48.jpg)  
Figure 46. (a) Schematic diagram of ZEBRA battery. (b) Cell voltage profiles during 1st, 15th, 30th, 45th, and 60th cycles at  $280^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . (c) Charge/discharge capacity vs. cycle number at  $280^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . (d) Charge/discharge energy density vs. cycle number at  $280^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . Reproduced with permission from ref 351. Copyright 2013 Elsevier.

![](images/0963ccb421a07750ba84ff0fbc6c1370747e86769835ee3f783d34a7a7583b05.jpg)

(per cathode and anode), and  $95\%$  and  $97.8\%$ , respectively, of the energy was retained after 60 cycles.

ZEBRA batteries offer a theoretical capacity up to  $305\mathrm{Ahkg^{-1}}$  and theoretical and practical energy densities of 788 and 120 Wh  $\mathrm{kg}^{-1}$ , respectively. The development of ZEBRA batteries is primarily aimed at stationary energy storage applications just like Na-S batteries. Concerning the utility of ZEBRA batteries, they have the similar advantages as Na-S batteries, including low self-discharge and high CE (about  $100\%$ ) thanks to the dense solid electrolyte membrane. In addition, such storage systems can offer additional advantages over Na-S batteries. First, the voltage of ZEBRA batteries is higher (2.58 V at 300 °C) than that of Na-S batteries (2.08 V at 350 °C). Second, ZEBRA batteries are relatively safer in terms of limited violent or hazardous reactions upon cell failure, through prompting Na metal to react with liquid electrolyte ( $\mathrm{NaAlCl_4}$ ) to produce Al and NaCl, an attribute nonexistent in the Na-S systems. Third, significant resistance to cell overcharge and overdischarge is also attributed to ZEBRA battery systems. To explain, the cathode can be overcharged at a higher voltage than usual, however at the expense of current collector consumption, thereby avoiding the risk of breakdown. Fourth, since metal halide cathodes and  $\mathrm{NaAlCl_4}$  melts are relatively less corrosive than sodium polysulfide, ZEBRA batteries offer convenience of selecting a wide range of materials for current collectors and battery cases. Finally, it is worth pointing out that, owing to the impressive discharge tolerance of the ZEBRA battery, the cell is

usually assembled in a discharged state without the need to deal with Na and anhydrous  $\mathrm{NiCl}_2$ .

However, due to the greater solubility of  $\mathrm{NiCl}_2$  in  $\mathrm{NaAlCl}_4$  and the growth and coarsening of Ni particles, the cell capacity eventually tends to decrease, which becomes a key factor hindering the development of ZEBRA batteries. [138,381,382] Another problem that arises from the operating temperature  $(300 - 350^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$  of ZEBRA batteries is the poor wettability of liquid Na on the surface of  $\beta$ -alumina. The high operating temperature not only increases the manufacturing and maintenance costs of the battery, but also causes safety issues. To address these challenges, Lu et al. [387] revealed that the addition of Cs to Na can significantly improve its wettability by enabling the battery to operate at lower temperatures (Figure 47a), down to as low as  $95^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . In order to understand the mechanism of alloying to enhance wettability of Na, the wetting process of Na-Cs alloy has been simulated, the results of which are demonstrated in the form of an image of the droplet containing clusters reaching equilibrium at a certain temperature (Figure 47b). The results show that as the concentration of Cs in the alloy increases, the height of the droplet decreases while the width increases, indicating the gradual improvement in Na wettability. Figure 47c,d shows the performance of a Na-S battery employing Na-Cs alloy, exhibiting superior cycle performance as compared with pure Na anodes.

In summary, the advantages of ZEBRA batteries, especially the intrinsic safety features, outweigh their drawbacks and hence

![](images/618e2b68283951c12bc8a4d1fe1f7667c9dfa0f309b663c62b25dca9ce50cfa7.jpg)  
a

![](images/f6b719104765cff3f62c3c80940d763d08e761cfa834ce299906f7b9fa86aa92.jpg)

![](images/87ae5c8253527fe7c52d3ce7f66d3c795fb177293e350c30836ffab7c85c18ea.jpg)  
Figure 47. (a) Wetting behavior of Na and Na-Cs alloy on the surface of BASE. (b) Simulation of droplet structure of liquid Na-Cs alloy on BASE at  $100^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  (I, Na; II, Na-Cs alloy; III, Cs). (c) Voltage profiles of a Na-Csuntreated BASES cell during the 1st, 5th, 10th, 20th, and 40th cycles  $(95^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$ . (d) Cycle performance of a Na-Csuntreated BASES cell  $(95^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$ . Reproduced with permission from ref 387. Copyright 2014 Springer Nature.

![](images/8eacd7594253ebda9736d551d19d7d02c5cd2d6a485c1e01d35db62260e741a6.jpg)
