Figure 48. (a) Timeline of development history of LMBs. (b) Schematic diagram of a LMB upon (left) discharging and (right) charging.

![](images/bbd38a78cfe52b5217bb9de0578883b5827be80a2a43fc3f2e75ee79ec758aeb.jpg)

promise bright prospects for stationary GSES applications. Since the inception of ZEBRA batteries, Beta Research and Development Ltd. (United Kingdom) has been working on their further research and development.[384] Subsequently, MES-DEA Company acquired the ZEBRA technology and has been striving for its commercialization. Recently, MES-DEA and FIAMM Energy LLC Companies have jointly established a new venture (named FZ Sonick SA) to manufacture sodium metal halide batteries. Currently, one of the major manufacturers of NaCl and  $\mathrm{NiCl}_2$  batteries in the world is FZ Sonick in Stabio (Switzerland) with a 48-TI80 ZEBRA battery pack, which is enclosed in a battery container. Until 2017,  $70 - 80\%$  of the total production of ZEBRA batteries has been intended for backup power

applications, while the remaining percentage  $(10 - 20\%)$  has been allocated to utilization for powering automobiles only, the second biggest market of ZEBRA technology. With regards to the Na-S batteries, ZEBRA batteries exclusively offer long service life, intrinsic safety in risky conditions, and low cost.

# 5.5. Liquid-Metal Batteries

LMBs can be traced back to the development of traditional electrometallurgy in the 1920s (Figure 48a). Although this technology is still in laboratory research phase, it is acknowledged as one of the best prospective candidates for LSES.[388] Operating on the principle of alloying and dealloying of the anode materials to achieve energy release and storage, LMBs yield long cycle lifetimes at low costs.[389] LMBs are composed of

![](images/f82b3b05e2c9543761a05792d5c789d9b21fac3e9aded66cd925e0586a520b40.jpg)  
a

![](images/c60f64693497b620ba70a4ffcefdb2672e8241485d2960706bb5080a7070f096.jpg)  
b

![](images/6fae5018c01778fbab42b00210f790c07322bf343226eb4b0e2468e36fc06fc7.jpg)  
C

![](images/92bca5a246ed5aceb49dca9c7dc5a8192cfa1946c1e552f51b492fb70e49b533.jpg)  
d

![](images/4daa875712839d7234b214898b904667e9e56e1bc9c3eca5667b058a908d55d3.jpg)  
e  
Figure 49. (a) Cross-sectional analysis diagram of a  $\mathrm{Mg}||\mathrm{Sb}$  cell: (i) SEM image of the cathode in the discharged state; (ii) Mg EDS mapping. Reproduced with permission from ref 391. Copyright 2012 American Chemical Society. (b) Profiles of charge/discharge (the 15th cycle) of Li||Sb-Pb cell cycled at  $275\mathrm{mAcm}^{-2}$  ( $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ). (c) Cycle profiles of CE, EE, and discharge capacity density of Li||Sb-Pb cell cycled at  $275\mathrm{mA cm}^{-2}$  ( $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ). Reproduced with permission from ref 402. Copyright 2014 Springer Nature. (d) Profiles during charge/discharge (the 15th cycle) of Ca-Bi||Ca-Sb cell cycled at different current densities (650 and  $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ). (e) Cycling profiles of Ca-Bi||Ca-Sb cell (650 and  $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ). Reproduced with permission from ref 409. Copyright 2014 The Electrochemical Society.

a low-density anode, a medium-density molten salt electrolyte, and a high-density cathode. The two liquid metal electrodes are separated by a molten salt electrolyte, as illustrated in Figure 48b. During discharge, the molten metallic anode (denoted as metal A) is oxidized to  $\mathrm{A}^{m+}$ , releasing electrons to the external circuit, while  $\mathrm{A}^{m+}$  ions pass through the electrolyte to the cathode, reacting with metal B to form molten state AB alloy. In other words, the anode decreases in thickness and, simultaneously, the thickness of cathode layer grows. On the other hand, the process is reversed during battery recharge. Considering this working principle, the operating temperature of LMBs must exceed the melting temperature of all the cell components, categorizing LMBs as a HT energy storage system. Further based on safety and environmental friendliness, the selection of electrode materials must abide by some stringent requirements.[390,391] To begin with, the melting and boiling temperatures of the electrodes should be higher than  $25^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  and lower than  $1000^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , respectively. Second, the selected electrode materials must be nontoxic and stable in nature (nonradioactive elements). Third, the electronic conductivity of metal electrodes should be higher than the ionic conductivity of a typical molten salt electrolyte ( $>1\mathrm{~S~cm}^{-1}$ ). For the suitable selection of electrode materials, their electronegativity and standard electrode potentials also hold importance. Usually, alkali and alkaline earth metals (Li, Na, Mg, K, Ca, S) having low electronegativity and standard potential of less than  $-2.0\mathrm{~V}$  are employed as anodes. On the other hand, metals (Al, Zn, Ga, Cd, In, Sn, Sb, Te, Hg, Ti, Pb, and Bi) with higher electronegativity and standard potential of more than  $-1.0\mathrm{~V}$  are the most apposite choices for cathodes in LMBs.[392]

In addition to the electrodes, the choice of molten salt electrolytes also has a significant impact on the performance of LMBs. High solubility of the liquid metal in molten salt electrolyte results in high self-discharge and low CE. Therefore, the most suitable molten salt electrolytes in LMBs should have low solubility of metals, density difference between anodes and cathodes, high ionic conductivity, low melting temperature, and minimal tendency for side reactions at working voltages. Overall, thanks to the three-layered structure of LMBs, these storage systems offer the following major benefits:

(1) High power characteristics: LMBs exhibit fast electrode charge transfer kinetics without slow de-intercalation of ions on the solid-state electrodes and the electrode-electrolyte interface. In addition, the ions have a fast migration rate  $(>3\mathrm{Scm}^{-1})$  in the highly conductive molten salt electrolytes.  
(2) Low cost: The electrode material elements being abundant in the Earth's crust along with the natural self-preparation of liquid components of LMBs render their manufacturing simpler and more cost-effective as compared with traditional batteries.  
(3) Long cycle life: There is no microelectrode degradation mechanism caused by long-term use of liquid electrodes, such as electrode deformation and dendrite growth.

In summary, LMBs constitute an energy storage technology with massive potential for GSES with low cost, long cycle life, and simple assembly procedure. Nevertheless, some drawbacks hamper their employability for portable equipment. First, the operating temperature (greater than  $200^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ) of LMBs may result in the thermal corrosion of the cell, putting more stringent

![](images/305d6d232bb91400871a5bad4620a934e946eb77d24ecea5ba859df3d2c80fb9.jpg)

![](images/a368422e55994d7f9234a7e85c6a08c6f924541946724080b510e2f6827293d6.jpg)

![](images/0beb500e40685f8a5fdec46995e387c8c353ef55c4318b95d4e438780900a76b.jpg)  
Figure 50. (a) Schematic of a Li||Bi cell. (b) Discharge profiles of Li||Bi cell at different current densities (at  $550^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ). Reproduced with permission from ref 403. Copyright 2015 Elsevier. (c) Charge/discharge profile of  $\mathrm{Ca - Mg}||\mathrm{Bi}$  cell at different current densities ( $550^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ). (d) Cycle profiles of discharge capacity, CE, and EE of  $\mathrm{Ca - Mg}||\mathrm{Bi}$  cell ( $550^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ). (e) Cross section of  $\mathrm{Ca - Mg}||\mathrm{Bi}$  cell in partially discharged state. Reproduced with permission from ref 410. Copyright 2016 Springer Nature.

![](images/2b71ffbdd9ac4cf3adf1ac6d5fcb2b0726640c58044ded0d3ac14152d76cf8ca.jpg)

![](images/6c62613160b8b70970e7b0f2f94ec71457c75ebd55906f8ecf49743ce320ab9d.jpg)

requirements on battery components, such as sealing devices. In addition, the low equilibrium cell voltage  $(< 1.0\mathrm{V})$  indicates the limited energy density of LMBs. Furthermore, some LMBs have severe self-discharge rates due to the high solubility of the electrode components in the molten salt electrolyte, requiring great consideration in the selection of electrode materials. All these disadvantages imply that LMBs are more suitable for stationary storage applications.

The chronological developmental history of LMBs is shown in Figure 48a. After their inception in the 1920s from the development of conventional electrometallurgy, the prototype three-liquid-layer Hoopes cell was developed by the Aluminum Company of America (Alcoa) for electrolytic production of high-purity aluminum.[393] Forty years later, Yeager proposed the thermally regenerative battery after which General Motors Corporation, Atomics International, and Argonne National Laboratory began to develop such a three-layer liquid thermal regeneration battery on a large scale.[394-401] Since 2006, Sadoway at Massachusetts Institute of Technology has conducted in-depth studies on LMBs and developed many electrode materials, such as Na-Bi, Mg-Sb, Li-Pb-Sb, and Ca-Mg-Sb.[334,402-404] Furthermore, in 2010, the Ambri company initiated manufacturing of LMBs on a commercial scale. In 2016, a Li-Sn-Sb system with long cycle life and low cost was proposed.[405] The LMBs are basically composed of stainless steel cases, insulating ceramics, current collectors, and other components.[406] Moreover, there have been many exciting studies pertaining to LMBs comprising different metal

systems. $^{337,391,402-405,407-417}$  In this section, we discuss several representative LMBs for potential applications in GSES.

In 2012, Sadoway and co-workers proposed a HT (700 °C)  $\mathrm{Mg}||\mathrm{Sb}$  LMB composed of a Mg anode,  $\mathrm{MgCl}_2 - \mathrm{KCl} - \mathrm{NaCl}$  molten electrolyte, and Sb cathode.391 Upon cycling at  $50\mathrm{mA}$ $\mathrm{cm}^{-2}$  for  $10\mathrm{h}$ , this cell can achieve a discharge voltage of  $0.4\mathrm{V}$  with CE of  $97\%$  and VE of  $71\%$ . Additionally, SEM and EDS analyses indicated that this cell did not undergo any obvious corrosion of the current collectors and walls, as shown in Figure 49a. The results also revealed that it is viable to reduce the thickness of the electrolyte or work alternatively at a lower current density to improve cell efficiency. The specific energy of this  $\mathrm{Mg}||\mathrm{Sb}$  cell and its total cost were computed to be  $25\mathrm{Wh}$ $\mathrm{kg}^{-1}$  and approximately  $\$375\mathrm{kWh}^{-1}$ , respectively.

Following the introduction of  $\mathrm{Mg}||\mathrm{Sb}$  LMB, in 2014, Sadoway et al.  $^{402}$  put forward the idea of a more advanced LiSb-Pb LMB. Such a cell is composed of a molten-state Li anode, a liquid antimony-lead alloy as cathode (Li/Pb/Sb = 45:38:17) and LiF-LiCl-LiI molten electrolyte (its molar ratio is 20:50:30 and the melting point is  $430^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ). The LiSb-Pb system operates at a high temperature of  $\sim 450^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , along with design capacity up to 1.9 Ah. A typical charge/discharge voltage profile and the cycling performance are shown in Figure 49b,c, respectively. It can be seen that, at a high charge and discharge current density of  $275\mathrm{mAcm}^{-2}$ , the recorded discharge voltage is  $0.73\mathrm{V}$ , which is three times higher than that of the MgSb LMB. Upon electrochemical testing, the battery exhibited an excellent CE of  $98\%$  and RTE of  $73\%$ , while maintaining an initial capacity of  $94\%$  after 450 cycles of full discharge. Upon

![](images/0b1c434667c18520e8204abd2bdb32e60247dd61d5778feaabf0851b4f0ada6e.jpg)  
a

![](images/4d12201880b66c3f67721b34feb95475ed68d31e88e8323bbb221cc0f43d5600.jpg)

![](images/57c6c9b0b405d5c2762094e1d242717f50113ecdf82071a1fb7fbaa9bd0d547e.jpg)  
Figure 51. (a) Schematics of Li-Pb||PbCl2cell and its discharge process. (b) Profiles of charge and discharge of Li-Pb||PbCl2cell at  $150\mathrm{mAcm}^{-2}$ $(410^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$  . (c) Cycle profiles of CE, discharge capacity, and EE of Li-Pb||PbCl2cell  $(410^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$  . (d) Optical image of the bottom view of a porous TiN membrane after 100 cycles. (e) SEM image of the area indicated in panel d. Reproduced with permission from ref 414. Copyright 2018 Springer Nature.

![](images/16b80c480e8569384173f12bbc26117072d452ba34ee009e75b5a9e0d87d5bf3.jpg)

![](images/eeeed03b75f5570d628269bd55139f99243add742826df794d2ed0ba16eb0baf.jpg)

further cycling, the battery manages to retain more than  $85\%$  of the initial capacity after 10 years of cycling. Furthermore, after  $1800\mathrm{h}$  of operation, the cross-section of the battery does not show any significant sign of corrosion of its components. Similarly, another study proposed an alternative Li|Pb-Sb electrochemical system, which employs a liquid lithium anode, a liquid antimony-lead alloy cathode  $(\mathrm{Li} / \mathrm{Pb} / \mathrm{Sb} = 52.4:19:28.6)$ , and a LiF-LiCl-LiBr molten electrolyte  $(22:31:47\mathrm{mol}\%, T_{\mathrm{m}} = 430^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$ . This battery reveals a charge capacity up to  $62\mathrm{Ah}$  and a discharge voltage of  $0.68\mathrm{V}$  at the current density of  $275\mathrm{mA}\mathrm{cm}^{-2}$ . The specific energy of this Li|Pb-Sb cell was calculated to be about  $104\mathrm{Whkg}^{-1}$  and its cost approximately  $\$65\mathrm{kWh}^{-1}$ .

Soon after the development of Li||Sb-Pb, Sadoway and coworkers further developed a novel electrochemical system, in which the performance of Ca-Sb alloy as the anode in the LMBs was examined. Specifically, a Ca in  $\mathrm{Bi} / \mathrm{LiCl} - \mathrm{NaCl} - \mathrm{CaCl}_2| \mathrm{Ca}$  in Sb cell was proposed and tested for its performance in a 3-electrode setup: Ca-Bi alloy ( $x_{\mathrm{Ca}} = 0.35$ ) as reference electrode (RE), Ca-Bi alloy ( $x_{\mathrm{Ca}} = 0.15$ ) as counter electrode, and pure Sb as WE. During operation, the equilibrium potential of the Ca-Sb anode was computed to be within the range of  $1.2 - 0.95 \mathrm{~V}$  vs. Ca at the current density of  $50 - 500 \mathrm{~mA} \mathrm{~cm}^{-2}$ . Upon charging and discharging at the current density of  $200 \mathrm{~mA} \mathrm{~cm}^{-2}$  (Figure 49d), the theoretical capacity of the battery was 0.124 Ah and 0.136 Ah at  $650$  and  $700^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$ , respectively. In addition, the CE values of the alloying and dealloying processes were determined to be  $100\%$  at  $650^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$  and  $102\%$  at  $700^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$ , respectively (Figure 49e). Furthermore, the estimated electrode cost of this cell was calculated to be approximately $90  $\mathrm{kWh}^{-1}$ , indicating the enormous potential of this system for GSES applications.

Based on the previous work on  $\mathrm{Mg}\| \mathrm{Sb},\mathrm{Li}\| \mathrm{Pb - Sb},$  and CaBi  $\| \mathrm{Ca - Sb}$  , a self-healing LiLiCl-LiFiBi LMB operating at 550  $^\circ \mathrm{C}$  was proposed.403 Figure 50a shows the structural schematic

of this cell. In order to study the effects of discharge ratio, the cells were discharged at different current densities. When the current density increased from 200 to \(1250\mathrm{mAcm}^{-2}\), the capacity of the electrode only decreased by about \(27\%\), as shown in Figure 50b. Furthermore, the Li||Bi LMB showed stable cycling performance after 1000 charge/discharge cycles. The battery also demonstrated CE of \(99.7\%\), EE of \(\sim 70\%\), and capacity retention of \(96\%\) after 1000 cycles, indicating a substantially small capacity decay per cycle (0.004% per cycle). Moreover, the system also demonstrated that the battery prototype has much room to scale up (10-100 times) as compared to its original capacity. However, for the 15 cm diameter cell, the material cost is computed to be about $220-242\mathrm{kWh}^{-1}\), which needs to be further reduced for commercial applications as compared to other systems available in the market.

In 2016, Sadoway and co-workers pioneered a novel  $\mathrm{Ca}-\mathrm{Mg}\| \mathrm{Bi}$  LMB system.410 Such cells are composed of a  $\mathrm{Ca}-\mathrm{Mg}$  anode (the molar ratio is 90:10), a Bi cathode, and a  $\mathrm{LiCl}-\mathrm{CaCl}_2$  molten electrolyte, operating at a temperature of  $550^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . Figure 50c illustrates the charge-discharge profiles of the  $\mathrm{Ca}-\mathrm{Mg}\| \mathrm{Bi}$  cell at various current densities ( $100-955\mathrm{mA~cm}^{-2}$ ). It is evident that, at the current density of  $100\mathrm{mA~cm}^{-2}$ , the discharge voltage of the  $\mathrm{Ca}-\mathrm{Mg}\| \mathrm{Bi}$  system was about  $0.8\mathrm{V}$ , and the capacity remained unchanged (about  $0.15\mathrm{Ah}$ ) even after 1400 cycles. Moreover, CE close to  $99\%$  and EE approaching  $70\%$  were achieved (Figure 50d). Figure 50e displays the cross-section of a  $\mathrm{Ca}-\mathrm{Mg}$  ( $90:10\mathrm{mol}\%$ )|Bi cell in a partially discharged state. After 400 cycles at  $550^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , the chemical analysis by direct current plasma emission spectrometry shows no corrosion on both electrodes and the current collector. The specific energy of such a  $\mathrm{Ca}-\mathrm{Mg}\| \mathrm{Bi}$  cell was calculated to be about  $36.8\mathrm{Wh~kg}^{-1}$ .

![](images/ef944d9b90d48e2f1da6f124ad72daa8daaa3123e304c91e433434f8c5c23812.jpg)

![](images/b09743cb74159dc0f7c7a659f9fcfa03d09e9b794b07c1e65e2057f38b8d9272.jpg)

![](images/8cb08bf6462c95636b27e866923acf0a85d7e12346c71b3c13c18cc3fccb6693.jpg)

![](images/d50d54de41276bf8abc9dd783b2b0f12f2fb9c6d66ef2726528a72583d920044.jpg)

![](images/a70826ef97fc1cd863d6249224d0a68fb37ece10afa81b60efb5e5391b513abf.jpg)

Jiang et al. [405] reported a LMB based on a LiSn-Sb system, which is composed of a Li anode, an environmentally friendly Sb-Sn cathode, and a LiF-LiCl-LiBr molten salt electrolyte (22:31:47 mol %, \( T_{\mathrm{m}} = 430^{\circ}\mathrm{C} \)). The study investigated the LiSn-Sb cell employing Sb-Sn electrodes with different molar ratios, the optimal value of which was 50:50, as revealed by the electrochemical performance of the cell. At a current density of \( 100\mathrm{mAcm}^{-2} \) and a capacity of 1.5 Ah, the discharge voltage of the LiSn-Sb cells was up to 0.8 V, which is much higher than that of the MgSnSb cell reported previously. [391] Based on the optimal molar ratio of the Sb-Sn cathode, a series of LiSn-Sb cells with different capacities (1.5, 6, 23, and 47 Ah) have been further developed. It was revealed that the discharge voltages of the cells remained about 0.8 V. It is important to point out that the LiSn-Sn cell showed excellent cycling stability even at a large capacity of 21.3 Ah. After 30 cycles, the battery maintained its discharge capacity at 20.6 Ah and the capacity retention of 96.7% along with the minimal capacity decay of 0.0078% per cycle. At a current density of \( 300\mathrm{mAcm}^{-2} \), the CE and round-trip EE exceed 98.5% and 68.5%, respectively. The cost of the LiSn-Sb system was calculated to be \( \\(72 - 81\mathrm{kWh}^{-1} \) and the specific energy about 174 Wh kg\(^{-1}\).

In 2018, Jiang et al. [408] proposed another high voltage LMB yielding high energy density, which is based on Te-Sn alloy cathode. Comprising a Li anode, a Te-Sn alloy cathode, and a LiF-LiCl-LiBr molten salt electrolyte, this Li||Te-Sn cell operated at  $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  with theoretical capacity of 2 Ah. For this system, various compositions of Li||Te and Li||Te-Sn have been investigated as potential candidates for suitable cathodes and have undergone charge and discharge testing at varying current densities. Consequently, the results have proved the efficacy of pure Te in suppressing the capacity fading of the cell, attributed to its low conductivity and high solubility. Further,

the  $\mathrm{Li}||\mathrm{Te}-\mathrm{Sn}$  cell exhibited discharge voltage of  $1.6\mathrm{V}$  at 100 mA cm $^{-2}$ , which is much higher than the LMBs previously reported, such as  $\mathrm{Mg}||\mathrm{Sb},^{391}\mathrm{Li}||\mathrm{Sb}-\mathrm{Pb},^{402}\mathrm{Li}||\mathrm{Bi},^{403}\mathrm{Ca}-\mathrm{Mg}||\mathrm{Bi}^{410}$  and  $\mathrm{Li}||\mathrm{Sb}-\mathrm{Sn},^{405}$  Furthermore, during long-term cycling, the  $\mathrm{Li}||\mathrm{Te}-\mathrm{Sn}$  cell revealed a phenomenal  $100\%$  CE during initial cycles and nearly  $100\%$  capacity retention after 50 cycles. The specific energy was also calculated to be about 495 Wh kg $^{-1}$ . The outstanding properties show great prospects of these systems in energy storage applications with higher specific energy requirements.

In addition to the extensive research on various electrode-based electrochemical systems, there are also a few innovative studies focusing on the electrolytes and membranes in LMBs. For instance, in 2018, Sadoway and co-workers reported a Faradaically selective membrane for LMBs.[414] This porous metal membrane achieves chemical selectivity through the preferred Faraday reaction rather than the regulated ion conduction. Figure 51a describes the anodic reaction at the interface between liquid Li-Pb and the upper surface of the membrane, the cathodic reaction at the interface between liquid Pb and electrolyte, and the Faraday protection reaction at the membrane/electrolyte interface. The LMB constructed by using this membrane is termed as a liquid displacement battery. Figure 51b,c illustrates the electrochemical performance of a Li-Pb||PbCl $_2$  cell (working at  $410^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ) based on the Faradaically selective membrane. It can be seen that over 100 cycles, the nominal voltage, CE, and RTE of the battery are  $1.3\mathrm{V}, 92\%$ , and  $71\%$ , respectively. In addition, Figure 51d,e indicates that this Faradaically selective membrane has the ability to prevent irrecoverable loss of Pb on the anode. Based on this membrane, the battery not only exhibited good performance but also lessened the selection limit for electrode materials. The applications of porous conductive membranes have been

![](images/02bf1839d58682cffa6141c37d0002da12109b8cda11b074c69bfcb2eff069b8.jpg)  
a

![](images/1f1e351fb387e688a4c0ecb9d41fb073f9787dc7934dd5c6864722ccf0b80d0c.jpg)  
b

![](images/e246e720b5ed98cda21ff016f32d01d4a0a8510f7a458bf277da5862a99f54b8.jpg)  
d

![](images/80b44a838edc97cb961e10ffb76b86aa8a8e1f745204d3facd07a7b283414141.jpg)

![](images/04e04f5cd1cfcc964f1e4ea93406a93b57bc4805cf68a7294210f3b156b9e669.jpg)  
e  
Figure 53. (a) Schematic of  $\mathrm{SELL - }$  brass/  $\mathrm{ZnCl_2}$  battery and the starting materials in the cathode. (b) Comparison of cycling stability at  $0.2\mathrm{C}$  rate with the control batteries  $(250^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$  . (c) XRD patterns of the LLZTO tube before and after cycling. Reproduced with permission from ref 336. Copyright 2020 Elsevier. (d) SEM images of cathodic metal particles of the Li-Mo-FeCl $_2$  battery in each stage of the Fe-Mo dealloy-alloy corrosion (scale bar is  $5\mu \mathrm{m}$  ). (e) Schematic illustrating cathode materials in each stage of reactions inside Li-Mo-FeCl $_2$  battery. (f) Cycle profiles of CE, EE, and volumetric capacity of Li-Mo-FeCl $_2$  battery  $(250^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$  . Reproduced with permission from ref 429. Copyright 2020 Wiley-VCH.

successfully demonstrated in multiple all-liquid replacement battery chemistries, showing a wide range of electrochemical applications.

In addition to the experimental advancement, numerical simulations have been carried out to gauge the hydrodynamics and interfacial stability of LMBs.416,418-425 These theoretical simulations have provided substantial support from all aspects for further improvement of LMBs. At present, LMBs show potential advantages in terms of high capacity, simple structure, low manufacturing cost, and long cycle life, thereby promising their prospective applications in LSES. However, such battery systems are still at the stage of laboratory research and have not yet been commercialized on a large scale. The major problems that detrimentally affect the performance of LMBs are their high operational temperature and their susceptibility to the corrosion of battery components (container, collector, sealing device, or insulator) due to highly active anode materials. For example, the Na-based LMBs experience high self-discharge due to high solubility of Na in molten electrolytes. Similarly, the Ca-based LMBs have high melting points. For electrochemical energy storage, Li is the most widely studied anode material with the characteristics of low melting point, high voltage, and specific energy. Moreover, among alkali metals or rare earth metals, the solubility of Li in molten salts is the lowest and is comparable to

that of  $\mathrm{Mg}$ , and the Li halides embody high ionic conductivity and relatively low melting point, therefore, drawing the most attention in the past three decades. In the future, research on LMB systems are expected to emphasize the development of new electrode materials with appropriate thermal management (lower operating temperature) to avoid HT corrosion and leakage.[426,427]

# 5.6. Solid Electrolyte Based Liquid Lithium Batteries

High safety, high energy density, and low cost are the most sought after features in batteries, especially for GSES. Ever since their development, LMBs have showed great potentials for LSES applications. Among LMBs, the one which has grabbed the most consideration among recent research employs molten Li metal as the anode. Such batteries generally use molten metal salt electrolytes, which have at least the following two major problems: (1) the high melting point of Li metal causes the battery to operate at temperatures generally above  $450^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  and the operational costs remain high; (2) the dissolution of Li in molten salts results in battery self-discharge and low CE  $(< 98\%)$ . Keeping an account of these issues, a series of liquid Li metal batteries have been proposed based on a garnet-type  $\mathrm{Li}_{6.4}\mathrm{La}_3\mathrm{Zr}_{1.4}\mathrm{Ta}_{0.6}\mathrm{O}_{12}$  (LLZTO) solid-state electrolyte, generating the so-called solid electrolyte based liquid lithium battery

Table 8. Summary and Comparison of Different MSBs  

<table><tr><td></td><td>traditional Na-S batteries</td><td>IT Na-S batteries</td><td>ZEBRA batteries</td><td>LMBs</td><td>SELL batteries</td></tr><tr><td>safety level</td><td>low</td><td>moderate</td><td>moderate</td><td>low to moderate</td><td>moderate to high</td></tr><tr><td>cost</td><td>$300 kWh-1</td><td>lower than HT Na-S batteries</td><td>$150 kWh-1</td><td>lower than Na-S batteries</td><td>mostly lower than $100 kWh-1</td></tr><tr><td>advantages</td><td>high theoretical energy density, high CE, long lifetime, and low self-discharge</td><td>sofer and cheaper than Na-S batteries</td><td>high theoretical energy density, high CE, long lifetime, and low self-discharge</td><td>high power and simple structure</td><td>high CE and high energy density</td></tr><tr><td>disadvantages</td><td>not safe and high operating cost</td><td>capacity decay</td><td>capacity decay</td><td>thermal corrosion, high self-discharge, and low voltage</td><td>limited cycle life</td></tr><tr><td>applications</td><td>mainly used in stationary applications</td><td></td><td>stationary and transportation fields</td><td></td><td></td></tr></table>

(denoted as SELL battery). Such electrochemical systems have the ability to not only effectively reduce cell operating temperature but also improve safety, thereby confirming the feasibility of the SELL battery concept.[336,360,428,429]

Cui and co-workers pioneered the SELL battery, which is composed of a liquid Li metal anode, a molten Sn-Pb or Bi-Pb cathode, and a LLZTO solid electrolyte, which operates at a temperature of  $240^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . The LLZTO solid-state electrolyte ceramic tube is structured in the battery as an assembly of two cell systems: Li||LLZTO||Sn-Pb (Figure 52a) and Li||LLZTO||Bi-Pb, also called SELL-Sn-Pb and SELL-Bi-Pb, respectively. Upon electrochemical testing, as shown in Figure 52b, the SELL-Sn-Pb battery demonstrates a stable volumetric capacity of about  $254\mathrm{mAhcm}^{-3}$  with the average CE of  $99.98\%$ . Figure 52c shows the voltage distribution of the SELL-Sn-Pb cell at different current densities within the range of  $50 - 300\mathrm{mA cm}^{-2}$ . It can be seen that even at a high current density of  $300\mathrm{mA cm}^{-2}$ , the battery still shows good cycle performance. The discharge voltage plateau is  $0.3\mathrm{V}$  with high power performance, reaching  $62\%$  of the theoretical capacity (Figure 52c). When it comes to the cost, it is computed to be about  $\$0.037\mathrm{g}^{-1}$  for LLZTO material, indicating significant applicability and feasibility of the SELL batteries.

Similarly, Li-S and Li-Se batteries are considered promising candidates for next-generation battery technology because of their high energy density and cost effectiveness. However, current development of such batteries is severely hampered by their lower CE and cycling stability due to the use of solid Li anodes and liquid organic electrolytes. Based on these problems, most recently, high energy density all-solid electrolyte liquid Li-S and Li-Se battery systems (also referred to as SELL-S and SELL-Se, respectively) have been reported, whose potential energy densities exceed 500 Wh  $\mathrm{kg}^{-1}$  and 1000 Wh  $\mathrm{L}^{-1}$ , respectively.[428] Figure 52d presents the structural schematic of a typical SELL-S/SELL-Se battery, composed of a liquid Li metal anode, a high Li storage capacity molten S or Se cathode, and highly dense LLZTO ceramic tubes as the electrolyte to separate the electrodes. These cells generally operate at  $240^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , at which LLZTO ceramic tubes have a high conductivity and hence yield high power densities. During discharge, S is lithiated to form  $\mathrm{Li}_2\mathrm{S}$ , with no polysulfide intermediate, as shown in Figure 52e. In other words, the utilization of the LLZTO solid electrolyte effectively prevents the formation of polysulfides (polysulfide shuttle effect) and Li dendrites. In addition, XRD results show the coexistence of S and  $\mathrm{Li}_2\mathrm{S}$  at  $50\%$  state of charge (SoC) and the presence of  $\mathrm{Li}_2\mathrm{S}$  at  $100\%$  SoC; therefore the cell shows stable electrochemical performance. In 50 cycles at  $0.5\mathrm{C}$ , the SELL-S battery shows a specific capacity of about  $1450\mathrm{mAhg}^{-1}$  along with stable CE of  $99.99\%$  (Figure 52f). In addition, such systems have low costs ( $<\$100\mathrm{kWh}^{-1}$ ), a paramount advantage for LSES applications.

As described previously, molten sodium-nickel batteries based on  $\beta\text{-Al}_2\mathrm{O}_3$  ceramic electrolytes are considered to have bright prospects for LSES. However, their cathodes require a large amount of high-cost Ni powder, which narrows their scope in potential applications. In this regard, the replacement of Ni powder with relatively low-cost metal (e.g., iron, aluminum, zinc, and copper) powder is proposed as viable mitigation strategy to widen the applications of ZEBRA batteries. Such proposition in turn has its own shortcomings in terms of high solubility of charge products of low-cost metals (e.g., metal chlorides) in the molten cathode electrolyte  $(\mathrm{NaAlCl}_4)$  and cation exchange between the cathode electrolyte and  $\beta\text{-Al}_2\mathrm{O}_3$  ceramic electrolyte. These disadvantages eventually lead to metal particle growth and, hence, rapid deterioration of overall cell performance along with failure of the ceramic electrolyte.[430,431] In order to further address these issues, a solid electrolyte-based liquid lithium-brass/zinc chloride (SELL-brass/ZnCl₂) battery operating at  $250^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  has been investigated.[336] Figure 53a showcases the schematic of the SELL-brass/ZnCl₂ battery along with the composition of its components. It can be seen that, in addition to a stable garnet-type solid electrolyte (LLZTO) to alleviate the ion exchange problem, this battery employs brass powder as a Zn source in the cathode, which inhibits the growth of Zn particles through reversible Cu-Zn alloying and dealloying during discharge and charge, respectively. In other words, the issue of Zn particle growth is successfully resolved, and good battery cycling is achieved upon using brass powder as a Zn source (Figure 53b). The XRD pattern also indicates good crystal structure stability of the LLZTO tube, as shown in Figure 53c, and the chemistry of the cell reaction and its ability to assemble in the discharged state ensures a high level of safety. Moreover, the low material cost of the battery ( $16\mathrm{kWh}^{-1})$  is achieved by using a  $\mathrm{ZnCl}_2$  cathode, which is more than  $50\%$  lower than ZEBRA cells mentioned previously. In short, the SELL-brass/ZnCl₂ battery has a huge scope for practical applications in GSES through full-cell performance and scale-up validation.

In addition to the  $\mathrm{SELL}-\mathrm{brass} / \mathrm{ZnCl}_2$  battery, Cui and coworkers[429] designed a LLZTO solid electrolyte-based molten lithium-molybdenum-iron(II) chloride cell (denoted as Li-Mo-FeCl $_2$  battery) operating at  $250^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . The cell is composed of a mixture of Fe, Mo, and LiCl serving as cathode, a Li anode, and a garnet-type lithium-ion ceramic electrolyte. In contrast to the conventional battery reaction mechanism, this battery system is acknowledged mainly due to reversible Fe-Mo alloying/dealloying reaction and the delithiation/lithiation process (Figure 53d,e). The porous Mo framework derived from the Fe-Mo alloy inhibits the growth of pure Fe particles. The use of Li anode and Li-ion ceramic electrolyte is conducive to overcoming the corrosion between the cathode and the solid electrolyte. As is evident from the experimental results (Figure

53f), such an electrochemical system not only exhibited stable cycling performance (472 mAh  $\mathrm{g_{LiCl}}^{-1}$  after 300 cycles) but also was highly tolerant to overcharge and overdischarge (3-1.6 V) and freeze-thaw (25-250 °C). In addition, at a similar cell cost ( $12\mathrm{kWh}^{-1}$ ), the Li-Mo-FeCl $_2$  battery yields theoretical gravimetric and volumetric energy densities that are  $\sim 25\%$  higher than those of the ZEBRA battery, reaching 576 Wh kg $^{-1}$  and 2216 Wh L $^{-1}$ , respectively.

In addition to being used as a solid-state electrolyte, the high Li-ion selectivity of LLZTO can be harnessed to obtain high-purity Li metal directly by electrolyzing low-purity  $\mathrm{LiCl}$ ,[432] which is cost-effective and less energy consuming than other conventional methods. To reiterate, all MSBs have earned the status of feasible and advantageous energy storage systems in terms of bulk energy density, weight energy density, remarkable performance, manufacturing cost, energy cost, and reliability.

# 5.7. Safety and Other Aspects of Molten Salt Batteries

In conclusion, MSBs along with many benefits are competitive candidates for LSES applications, although they still have shortcomings in some aspects. Table 8 presents a comparative summary of several types of MSBs. To explain, traditional Na-S and ZEBRA batteries have high theoretical energy densities, long cycle life, and high CEs, thereby offering a broad range of prospects for stationary energy storage and vehicular applications. However, for traditional Na-S batteries, the problems of safety and high operating costs caused by high operational temperatures are considerable factors limiting their further development. It is important to mention here that most of the methods for improving the safety of Na-S batteries commonly revolve around the reduction of their operating temperatures. With lower operational temperatures, Na-S batteries have supplementary benefits in terms of convenience to perform heat management and low operating costs. In short, the pros and cons of Na-S systems render their employability mainly in stationary energy storage applications. Similarly, IT Na-S batteries, operating at about  $\sim 150^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , have begun to attract wide attention. Compared with traditional HT Na-S batteries, the relatively lower operating temperatures of IT Na-S batteries aid in the suppression of causticity of the internal electrodes and improve their safety. However, the biggest problem of IT Na-S batteries is the dissolution and shuttle effect of polysulfides, resulting in rapid capacity loss, thereby rendering them impractical for stable LSES applications.

Concerning ZEBRA batteries, despite their operation under high temperatures, their multilevel safety is substantially higher than the conventional Na-S battery systems. Their inherent safety aspects enable the utility of ZEBRA cells in stationary grid storage applications. On the other hand, the key factors restricting the development of ZEBRA batteries include the decline in their capacities due to the higher solubility of  $\mathrm{NiCl}_2$  in  $\mathrm{NaAlCl}_4$ , and the growth and coarsening of Ni particles, which require further research and improvement.

LMBs pose no risk of damage because of the absence of the separator. However, the operating temperature of LMBs is generally above  $400^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , which may result in the thermal corrosion of the cell, demanding stringent material selection of battery components. In addition, some LMB systems have high self-discharge rates due to the high solubility of the electrodes in the molten salt electrolytes. All these benefits and drawbacks determine that LMBs are suitable for stationary storage applications. When it comes to SELL batteries, using LLZTO solid electrolyte can effectively address the problems experi

enced by LMBs, especially safety. Although previous studies have verified the feasibility of LLZTO in different liquid Li batteries, the cycle life of SELL batteries is still far from the requirements of LSES, and the cost of LLZTO material ( $0.037 g$ ^{-1}) is too expensive for its widespread economical applications.

Safety holds precedence over cost to the general masses when it comes to the energy storage. However, the safety aspect of the battery systems must be taken into further consideration from the dimensions of accidents and abuses that may occur during cycling and general handling, such as mechanical abuse, heat abuse, short circuit, deep discharge, and overcharge.

From a safety point of view, mechanical defects in the ceramic electrolyte are the most critical factor compromising the safety of Na-S batteries. This is because when the ceramic separator structure is destroyed, the S comes in direct contact with the liquid Na, raising the risk of fire or even explosion. The main risk of mechanical abuse of the Na-S battery system is predicated on the damage to the individual batteries in the battery assembly. In addition, the efficient thermal isolation also acts as a thermal shield to resist the influence of external temperature. Although excessive heating from the outside usually does not affect the safety of the battery system, the failure of thermal management leading to thermal abuse may cause the cell overheating, which may eventually result in further rigorous exothermic reactions inside the battery. Lastly, the Na-S batteries do not have an internal protection mechanism to prevent overcharge and overdischarge, and the indication of reaching up to the charge and discharge limits emerges from the significant increase in the internal resistance of the battery. In the Na-S battery assembly, there is also a huge risk of transferring charging and discharging loads to adjacent cells, thereby amplifying the risk of overload and reaffirming the low safety of the stack.

Compared with Na-S batteries, in ZEBRA batteries, metal halides are generally less corrosive than sodium polysulfide. This lower sensitivity enables the selection of a wide range of materials for battery container and current collector. Unlike Na-S batteries, ZEBRA batteries, upon damage to the ceramic separator, experience reaction between Na and  $\mathrm{NaAlCl_4}$  to form NaCl and metallic Al. However, such phenomenon is not as violent as the corresponding reaction between Na and S in the Na-S systems owing to the low heat generation. Moreover, the products of the aforementioned reactions may facilitate filling the defective sites. In case of larger cracks in the separator, the resulting Al has the ability to form a direct conductive bridge between the cathode and anode, thereby keeping the battery circuit intact. Another advantage of ZEBRA batteries is their resistance to overcharge and overdischarge, rendering them as a potential electrochemical system in practical GSES applications.

In the IT or HT LMBs operating above  $400^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , thermal corrosion is dominant, which detrimentally influences their safety through mechanical abuse. Although LMBs do not require an extra separator between their electrodes owing to the unique natural layered structure, the external interference may cause mixing of different metal layers, thereby increasing their safety risk, which is why such systems are mainly viable for stationary energy storage. In comparison with other LMBs, SELL batteries avoid short-circuiting through separation of electrodes and operations at lower temperatures, making them a safer choice compared to LMBs. In addition, some SELL battery systems, such as SELL-brass/ZnCl $_2$ , are highly tolerant to overcharge and overdischarge. To recapitulate, among prospective candidates for LSES, the current and future research and

![](images/0a463c6bcadda80b007389a7733928b739034e242b15631e426d30c5f237bf8e.jpg)

![](images/5197aae5694c9141e13b697e9e59dbd5db1a8b1c1cc1c260c87740da52917259.jpg)

Figure 54. Structures, mechanisms, and redox potentials of rechargeable alkaline batteries. (a) Schematic of rechargeable alkaline batteries. (b) Standard potential (versus the standard hydrogen electrode) of different redox couples in alkaline solution. Reproduced with permission from ref 441. Copyright 2015 Elsevier. (c) Schematic of reaction mechanisms and structures of nickel hydroxides. Reproduced with permission from ref 433. Copyright 2020 Elsevier.

development pertaining to the MSBs should focus on improving their safety and operating stability and reducing costs.

# 6. ALKALINE BATTERIES

# 6.1. Introduction

With a history of more than 120 years of rechargeable alkaline batteries, $^{433}$ $\mathrm{Ni(OH)}_2 / \mathrm{NiOOH}$  is considered to be the key redox couple owing to its good reversibility, high redox potential, adequate theoretical capacity, abundance, low toxicity, and cost effectiveness. $^{434}$  In general,  $\mathrm{Ni(OH)}_2$  has two polymorphic forms,  $\alpha\text{-Ni}(\mathrm{OH})_2$  and  $\beta\text{-Ni}(\mathrm{OH})_2$  (Figure 54c), and the latter is widely used as cathode material, exhibiting higher thermodynamic stability in alkaline solution than its  $\alpha$  counterpart. $^{434}$  In contrast,  $\alpha\text{-Ni}(\mathrm{OH})_2$  yields higher theoretical energy density than  $\beta\text{-Ni}(\mathrm{OH})_2$ . During charging,  $\beta\text{-Ni}(\mathrm{OH})_2$  and  $\alpha\text{-Ni}(\mathrm{OH})_2$  are oxidized into  $\beta\text{-NiOOH}$  and  $\gamma\text{-NiOOH}$ , respectively.  $\beta\text{-NiOOH}$  is further subjected to irreversible oxidation to form an upper valence composite of  $\gamma\text{-NiOOH}$ , which undergoes volumetric change during overcharging. In addition,  $\alpha\text{-Ni}(\mathrm{OH})_2$  is dehydrated and recrystallized in the concentrated alkaline electrolyte to form  $\beta\text{-Ni}(\mathrm{OH})_2$  with aging. $^{435}$  Here, the emphasis has exclusively been placed on the redox couples and the corresponding redox potentials (Figure 54a,b) of different rechargeable alkaline battery chemistries, including nickel-cadmium (Ni-Cd), $^{436}$  nickel-metal hydride (Ni-MH), $^{437}$  nickel-iron (Ni-Fe), $^{438}$  nickel-hydrogen (Ni- $H_2$ ), $^{49,56,439}$  and nickel-zinc (Ni-Zn), $^{166,440}$  for GSES.

# 6.2. Ni-Cd Batteries

As one of the classic alkaline batteries, the Ni-Cd battery is composed of a  $\mathrm{Ni(OH)}_2$  cathode,  $\mathrm{Cd(OH)}_2$  anode, and KOH electrolyte, the assembly of which upon electrochemical cycling

yields a standard voltage of  $1.3\mathrm{V}$  through the following electrode reactions:

Cathode:

$$
\mathrm {N i O O H} + \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} + \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {N i} (\mathrm {O H}) _ {2} + \mathrm {O H} ^ {-}
$$

$$
E ^ {\circ} = 0. 4 9 \mathrm {V} \tag {14}
$$

Anode:

$$
\mathrm {C d} (\mathrm {O H}) _ {2} + 2 \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {C d} + 2 \mathrm {O H} ^ {-} \quad E ^ {\circ} = - 0. 8 1 \mathrm {V} \tag {15}
$$

Overall:

$$
2 \mathrm {N i O O H} + 2 \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} + \mathrm {C d} \leftrightarrow 2 \mathrm {N i} (\mathrm {O H}) _ {2} + \mathrm {C d} (\mathrm {O H}) _ {2}
$$

$$
E ^ {\circ} = 1. 3 0 \mathrm {V} \tag {16}
$$

As is evident, during battery charge, the  $\mathrm{Ni(OH)}_2$  cathode is converted to nickel oxyhydroxide through a simple deprotonation mechanism, while  $\mathrm{Cd(OH)}_2$  is reduced to metallic Cd at the negative electrode. Concerning their potential applications, Ni-Cd batteries once occupied a huge market share in portable electronics due to their practical low-temperature performance, constant discharge voltage, and long cycle life. However, the environmental and economic concerns accompanied by this technology have hindered its furtherance on industrial scale.[435,436] In other words, the increased production cost of toxic Cd and severe soil and water contamination render this storage system impractical for GSES applications.

# 6.3. Ni-MH Batteries

Originally introduced in the 1960s, the nontoxic Ni-MH battery surfaced as a replacement for the Ni-Cd system. Although the structural configuration of the Ni-MH battery

![](images/4431bd638bd61dc25f5129abd0efd16984b6624b6536cad5f6644aad3168670b.jpg)  
Current Collector

![](images/d307244de3cbdb798c5bac4e7cf36fe59bce1e533d928e2ac91813fc41144b4b.jpg)  
Current Collector

![](images/425f3d3544abc35fc10ae01bbc9ecb8fa83c2c3833093751c79f85ef259c40b8.jpg)  
Figure 55. Schematics showing the electrochemical reactions between water and MH during (a) charge and (b) discharge. Reproduced with permission from ref 443. Copyright 2013 Multidisciplinary Digital Publishing Institute.

![](images/09619819cfe96be21ac71ded3736c6b896cddcaac83dc9c94335d6cd21a5045b.jpg)  
Figure 56. Commercial Ni-MH batteries. (a) Structure of large-sized high-power Ni-MH batteries (GIGACELL). (b) Discharge curve of 30-K6 GIGACELL batteries (30-cell module with a 150 Ah rated capacity) at 293 K and various current rates. (c) Cycle performance of 3.5 Ah single cell at 298 K. Reproduced with permission from ref 445. Copyright 2013 Elsevier.

![](images/18f11050b81d9aa0a7d38f4e82ec3a661d5435fd5410c0f6e0bcf76733c5fa3b.jpg)

resembles that of the Ni–Cd battery, unlike Ni–Cd, it employs a hydrogen-absorbing alloy as anode material, resulting in higher energy density and no memory effect. $^{442}$  Electrons enter the MH anode to neutralize the protons during charge and exit the MH anode through the current collector during discharge (Figure 55). In general, the Ni–MH battery produces a standard voltage of  $1.3\mathrm{V}$  through the following electrochemical reactions:

Cathode:

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathrm {N i O O H} + \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} + \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {N i} (\mathrm {O H}) _ {2} + \mathrm {O H} ^ {-} \\ E ^ {\circ} = 0. 4 9 \mathrm {V} \tag {17} \\ \end{array}
$$

Anode:

$$
\mathrm {M} + \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} + \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {M H} + \mathrm {O H} ^ {-} \quad E ^ {\circ} = - 0. 8 1 \mathrm {V} \tag {18}
$$

![](images/676bb0e7133d810166327e21196d3e1be38371a787f68bc0758a32e96758663a.jpg)  
a

![](images/b9a095a84cfaed06cd8813cfe7e7393978a9ebe6299f3ba67f00d15dbf458697.jpg)

![](images/482c3e951a71e600e6b35a3f248fdcf66800fe49f29095835d34f25915dc09ea.jpg)  
b

![](images/97050641d245e31cc25801954f276f3732b6e507bee2b2cec6c651c6a2793e22.jpg)  
C  
d

Figure 57. Progress in Ni-Fe battery systems. (a) Schematic drawing of the Ni-Fe battery made from inorganic/carbon hybrid materials. (b) SEM image of  $\mathrm{Ni(OH)}_2 / \mathrm{MWNT}$  and  $\mathrm{FeO}_x / \mathrm{graphene}$  hybrid. Scale bar is  $200\mathrm{nm}$ . (c) Galvanostatic discharge curves of the Ni-Fe battery at various current densities. (d) Ragone plot of the Ni-Fe battery measured in part c. (e) Capacity retention vs. cycle number for the ultra-Ni-Fe battery at a galvanostatic charge and discharge current density of  $37\mathrm{A g}^{-1}$ . Reproduced with permission from ref 446. Inset of panel h shows the charging and discharging curves before and after 2000 cycles. Reproduced with permission from ref 29. Copyright 2020 American Chemical Society.

Overall:

$$
\mathrm {N i O O H} + \mathrm {M H} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {N i} (\mathrm {O H}) _ {2} + \mathrm {M} \quad E ^ {\circ} = 1. 3 0 \mathrm {V} \tag {19}
$$

Among numerous studies on MH alloy based electrodes, the rare earth-based  $\mathrm{AB}_5$  alloy, first reported by Van Vucht et al. (1970),[44] is the most common anode material used in Ni-MH batteries. In addition,  $\mathrm{A}_2\mathrm{B}$ -type, superlattice  $\mathrm{A}_2\mathrm{B}_7$ -type, Ti-Ni-based, and  $\mathrm{Mg}-\mathrm{Ni}$ -based alloys have also been investigated for Ni-MH battery applications.[43] As much as this battery chemistry has been a preferred choice for powering portable

electronics, the limited hydrogen storage capacity of MH alloys has restricted the scope of its further applications, later leading to the dominance of energy market by currently known LIBs.

Nevertheless, the market for Ni-MH batteries has experienced a shift from consumer portable devices to hybrid electric vehicle and stationary applications due to their environmental friendliness, unprecedented safety, and wide operating temperature range. For example, the large-sized high-power Ni-MH battery named GIGACELL, manufactured by Kawasaki Heavy Industries, is composed of multiple individual cells with bipolar structure (Figure 56a), which can accomplish high operational

![](images/8dc210bdece5588a7ef894bf6e991c3cd739e7d07b86d7fa10d52c0ba2f8044c.jpg)  
a

![](images/190a2611cdb66b02e5bec657e2120eb46a0c28f58dfb2c8bfb27ae66163d4fe0.jpg)

![](images/a9ce97e60485fed44f22a1c174b16920bcdb713be25761cb89ddf0c14e42d9c6.jpg)

![](images/7985bc3848473f388948472089ea99d6b01e03e1aef467855ed7aa95ad58c6af.jpg)  
NiAlCo LDH/CNT

![](images/f5da6e07e225825f84e9ea6761559e3a09ee17dc8220c2e07858458a7d8a68b7.jpg)

![](images/440d15d6630ec9ebe9cf40563281434bd74ac121eb6ddd934514be8502a91f44.jpg)
