Figure 58. Ni-Zn batteries. (a) Schematic of the effect of recharging Ni-Zn (conventional powder zinc anodes) vs. Ni-3D Zn batteries. SEM images of (b) precycled and (c) postcycled Zn sponges after more than 100 cycles. Reproduced with permission from ref 451. Copyright 2017 The American Association for the Advancement of Science. (d) TEM image of NiAlCo LDH/CNT hybrid. (e) SEM image of NNA@CNH nanostructure. (f) SEM image of the Ni-NiO nanosheets. (g) SEM images of porous NiO nanoflakes grown on CFs. (h) SEM image of NiCo-DH micronano sheets. (i) Capacity retention vs. cycle number of the NiAlCo LDH/CNT hybrids at a charge/discharge current density of  $66.7\mathrm{A}\mathrm{g}^{-1}$ . Panels d and I reproduced with permission from ref 440. Copyright 2014 Royal Society of Chemistry. Panel e reproduced with permission from ref 452. Copyright 2016 Elsevier. Panel f reproduced with permission from ref 453. Copyright 2017 Wiley-VCH. Panel g reproduced with permission from ref 454. Copyright 2016 Wiley-VCH. Panel h reprinted permission from ref 456. Copyright 2019 Wiley-VCH.

voltage. In addition, the compact structure and auxiliary equipment of Ni-MH GIGACELL yield superior capacity and minimize energy loss. Figure 56b illustrates the charge/ discharge curves of a 30-cell module with a  $150\mathrm{Ah}$  rating.[445] It is evident that a discharge voltage of  $\sim 38\mathrm{V}$  and discharge capacity up to  $95\%$  can be obtained at  $1\mathrm{C}$  rate (150 A). Even at higher currents up to  $5\mathrm{C}$  rate (750 A), discharge voltage of  $\sim 35\mathrm{V}$  and discharge capacity of  $\sim 90\%$  are maintained. Figure 56c shows no capacity decay after 11000 cycles with CE of approximate  $98\%$ . From the perspective of commercial utility of this technology, Nishime Wind Farm in Japan manufactured a massive  $102\mathrm{kWh}$  energy storage facility in 2007 by connecting 48 modules of GIGACELL batteries in series, wherein each module encloses  $10\mathrm{Ni}-\mathrm{MH}$  cells of  $177\mathrm{Ah}$  each for smoothly handling the wind

power output. Regardless, the capacity fade upon electrochemical cycling owing to alloy pulverization and corrosion and undesired high self-discharge due to active material decomposition remain the predominant challenges in Ni-MH battery systems and require effective mitigation. Additionally, the limited reserves of rare earth metals, whose alloys are utilized in Ni-MH batteries, demand the development of more advanced and efficient extraction and recycling technologies.

# 6.4. Ni-Fe Batteries

Principally comprising a  $\mathrm{Ni(OH)}_2$  cathode and an Fe anode, the Ni-Fe battery has been moderately developed over the last century since its advent in 1899-1902.[446] Nevertheless, the performance of commercial Ni-Fe batteries is generally below par as a consequence of low energy density and EE ( $\sim 60\%$ ) and

![](images/bcebc36cdf5249b9bce75a2eecdef1726a596c9d23c4206d096d1dfbb5134a47.jpg)  
Figure 59. (a) SEM images of the NiS-coated  $\mathrm{Ni}_{0.95}\mathrm{Zn}_{0.05}(\mathrm{OH})_2$  microspheres. (b) Cycling performance in comparison with the commercial Ni-Zn battery. Reproduced with permission from ref 458. Copyright 2020 Royal Society of Chemistry.

![](images/f1c4fe71a1ddd3a8c0faa4b0e3f157699ca7174bc64cd3bb0f9cddc874d2aba0.jpg)

high self-discharge. In this regard, factors such as large particle size of active Fe electrode, its poor conductivity, weak coupling with current collector, and severe passivation during cell discharge are blamed. In recent years, research investigations on Ni-Fe battery increased due to its long cycle life, inherent safety, and crustally abundant sources of Ni and Fe, considerations that are highly desirable for LSES. For instance, in 2012, Dai et al. $^{446}$  designed a Ni-Fe battery with ultrafast rate performance to deliver specific energy of  $120\mathrm{Whkg^{-1}}$  and specific power of  $15\mathrm{kWkg^{-1}}$ . This cell (Figure 57a,b) is structurally composed of  $\mathrm{Ni(OH)_2}$  nanoplates grown on oxidized multiwall CNTs as cathode and  $\mathrm{FeO}_x$  nanoparticles grown on RGO sheets as anode. As shown in Figure 57c, complete electrochemical discharge at various rates can be achieved from  $\sim 5$  min to  $\sim 9$  s, whereas full charging is completed in as little as  $\sim 2$  min, which is significantly faster than the previously reported conventional Ni-Fe battery designs. In addition, the ultrafast Ni-Fe cell shows excellent power density up to  $36\mathrm{kWkg^{-1}}$  with a potential downside in terms of insufficient cycling stability with  $\sim 20\%$  capacity decay over 800 cycles (Figure 57d,e). The outstanding power density of the cell is ascribed to the reasonable conductivity of the graphitic carbon materials and strong coupling between inorganic nanocrystals and oxidized nanocarbon. The major challenge of insufficient cycling of ultrafast Ni-Fe cells has been addressed with the emergence of carbon-based coatings, $^{29,447,448}$  which not only provide the Fe anode with rapid interfacial electron transfer but also hamper the degradation of the Fe nanoparticles, thereby enhancing energy and power densities, as well as cycling stability of Fe anode. To demonstrate, an efficient and scalable method for synthesizing conformal carbon-coated Fe nanoparticles for Ni-Fe batteries was reported recently. $^{29}$  A protective Fe oxidation-free  $\mathrm{Na_2CO_3}$  coating is formed on Fe nanoparticles in situ using an aerosol spray method (Figure 57f).  $\mathrm{Na_2CO_3}$ -stabilized Fe nanoparticles are then subjected to pyrolysis by mixing them with sucrose (acting as carbon source) to deposit a uniform carbon coating (Figure 57g). The electrochemical cycling of the cell based on carbon-coated Fe nanoparticles confirmed  $87\%$  capacity retention even after 2000 cycles (Figure 57h). Regardless, the low EE of Ni-Fe battery still restricts its utility for LSES, and its self-discharging mechanisms have not yet been clearly studied, therefore requiring further investigations.

# 6.5. Ni-Zn Batteries

Zinc has earned the reputation as a prospective anode material for alkaline batteries due to the low redox potential of  $\mathrm{Zn} / \mathrm{Zn}^{2+}$ , high specific capacitance (820 Ah kg $^{-1}$ ), and natural

abundance. $^{449,450}$  That is why Ni-Zn batteries have become widely known for their low manufacturing cost, high energy and power densities, high working potential, and wide operating temperature range. $^{440}$  However, the cycle life of Ni-Zn batteries is shortened by the formation Zn dendrites during repeated charge-discharge cycling and the severe capacitance deterioration of the  $\mathrm{Ni(OH)}_2$  cathode. To address such issues, numerous efforts have been devoted to designing different nanostructured electrode materials of diverse morphologies and compositions. In 2017, one such important attempt was made by Parker et al., who developed a high-energy Ni-Zn battery based on a 3D Zn sponge anode (Figure 58a). $^{451}$  It has been revealed that monolithic zinc sponge anodes can be electrochemically cycled from hundreds to thousands of times without undergoing typical passivation or macroscale dendrite formation (Figure 58b,c). This work has alleviated the life issues of zinc anodes to a certain extent. In addition, a Ni-Zn battery with a high-capacity hybrid cathode of NiAlCo-layered double hydroxide (LDH) coupled with CNTs (NiAlCo-LDH/CNT) has been investigated by Dai et al. (Figure 58d). $^{440}$  Substantial evidence has been provided for supporting the efficacy of Al and Co co-doping for stability enhancement of the LDH phase and  $\alpha$ -Ni(OH) $_2$  with capacity loss of merely  $6\%$  over 2000 cycles at  $66.7\mathrm{A g}^{-1}$  (Figure 58i). Hitherto, several Ni-based nanostructures including Co-doped  $\mathrm{Ni(OH)}_2$  on nanowire arrays (NNA@CNH), $^{452}$  Ni-NiO heterostructured nanosheets, $^{453}$  NiO nanoflakes on hierarchical CFs, $^{454}$  nickel@nickel oxide core-shell electrodes, $^{455}$  and nickel-cobalt double hydroxides (NiCo-DHs) $^{456}$  have been studied as cathode materials for Ni-Zn batteries (Figure 58e-h). The electrochemical characterization results of the aforementioned materials in these studies have aimed to develop ultrafast, high-capacity, and safe Ni-Zn battery systems. Taking into account the practicality, the areal and volumetric capacities have been given relatively more imperative than specific capacity evaluated by mass loading, which explains why there is dire need for the development of novel porous nanomaterials with high areal capacity in Ni-Zn chemistries for their LSES utility. $^{457}$

Recently, Chao et al. [458] proposed the utilization of a dense Co-free microscale cathode for developing a commercially viable  $\mathrm{Ni - Zn}$  battery with excellent electrochemical performance. The SEM micrographs (Figure 59a) demonstrate  $\mathrm{Ni}_{0.95}\mathrm{Zn}_{0.05}(\mathrm{OH})_2$  microspheres coated with a NiS film with equiaxial nanodots of  $\sim 7.0\mathrm{nm}$  in size, which offer high conductivity, reasonable surface area, and rapid electrode kinetics, enabling the  $\mathrm{Ni - Zn}$  battery to yield high gravimetric and volumetric energy densities. When subjected to charge-discharge cycling, the commercial-scale 3.5 Ah  $\mathrm{Ni - Zn}$  pouch cell based on the

![](images/08d0082420f657f6ee2ed964aab054f03f5dac87083c4645a83f906e3f7b9cfb.jpg)  
b

![](images/2f7a1921ec27daf67f934c380bad11586dd0f5e3e2b18693bbdea5f5680b77ad.jpg)  
Figure 60. (a) Schematic structure of RFB. (b) Charge and discharge mechanisms of a typical RFB.

aforementioned cathode showed better cycling performance in terms of  $89.4\%$  capacity retention after 420 cycles compared with  $8.2\%$  computed for its commercial counterpart (Figure 59b). Such findings have massive potential to become cornerstones for the development of high energy and power density aqueous cathode materials. However, due to the lethargic research on addressing zinc anode issues, there is still a long way to go for Ni-Zn chemistry to achieve long cycle and calendar life.

# 6.6. Summary and Future Perspectives for Alkaline Batteries

From the perspective of LSES, this section entails succinct yet comprehensive history and current status of the rechargeable alkaline batteries. Although attempts have been made to scale up Ni-MH batteries for assembling large-sized high power battery system, the cost of metal hydrides is too high for widespread applications. The research on Ni-Fe and Ni-Zn batteries has recently gained momentum through investigations focusing on compositional and structural modification of electrode materials. Despite notable progress, the EE of the Ni-Fe battery is still insufficient for LSES. Novel Ni-based nanostructures have been proposed for achieving high rate, supreme capacity, and safety features in Ni-Zn cells. In contrast, the low volumetric energy density and short calendar life due to zinc dendrites render Ni-Zn battery technology cost ineffective and hence challenging for LSES applications.

# 7. REDOX-FLOW BATTERIES

Since their invention in 1974 by Thaller et al.,[459] RFBs have been regarded as one of the most practical alternatives for medium and large scale energy storage applications.[42,63,350] Unlike aforementioned MIBs that store energy in their electrodes, RFBs have a unique architecture that is capable of storing energy in storage tanks filled with electrolytes, as shown in Figure 60a. Typically, positive and negative redox species are dissolved in supporting electrolytes to form catholyte and anolyte, respectively, which are separated by a functional membrane.[460-462] During electrochemical cycling, the ion-conducting anolyte and catholyte are pumped from the storage tanks to flow through the porous electrodes where the active species undergo their respective redox reactions (Figure 60b).[463] It is fundamental to mention that the separating membrane between the anolyte and the catholyte is of vital importance for the EE and cycle life of the RFBs. It allows transport of charge carriers to maintain the overall electroneutrality of the RFB system while preventing the cross contamination of active ions all the time.

The redox reactions of RFBs take place on the surface of electrodes without compromising the integrity of their internal structures, rendering them suitable for long cyclic life of more than 10000 charge-discharge cycles (10-20 years). The power rating (kW) of RFBs is a function of the electrode size and the number of cells in a stack. Their energy storage capacity (kWh) is dependent on the concentration of active species and volume of the electrolyte. The independent power and capacity architectures of RFBs enable their excellent design flexibility for GSES applications. Furthermore, a reliable separation between catholyte and anolyte along with simplicity in cell stack structure imparts inherent safety to RFBs, a fundamental consideration for LSES purposes. Moreover, the molten electrolytes and their intimate interfaces with porous electrodes promise rapid redox kinetics. Therefore, RFBs earn distinctive advantages for transitioning energy storage technology toward sustainable power generation and transmission.

Based on the type of redox species and supporting electrolytes (catholyte and anolyte), RFBs have been categorized into inorganic aqueous RFBs (ARFBs), organic ARFBs, nonaqueous RFBs, and so on. [461,464] However, most of the research on RFBs is devoted to ARFBs, which are known for their indispensable features, including high ionic conductivity, intrinsic safety, and facile manufacturing. The early research studies on ARFBs have emphasized the development of inexpensive transition metal and halogen based redox species with high solubility and stability in supporting electrolyte, high voltage difference, and rapid electrochemical kinetics. [465] Having made substantial progress over the last four decades, ARFBs have earned their reputation in demonstrating LSES based on numerous redox couples, including but not limited to iron-chromium RFBs (ICBs), [466] VRFBs, [463] zinc-bromide RFBs (ZBBs), [467] and zinc-iron RFBs (ZFBs). [468] For instance, a 200 MW/800 MWh VRFB-based energy storage and peak shaving power station has been under construction in Dalian (China) since 2016, validating the practicality of RFB technology for LSES. However, unlike the great improvement in the energy density of LIBs since the 1990s, [63] the energy density of major RFB systems has remained almost the same for more than three decades. In particular, the utility of conventional metal- or inorganic-based ARFBs (such as VRFBs) is predominantly limited by their low energy density ( $\sim$ 25 Wh kg $^{-1}$ ) and high investment cost. To address these challenges, recent investigations have focused on improving the energy density and reducing the cost through the development of novel and inexpensive RFBs. Such systems utilize advanced redox couples and low-cost membranes, yielding high operating voltage. In this regard, some comprehensive topical reviews have summarized

![](images/feaccecd24fc003937054175f0a24e82d18b3da60c5bd156f033c08436dcdb92.jpg)  
Figure 61. Selected examples of inorganic redox species with different redox potentials. The colors of the cells correspond to the pH value of the electrolytes: red, acidic; blue, basic. Redesigned with permission from ref 465. Copyright 2015 Wiley-VCH.

the recent advances and performance metrics of conventional RFBs. $^{463,465,469-471}$  Herein, we delineate the representative work on electrodes, membranes, and organic redox couples for novel flow battery chemistries from the perspective of LSES applications. The gap between academia and industrialization of these battery chemistries will also be discussed in detail pointing toward the direction for the next-generation practical RFBs.

# 7.1. Conventional RFBs

A wide range of metal and halogen-based redox species can be coupled to construct a RFB. $^{465}$  Figure 61 summarizes the various redox species along with their corresponding redox potentials. It is fundamental to mention that the combination of two redox couples with low cost, high operating voltage, and ample solubility is very difficult. This explains why only a handful of RFB chemistries have been considered for LSES applications given their research history spanning over the last four decades.

7.1.1. Iron-Chromium RFBs. As a consequence of the oil crisis in the 1970s, NASA's Inventions and Contribution Board put forward the unprecedented RFB chemistry comprising of two redox couples, ferric/ferrous  $(\mathrm{Fe}^{2+}/\mathrm{Fe}^{3+})$  and chromic/ chromous  $(\mathrm{Cr}^{2+}/\mathrm{Cr}^{3+})$ , to be used as positive and negative active species, respectively, in halide solution.[459] The aforementioned RFB chemistry undergoes the following redox reactions while operating at standard voltage of  $1.18\mathrm{V}$ :

Cathode:

$$
\mathrm {F e} ^ {3 +} + \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {F e} ^ {2 +} \quad E ^ {\circ} = 0. 7 7 \mathrm {V} \tag {20}
$$

Anode:

$$
\mathrm {C r} ^ {3 +} + \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {C r} ^ {2 +} \quad E ^ {\circ} = - 0. 4 1 \mathrm {V} \tag {21}
$$

Overall:

$$
\mathrm {F e} ^ {3 +} + \mathrm {C r} ^ {2 +} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {F e} ^ {2 +} + \mathrm {C r} ^ {3 +} \quad E ^ {\circ} = 1. 1 8 \mathrm {V} \tag {22}
$$

Keeping in consideration the material costs of Fe and Cr metals, this RFB technology qualifies for inexpensive and efficient LSES. For instance, a  $250\mathrm{kW} / 1$  MWh storage facility has been set up by the Inventions and Contribution Board in California. This type of RFB delivers high EE of  $78.4\%$  at a current density of  $120\mathrm{mA}\mathrm{cm}^{-2}$ . On the other hand, the battery exhibits an undesirable capacity decay rate of  $1.2\%$  per cycle due to high crossover rate and rapid kinetics of side reactions during cycling. Also, the sluggish  $\mathrm{Cr^{3+} / Cr^{2+}}$  redox kinetics along with slow HER account for major shortcomings pertaining to the ICB technology. This is why the progress on this RFB technology has stagnated.

7.1.2. Zinc-Bromine RFBs. The ZBB operates on the principle of zinc metal dissolution/plating at the anode and  $\mathrm{Br}^{-}/\mathrm{Br}_{2}$  conversion at the positive electrode.[467] Following are the redox reactions governing the operation of a typical ZBB:

Cathode:

$$
\mathrm {B r} _ {2} + 2 \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \leftrightarrow 2 \mathrm {B r} ^ {-} \quad E ^ {\circ} = 1. 0 6 \mathrm {V} \tag {23}
$$

Anode:

$$
Z n ^ {2 +} + 2 e ^ {-} \leftrightarrow Z n \quad E ^ {\circ} = - 0. 7 6 V \tag {24}
$$

Overall:

$$
\mathrm {Z n} + \mathrm {B r} _ {2} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {Z n} ^ {2 +} + 2 \mathrm {B r} ^ {-} \quad E ^ {\circ} = 1. 8 2 \mathrm {V} \tag {25}
$$

In addition to the low-cost of Zn, thanks to its abundance, another potential upside of the ZBB is its higher energy density than ICB. The credit is given to the low electrochemical potential of the zinc anode. The developmental research on ZBBs has enabled scaling-up from kilowatt to megawatt scale for large scale storage demonstration. In 2015, for instance, a principal electricity generation company, Samruk Energy, based in the Republic of Kazakhstan purchased a ZBB system with 25 MW/100 MWh capacity from Primus systems. Despite all the advancements, the common challenge of dendrite formation on the Zn anode still remains, leading to electrical short circuit and hence questioning the inherent safety of this ZBB technology.[472,473] Furthermore, health and environmental concerns arising from the corrosive and hazardous nature of bromine gas have been a major hindrance in the furtherance of this technology for LSES purposes,[474,475] which recently have been minimized using methods such as bromine complexing agents.[476,477]

Following the invention of ZBBs, Lockheed Missiles and Space Company pioneered the concept of an early ZFB chemistry,[472] which employs ferro-ferricyanide as the cathode active material. The charge and discharge of this ZFB cell are governed by the following redox reactions:

Cathode:

$$
\mathrm {F e} (\mathrm {C N}) _ {6} ^ {3 -} + \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {F e} (\mathrm {C N}) _ {6} ^ {4 -} \quad E ^ {\circ} = 0. 3 6 \mathrm {V} \tag {26}
$$

Anode:

$$
\mathrm {Z n} (\mathrm {O H}) _ {4} ^ {2 -} + 2 \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {Z n} + 4 \mathrm {O H} ^ {-} \quad E ^ {\circ} = - 1. 2 2 \mathrm {V} \tag {27}
$$

![](images/e7e4e62d8bb1fdf060995a32a48b6625ba437e96a610bfd201081139533b0bce.jpg)

![](images/9890eb0c572598900ced5df6c3cf185f6f537e111dcf9b07fe90f757ab769fc6.jpg)

![](images/93f6ade1b49905121afb95248d522fcea5fe7ba00e9695ab7d124c24137ae65a.jpg)

![](images/1f453f3853baf9c0ad0d8d2a853f9b28aa0d116a50812676e71ab7e4f16059f9.jpg)

![](images/1fc739be8909e4dae35fa7d72e6d7891845636bfa625e476a6a82e8a323bb765.jpg)

![](images/a440c441a644cca4c1d3a62549239a85d3be07d6df30b2db7a9aca1c33b24807.jpg)

![](images/292a18eb9d29d2fcaac5693436b9f19f905086aeaee02c98e989294922ee7571.jpg)  
Figure 62. Strategies to increase the catalytic activity of electrodes in VRFBs. (a) Pristine CF. (b) Creating pores on the electrode fibers via surface catalytic etching methods. Reproduced with permission from ref 483. Copyright 2019 Elsevier. (c) Introducing functional groups onto the surface of electrode by thermal treatment. (d) Decorating metal-based electrocatalyst. Reproduced with permission from ref 482. Copyright 2019 Elsevier. (e) Decorating carbon-based nanostructured electrocatalyst: CNF/CNT composite catalyst on CF surface. Reproduced with permission from ref 487. Copyright 2015 Wiley-VCH. (f) Catalyst based on the graphene nanowall. Reproduced with permission from ref 488. Copyright 2013 American Chemical Society. (g) Electrochemical performance of VRFBs with untreated, oxidized, and decorated CNF, CNT, and CF. Reproduced with permission from ref 487. Copyright 2015 Wiley-VCH.

![](images/06752193cc075d7fc7fd19c130401be7a95e1910407a88de798ac68daf5c59a2.jpg)

![](images/906b91f6eebbd421e5eb88c1d53778fa077b6f143a7fcc10fc86d5fc01594c6f.jpg)

Overall:

$$
\begin{array}{l} 2 \mathrm {F e} (\mathrm {C N}) _ {6} ^ {3 -} + \mathrm {Z n} + 4 \mathrm {O H} ^ {-} \leftrightarrow 2 \mathrm {F e} (\mathrm {C N}) _ {6} ^ {4 -} \\ + \mathrm {Z n} (\mathrm {O H}) _ {4} ^ {2 -} \quad E ^ {\circ} = 1. 5 8 \mathrm {V} \tag {28} \\ \end{array}
$$

Both the  $\mathrm{Zn(OH)}_4^{2-}/\mathrm{Zn}$  couple and the nontoxic and cost-effective  $\mathrm{Fe(CN)}_6^{3-}/\mathrm{Fe(CN)}_6^{4-}$  couple in alkaline solution are able to lower the ZIB cost to  $\$120\ \mathrm{kWh}^{-1}$ . However, the low ionic conductivity of the Nafion membranes and the low solubility of ferro-ferricyanide in alkaline solutions are responsible for the low power and energy densities of the battery.[473] Nevertheless, the technology has been currently commercialized by ViZn Energy Systems Inc. for grid-scale applications. In this regard, Hecate Energy with the support of ViZn Energy Inc. has set up a  $2\ \mathrm{MW}/6\ \mathrm{MWh}$  ZFB system for the provision of ancillary services to the Ontario grid in Canada. However, limited information on the battery chemistry and performance has been disclosed. Recently, a novel ZIB[468] based on a Zn redox pair in base and an Fe redox pair in acid has emerged as a promising candidate for achieving phenomenal

electrochemical performance along with minimal manufacturing cost. $^{478}$

7.1.3. All Vanadium RFBs. Originally pioneered by Skyllas-Kazacos and co-workers during the 1980s at University of New South Wales, the VRFB operates with  $\mathrm{V}^{2+}/\mathrm{V}^{3+}$  and  $\mathrm{VO}^{2+}/\mathrm{VO}^{2+}$  as redox couples, eliminating the cross-contamination between negative and positive compartments.[479,480] Upon mixing catholyte and anolyte, the initial oxidation states of vanadium can be recovered. In general, the VRFBs offer advantages of excellent electrochemical kinetics and negligible side reactions. Since the emergence of research on VRFBs in 1990s, their potential for achieving storage capacity up to multi-MW/MWh scale has been validated in the laboratory and on commercial scales. In 2015, Hokkaido Electric Power Company and Sumitomo Electric Industries Ltd. collaborated jointly to complete a whopping 15 MW/60 MWh VRFB storage system at Minami Hayakita Transformer Station in Abira-chou, Hokkaido (Japan), which is one of the world's largest RFB facilities in operation to date. It clearly shows that having been partially commercialized, VRFBs occupy most of the share in the

![](images/3022d6bd3b5766233e737f004939fc2201528793d4c8b72f4d7ad3c378df119c.jpg)  
a

![](images/8905e94a4086b197c24a8843cc358d2b7689cafc4537dff35d1dcffa69b2308a.jpg)  
b

![](images/f420a6431337a186264e4104b9d9f6765d8bcf75ba25d84730be4075a46c79e1.jpg)  
C

![](images/f1d16603cbfe8c169fa978e8d77fdd3535ba85b46687170798946033b91bf018.jpg)  
d  
Figure 63. Nafion membrane in VRFBs. (a) Proposed structure of Nafion. (b) Water channel model for the morphology of Nafion. Reproduced with permission from ref 493. Copyright 2007 Springer Nature. (c) Schematic illustration of Nafion/inorganic hybrid membranes. (d) Schematic illustration of Nafion/organic composite multilayer membranes. Reproduced with permission from ref 491. Copyright 2011 Royal Society of Chemistry.

RFB market and are still emerging as one of the most mature flow battery systems. For a typical VRFB, the standard potential at ion concentrations of  $1\mathrm{mol}\mathrm{L}^{-1}$  is  $1.26\mathrm{V}$ , while the energy conversion takes place by transitions in valence states of vanadium through the following electrode reactions:

Cathode:

$$
\mathrm {V O} _ {2} ^ {+} + 2 \mathrm {H} ^ {+} + \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {V O} ^ {2 +} + \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} \quad E ^ {\circ} = 1. 0 1 \mathrm {V} \tag {29}
$$

Anode:

$$
\mathrm {V} ^ {3 +} + \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {V} ^ {2 +} \quad E ^ {\circ} = - 0. 2 5 \mathrm {V} \tag {30}
$$

Overall:

$$
\mathrm {V O} _ {2} ^ {+} + \mathrm {V} ^ {2 +} + 2 \mathrm {H} ^ {+} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {V O} ^ {2 +} + \mathrm {V} ^ {3 +} + \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O}
$$

$$
E ^ {\circ} = 1. 2 6 \mathrm {V} \tag {31}
$$

It is worth mentioning that the VFRBs are able to operate with EE up to  $80.3\%$  at a current density of  $120\mathrm{mAcm}^{-2}$ . In addition, the capacity decay rate of the cell is as low as  $0.3\%$  per cycle, which is substantially lower than that computed for ICBs.[470] Nonetheless, the market penetration of the VRFBs for LSES is inhibited by their low energy density and the high cost of vanadium  $(\sim \$  27\mathrm{kg}^{-1})\) and Nafion membranes  $(\sim \\( 500\mathrm{m}^{-2})$ .[481] Currently, significant efforts have been devoted to the development of novel electrodes, membranes, and redox electrolytes to achieve electrochemical performance comparable to that of LIBs.

# 7.2. Recent Developments in RFBs

7.2.1. Electrodes. Suitable selection of electrode plays a crucial role in determining the current density and the long-term stability of RFBs as it not only offers the active areas for redox reactions but also affects the ion and mass transport. $^{471}$  The high

current density and operationally feasible VRFBs enable a compact cell stack, which leads to a lower cell stack cost. Therefore, the stringent and critical requirements of an excellent electrode for high power density and long life RFBs include high surface area, suitable porosity, good wettability, high electrical conductivity, excellent reactivity toward the redox species, and high chemical and electrochemical stability. Among electrode materials, carbon based electrodes are the most favored and hence widely studied for VRFBs due to their wide operating potential range, reasonable stability, excellent electrical conductivity, high corrosion resistance, and relatively low cost as compared to other alternatives studied to date.[482,483] In contrast to the aforementioned benefits, the carbon felt (CF)-based electrodes exhibit inferior electrochemical performance, primarily owing to their sluggish redox kinetics and low specific area, resulting in insufficient number of active sites for rapid redox reactions.[484] Various solutions have been proposed and investigated to promote slow redox kinetics through enhancing the surface area of the electrodes. In this regard, the remedial pretreatment solutions are generally divided into three categories: (1) employing surface etching methods for creating abundant secondary pores on the surface of carbon fibers for providing additional active sites for the electrochemical reactions;[485] (2) introducing functional groups onto the surface of the electrode for improving its electrocatalytic activity, leading to enhanced battery performance;[486] and (3) anchoring electrocatalysts on the surface of carbon fibers.[487,488]

Among surface treatment methods, thermal treatment, nitrogen treatment, and acid treatment are commonly known. The surface morphologies of different carbon fibers in SEM images (Figure 62a-f) demonstrate their efficacy in augmenting the electrocatalytic performance of electrodes in VRFBs. In this regard, a similar method has been recently proposed by the

![](images/71b055af4a7e0cc7398217f6023701697e5b9c1f889afefb96ef59f9862a2474.jpg)  
a

![](images/d0e2ce2837ac1dd41457dc98c33a9669c9853fa81fb124bd96df00e8b5fadb8c.jpg)

![](images/be3388719eee33eeb69e7b31d37434571b39bf78624d511a6a6dc83b1de98f88.jpg)

![](images/8ed45c7a74da81e308172e7db4d3a3da96b6b28d2f3b7b4ce114b308e4ef051c.jpg)

![](images/e94feb1a7d2cb75f45a858690abe36a9353401d9038f2f26e8e5d13874448a59.jpg)

(b) Surface and cross-section morphology of membranes (left) and TEM image of ZSM-35 zeolite (right). introduction of a CNF/CNT composite catalyst on the CF surface for achieving high performance in VRFBs.488 Such a composite catalyst accelerates the electron and mass transfer kinetics, resulting in increased VRFB efficiency at a high rate along with low overpotential for vanadium redox reaction (Figure 62e,g). It has been further shown that the as-prepared CNF/CNT electrode in VRFBs exhibits the lowest charge transfer resistance for both  $\mathrm{V}^{2 + } / \mathrm{V}^{3 + }$  and  $\mathrm{VO^{2 + } / VO_{2}}$  redox reactions and, therefore, demonstrates increased capacity retention and rate capability (up to  $100\mathrm{mAcm}^{-2}$ ). Such characteristics are therefore conducive to improving electron

transfer and lowering polarization. However, a major issue associated with decorating the carbon fiber surface is that such nanomaterials are prone to being dislodged by the flowing electrolyte, deteriorating the cycling stability of the electrodes.

In addition, tuning the electrode structures plays an important role in enhancing the redox kinetics and surface area of the electrodes.[489] The insufficient mass transport into the dense electrode structure during electrochemical cycling decreases the local concentration of redox species, which further causes substantial concentration loss. In other words, only the active surface next to the electrolyte is exposed to abundant redox

species, leading to the death of surface area (sites), therefore leading to higher polarization as well as undesired pumping burden. To address this issue, Zhao et al. $^{490}$  has proposed a novel electrode structure with a porosity gradient for ensuring the enhanced mass transport without sacrificing the active surface area. In other words, the active reaction surface area

gradually increased from the flow field to the membrane side owing to the increased porosity. Upon electrochemical cycling at a current density of  $240\mathrm{mAcm}^{-2}$ , the cell based on the aforesaid electrode delivers a higher discharge capacity by a whopping margin of  $69\%$  as compared to the conventional electrode

design, hence proving the efficacy of the porosity gradient based electrode design.

7.2.2. Membranes. Ideally, a membrane ought to exhibit high ionic conductivity, good ionic selectivity, excellent chemical and thermal stability, and inexpensive manufacturability. With their established classification into ion-exchange membranes (IEMs) and nonionic porous membranes, the former are generally made of cross-linked linear polymer chains in a 3D network along with attached functional groups, enabling the exchange of ions in or across the membrane.[491] Based on the polymer matrix, IEMs are further categorized into perfluorinated and nonfluorinated membranes. With regards to the former type of IEMs, Nafion has earned its reputation as an effective and conveniently available membrane owing to its numerous advantages in terms of high water swelling ratio, commendable proton conductivity, desired mechanical strength, and excellent thermal and chemical stability in RFBs.[492] The structure of currently employed Nafion membranes includes a flexible perfluorinated backbone and a side chain with perfluorovinyl ether groups, which are terminated with sulfonate groups (Figure 63a). The hydrophobic backbone endows the membranes with excellent mechanical and chemical stability thanks to the strength of the C-F single bond, while the hydrophilic sulfonated groups are responsible for providing rapid proton transport access. In order to determine the role of Nafion morphology in ion transfer enhancement, a well-known water channel model has been proposed (Figure 63b).[493] According to this, the sulfonic acid functional groups self-organize into hydrophilic water channels up to  $2.5\mathrm{nm}$  in diameter, thereby allowing a large number of vanadium ions to pass through the membrane and resulting in relatively low ion selectivity. Curbing the formation of hydrophilic clusters by using inorganic or organic fillers, such as  $\mathrm{SiO}_2$ ,  $\mathrm{TiO}_2$ , poly(ether ether ketone) (SPEEK), polytetrafluoroethylene (PTFE), or a thin ion selective membrane, is an effective way to solve the low ion selectivity conundrum (Figure 63c,d).[492,494,495] For example, Sun et al.[496] has proposed a hybrid membrane via a solution casting method through the dispersion of superhydrophilic  $\mathrm{TiO}_2$  nanotubes in the Nafion matrix. The incorporation of  $\mathrm{TiO}_2$  nanotubes is conducive to hindering the permeation of ions and hence lowering the capacity loss of the cell. Upon assembly of Nafion/ $\mathrm{TiO}_2$  in VRFB, it exhibits high capacity retention of  $55.7\%$  after 1400 cycles (over  $518\mathrm{h}$ ). This is in contrast to the rapid capacity drop to  $20\%$  in just 410 cycles (about  $89\mathrm{h}$ ) for the cell with Nafion 212.

However, the cost of the commercial DuPont Nafion membrane is approximately  $500 -$ 800 per square meter, which makes up ~40% of the total manufacturing cost of a VRFB stack, ruling out the practicality of Nafion composite membranes. In contrast, inexpensive nonfluorinated IEMs like sulfonated or quaternized polyaromatic polymers with high ion selectivity suffer from poor chemical stability, which is due to the introduction of ion exchange groups, particularly in the highly oxidative and strongly acidic electrolyte containing VO $^{2+}$  species.[497] IEMs based on perfluorinated and nonfluorinated materials have been summarized in other reviews and are not the center of attention in this topical review.[491,498] However, besides IEMs, porous membranes with their excellent mechanical and chemical stability and lower cost have recently attracted much more attention for RFB applications.[499,500]

Easily accessible and well-established porous membranes are widely used in commercial LIBs owing to their inherent ability to prevent permeation of unwanted redox species or compounds

by the size exclusion effect. $^{500}$  Several kinds of porous membranes have been designed, fabricated, and investigated for their utility in VRFBs. These include polyacrylonitrile (PAN), $^{500}$  poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVDF), $^{501}$  and PTFE. $^{502}$  It is imperative to mention that the ion selectivity of these porous membranes is relatively inferior to IEMs, which leads to low EE when employed in RFBs. $^{503,504}$  To enhance the ion selectivity of membranes, several strategies have been reported to decrease the pore size and include additives, such as polymers or functional groups, on the surface and inside the porous membranes. $^{505}$

For instance, a novel porous membrane comprising an ultrathin ZSM-35 zeolite flake uniformly distributed onto a poly(ether sulfone) (PES)-based porous membrane has been introduced, as shown in Figure 64a,b. The pore size of the zeolite (ca.  $0.5\mathrm{nm}$ ) is between those of hydrated vanadium ions  $(>0.6\mathrm{nm})$  and protons  $(<0.24\mathrm{nm})$ . Therefore, the RFB with the prepared membrane has showed exceptionally high CE and VE, clearly outperforming the Nafion membranes (Figure 64c). In addition to membranes coated with an inorganic thin layer, an important study has used interfacial polymerization to coat a polyamide selective layer on a porous polymer substrate for electrochemical performance investigation (Figure 64d-f). The synthesized ultrathin polyamide selective layer, having compact cross-linked structure and pore size of  $1\mathrm{nm}$  (between the radius of hydrated vanadium ions and protons), offers high selectivity and chemical stability. Upon cycling, the VRFB with the polyamide selective layer-based sulfonated poly(ether ether ketone) SPEEK/PES membrane performs exceptionally, delivering EE of  $\sim80\%$  at a high current density of  $260\mathrm{mA}$ $\mathrm{cm}^{-2}$  (Figure 64g).

Membrane modification based on structural design is another effective tactic to enhance its electrochemical performance in RFBs. For instance, Zhang et al. $^{508}$  employed the vapor-induced phase inversion method to design a membrane with bulk sponge structure in which thousands of micrometer-sized cells are separated by ultrathin walls. Figure 65a displays the TEM micrographs of fractured sponge membranes stained with  $\mathrm{PdCl}_4^{2-}$ , revealing disconnected pores and ultrathin walls in the membranes. This unique porous structure guarantees high selectivity and high proton conductivity. As far as the electrochemical performance of the aforesaid membrane in RFBs is concerned, the cell upon cycling shows EE of over  $84\%$  at a current density of  $100\mathrm{mA cm}^{-2}$  for 130 cycles (Figure 65b). Similarly, polybenzimidazole (PBI) polymers yield remarkably high thermal, mechanical, and chemical stability along with superior proton conductivity when protonated in acidic conditions. The efficiency of the transport mechanism of protons is predicated on the interaction between protons and nitrogen groups placed on the heterocyclic rings. Pertaining to this, Zhang et al. $^{499}$  has further investigated a new kind of advanced sponge-like porous PBI membrane for achieving superhigh stability and selectivity for VRFB applications (Figure 65c). $^{499}$  In relation to this, the ionic conductivity of the proposed spongy PBI membrane was assured by the acid doped in the PBI pores, whereas the high selectively was attributed to the positively charged pores. As for the ion transfer mechanism, the prepared membrane showed much lower permeability for  $\mathrm{VO}^{2+}$  species and a real resistance than that recorded for Nafion 115 membrane (Figure 65d,e). The supplementary upsides of the spongy PBI membrane include facile fabrication of the low-cost raw materials, ease of scale-up, and higher selectivity and chemical stability, outperforming Nafion. Furthermore, a VRFB

![](images/8c054eacfa9c4de5c5442d3b44e2cee46485057b36c7e101b8cb7e07a407273f.jpg)

![](images/1085cbf1ea984be4bd25f3c86149e55b968bb0ca09328902e09f5e981dfdd3f1.jpg)

Figure 66. PIM for RFBs. (a) Proposed working principle of the hydrophobic PIM. (b) Cross section morphology of PIM-1/PAN composite membrane. based on this membrane delivered excellent electrochemical stability for more than 13000 cycles, as shown in Figure 6Sf.

A hydrophobic polymer with intrinsic microporosity (PIM) has been designed for performance improvement in VRFBs. The PIM is characterized by inherent microporous structure with a pore size less than  $2\mathrm{nm}$  and limited swelling in aqueous solution, which is induced by hydrophobic molecular structure (Figure 66a).509 When assembled in RFBs, the PIM/PAN composite membranes showed high CE of  $\sim 100\%$  at a low current density of  $1\mathrm{mAcm}^{-2}$ , implying no occurrence of vanadium ion crossover (Figure 66b,c). However, the high selectivity of the PIM membrane comes at the expense of proton conductivity. For example, the VE decreases gradually with increasing current density (up to  $40\mathrm{mAcm}^{-2}$  as shown in Figure 66d), which may

be attributed to the exclusion effect of hydrophobic molecules in the membrane. Similarly, a new approach for designing membranes based on hydrophilic PIMs has been recently introduced by Song et al.[505] for enabling fast ion transport and high molecular selectivity (Figure 66e). In this investigation, the incorporation of hydrophilic functional groups (including ionizable groups) was responsible for generating interconnected water channels for augmenting ion conductivity. To explain, the PIMs chosen for the aforementioned investigation were derived from Tröger's base (TB-PIMs) or based on dibenzodioxin with hydrophilic and ionizable amidoxime groups (AO-PIMs), as shown in Figure 66f. When electrochemically cycled in RFBs, these membranes showed fast ion transport combined with high molecular selectivity for aqueous organic flow batteries and

![](images/84c13913f727d57881be1349ab2faf4680ac6b3e1c9cbf2a8efeac7551abcb1e.jpg)

delivered high EE and high capacity retention, which are comparable to those with standard Nafion membranes, as evident in Figure 66g.

A contemporary concept based on membrane-free RFBs has recently emerged to reduce their manufacturing cost. However, such RFBs are still far from being practical. This proposition is typically based on the fluid mechanics of catholytes and anolytes or fine-tuning of the interfacial reactions. For example, a membrane-free hydrogen bromine laminar flow battery, consisting of hydrogen and aqueous bromine oxidant, has been reported.[510] This membrane-free RFB system relies on controlled diffusion for separating reactants and, upon charge-discharge cycling, achieves a power density up to  $0.795\mathrm{Wcm}^{-2}$  with VE of  $92\%$  (Figure 67a). An alternative unique strategy for designing a membrane-free RFB is exploiting immiscible catholyte and anolyte. In this regard, an acidic solution of hydroquinone  $(\mathrm{QH}_2)$  and a hydrophobic IL, 1-butyl-1-methylpyrrolidinium bis(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide, containing dissolved parabenzoquinone (forming a biphasic system), has

been demonstrated for a membrane-free RFB (Figure 67b).511 The cyclic voltammograms (Figure 67c) indicate quasi-reversible redox behavior for both electrolytes. As for the electrochemical performance, this membrane-free RFB operates at a low current density of less than  $0.4\mathrm{mAcm}^{-2}$  (Figure 67d).

Metal electrodes based on stripping-deposition reactions have also been employed to assemble a membrane-free hybrid RFB system, whose operation is based on the immiscibility between metals and electrolytes. Concerning this, the  $\mathrm{Zn / MnO_2}$  flow battery has been introduced as a classic example of a membrane-free RFB, as shown in Figure 67e.[237] The operation of such an RFB system is predicated on enabling dissolution-deposition reactions on both cathodes  $(\mathrm{Mn}^{2 + } / \mathrm{MnO}_2)$  and anodes  $(\mathrm{Zn}^{2 + } / \mathrm{Zn})$ , allowing mixing of anolyte and catholyte into only one electrolyte. This eliminates the otherwise stringent requirement for an ion selective membrane. Upon repetitive charge-discharge cycling, this  $\mathrm{Zn / MnO_2}$  membrane-free RFB displays a high discharge voltage of  $\sim 1.78\mathrm{V}$ , reasonable rate capability with  $10~\mathrm{C}$  discharge, and excellent cycling stability for

7.2.3. Electrolytes. RFB electrolytes are typically composed of a supporting electrolyte and a redox species. The performance of conventional ARFBs is mainly limited by the narrow electrochemical stability window (below 2 V) of aqueous supporting electrolyte, the low concentration of redox species, severe cross-contamination, and the high cost of metal redox species.[469] The modern investigations into developing next generation electrolytes tend to lay strong emphasis on exploring new organic redox species and novel ways to endow the supporting aqueous electrolytes with a high potential window.

7.2.3.1. Redox Species. In RFBs, the redox species hold the utmost importance for dictating the overall cell performance. To explain, the equilibrium potential and solubility, rate of reaction kinetics, chemical stability, and cost effectiveness of redox species directly influence the energy density, power density, cycle life, and practicality of a RFB system.[465] As mentioned earlier, most of the early research on ARFBs employed metal- and halogen-based ions as redox species. Several reviews have summed up the recent advances pertaining to redox species in RFBs.[462,465,481] VRFBs, constituting the most characteristic kind of RFB, still face several key shortcomings, such as high cost and insufficient energy density. To solve these issues, numerous inexpensive and sustainable organic redox materials have been proposed as efficient alternatives to the present metal-based redox species.[512] In this regard, it has been speculated that the redox potential and solubility are likely to be tailored by designing specific side groups on the organic molecular structures, leading to the manufacturing of low-cost high energy density RFBs. Some known organic redox materials, including

metallocenes, carbonyls (including quinones), nitroxide radicals, dialkoxybenzenes, and heterocyclic aromatics, have already been reviewed.512-515 Herein, the most recent studies on synthesis and applications of classic metal complexes and organic redox species have been summarized to serve as a reference for the further development of aqueous organic RFBs.

Compared with single transition metal ions, metal complexes are considerably larger in size, which explains why the latter have lower risk of cross-contamination. Structurally, transition metal complexes are composed of a central transition metal and peripheral ligands. In general, the transition metal ions act as the charge transfer center, while the ligands play their role in tuning the potential. With the proper selection of ligand, the redox potential of the complex can be tuned to meet the rigorous requirements of catholyte and anolyte. Moreover, ligands are beneficial in improving the solubility, reaction kinetic rates, and stability of RFBs. Until now, metal complexes of several metal ions (such as Fe, Co, V, Ce, or Cr) have been studied in conjunction with various ligands, for example, ethylenediaminetetraacetate acid (EDTA), diethylenetriaminepentaacetic acid (DTPA), phenanthroline, and triethanolamine.[516-519] In 2019, for example, Marshak et al.[516] proposed 1,3-propylenediaminetetraacetic acid (PDTA) chelated with chromium metal (hence CrPDTA) as a solvent barrier in electrolyte for inhibiting the formation of  $\mathrm{H}_{2}$ , thereby rendering the development of low-cost and unprecedentedly high-voltage RFBs suitable for GSES applications. In this regard, the  $\mathrm{Cr}^{3+}/\mathrm{Cr}^{2+}$  reduction potential of CrPDTA is  $-1.10\mathrm{V}$  (vs. SHE), which is more negative than its similar counterpart of CrEDTA (Figure 68a-c). It contributes significantly to improving electrode kinetics, thereby resulting in enhanced voltage and efficiency when employed as a catholyte in the flow battery. In addition, the study harnessed the  $\mathrm{Br}_{2}/\mathrm{Br}^{-}$ redox couple in a RFB for delivering unprecedented potential of over  $2.1\mathrm{V}$ . Figure 68d illustrates the discharge curve of a  $\mathrm{Cr}-\mathrm{Br}$  RFB, operating at a cell voltage of  $2\mathrm{V}$  during complete

discharging. Regardless of the excellent electrochemical performance of CrPDTA in RFBs, most metal-ligand complexes have intrinsic limitations for practical applications. Such shortcomings include the scarcity and toxicity of common metal elements and intricate synthesis processes, as well as low solubility of these redox couples.

In 2009, a cadmium/chloranil flow battery proposed by Yang et al., $^{520}$  utilized Cd as the anode redox couple and insoluble tetrachloro- $p$ -benzoquinone (chloranil) as the organic cathode, hence pioneering the concept of organic/inorganic RFBs. Since the inception of this battery system, researchers have paid considerable attention to the utilization of organic redox species in combination with various redox pairs for aqueous and nonaqueous systems. In addition, equivalent emphasis has been

laid on harnessing the efficacy of metal-free compounds, especially in bringing down the manufacturing costs.[521,522]

In 2014, for instance, an innovative metal-free, organic/ inorganic ARFB was reported, $^{522}$  whose chemistry is based on 9,10-anthraquinone-2,7-disulfonic acid (AQDS) and bromine redox couples in sulfuric acid (Figure 69a). Upon electrochemical operation, such ARFB chemistry delivers a notably high power density of  $600\mathrm{mWcm}^{-2}$  at a high current density of  $1300\mathrm{mA cm}^{-2}$ . Despite the excellent capacity retention of  $99.2\%$  at a current density of  $500\mathrm{mA cm}^{-2}$  after 10 cycles, the long-term cycle stability of the ARFB cell over a prolonged time was unknown (Figure 69b). Additionally, this novel ARFB chemistry did not prove eligibility for practical applications owing to its low average discharge voltage ( $\sim 0.6\mathrm{V}$ ) as well as the use of corrosive and hazardous bromine gas. Later, in 2015, the same group

![](images/cacec70e4b5663b94a682ea46e7e88a0f13955c20ef13da7458727e462436979.jpg)  
a

![](images/c0777ed9cfd9a8aff7e85b8450e242729933ddb19f1dec56321c761e7789cdcb.jpg)

![](images/34ebb677bf1fbbd38e3347b11c15f5068a979af31f9c95fd10ba74ff5c678d80.jpg)  
f

reported an alkaline quinone flow battery, comprising commercially available 2,6-dihydroxyanthraquinone (2,6-DHAQ) and low toxicity, nonvolatile ferrocyanide/ferricyanide. The assembled cell, with a high discharge voltage ( $\sim1.1$  V), undergoes cycling at a constant current density of  $\pm0.1$  A cm $^{-2}$  for 100 cycles, and the results are illustrated in Figure 69c,d. It can be seen that the battery exhibits stable RTE of  $84\%$  and the capacity loss of  $0.1\%$  per cycle. Due to these findings, the reported RFB qualifies itself as a potential candidate for cost-efficient, nontoxic, nonflammable, and safe energy-storage system. However, the low solubility (maximum  $0.5\mathrm{M}$ ) of the used organic materials (e.g., 2,6-DHAQ) and strongly alkaline electrolyte need further optimization.

In 2017, a neutral pH organic ARFB was introduced, comprising a novel (ferrocenylmethyl)trimethylammoniumchoride (FcNCl) aqueous catholyte, methyl viologen (MV) anolyte, NaCl supporting electrolyte, and low-cost IEM.524 Upon operation, the FcNCl/MV organic ARFBs,  $0.5\mathrm{M}$  FcNCl

and  $0.5\mathrm{M}$  MV both dissolved in  $2.0\mathrm{MNaCl}$  aqueous solution, exhibit long cycling performance (700 cycles at  $60\mathrm{mAcm}^{-2}$ ), exceptional capacity retention up to  $99.99\%$  per cycle, and power density up to  $125\mathrm{mWcm}^{-2}$  (Figure 69e, f). Despite the low EE of FcNCl/MV (as low as ca.  $60\%$ ), the neutral aqueous organic RFBs with noncorrosive electrolytes and low-cost IEMs promise bright prospects for enabling safe, long life, and low-cost LSES systems.

The first RFB with two redox-active organic compounds, 2,2,6,6-tetramethylpiperidine-1-oxyl (TEMPO) and  $N$ -methylphthalimide acting as the cathode and anode active materials, respectively, was reported by Li et al. in 2011.[525] Even today, the progression of all-organic RFBs carrying bipolar redox-active material has received high interest as a result of its constituent electrode materials undergoing oxidation as well as reduction reactions and, therefore, being able to function as both cathode and anode. For example, Liu et al.[526] introduced an all organic neutral ARFB (Figure 70a) that employs MV anolyte and 4-

![](images/b2ae761d9f126eb5c2a780e7afeedf7eacc99e413e70ca042ca915598c677e99.jpg)

![](images/90ef9dbfee41a8a0d3ea36066b19411718860ec2f6aa58e2f6c460658178aa25.jpg)

![](images/0b8c8d268062b81425c6e0eada69dd69454e98171ec6b38b787ee0cc9c1e4564.jpg)

![](images/955ae7344018bdd6780ccdda718a271c7ada430e3a504ca69c2d278d8c7c027d.jpg)

hydroxy-2,2,6,6-tetramethylpiperidin-1-oxyl (4-HO-TEMPO) catholyte. Even though this battery chemistry exhibited high capacity retention at  $60\mathrm{mAcm}^{-2}$  after 100 charge/discharge cycles (Figure 70b), its long-term cycle stability over extended periods of time is limited. Similarly, Liu and co-workers[527] have reported a long life all organic neutral ARFB (Figure 70d) in which positively charged functional groups introduced to enhance Coulombic repulsion help to extend the lifetime of the MV derivative bis(trimethylammonio) propyl viologen (BTMAP-Vi). In contrast, a stable variant of the radical molecule 4-[3-(trimethylammonio)propoxy]-2,2,6,6-tetramethylpiperidine-1-oxyl (TMAP-TEMPO) chloride can be synthesized from 4-OH-TEMPO by adding Coulombic repulsion to suppress bimolecular interactions, thereby eliminating molecular decomposition (Figure 70c,e). Upon cycling, the TMAP-TEMPO/BTMAP-Vi RFB displays CE of more than  $99.73\%$  along with significantly long cycling lifetime. In addition,

the cell retains  $94\%$  of the initial capacity after 1000 repetitive cycles, projecting a capacity retention rate of  $99.993\%$  per cycle (Figure 70f).

In an attempt to further the development of novel all-organic ARFBs, an all-organic ARFB was reported in 2015 that makes use of organic polymers as the redox-active species in an aqueous NaCl electrolyte, along with an inexpensive dialysis membrane for separating analyte and catholyte (Figure 71b). The cathode material involves the TEMPO radical as the redox-active moiety, while the anode material is a 4,49-bipyridine derivative (named Vi). The cathode and anode redox species are synthesized by free radical polymerization and succeeding polymer-analogous oxidation and functionalization, respectively (Figure 71a). The dialysis membrane with pore size of less than  $1\mathrm{nm}$  facilitates the retention of redox-active polymers with a hydrodynamic radius of  $\sim 2\mathrm{nm}$ . Moreover, the use of a high molecular weight and inexpensive polymeric dialysis membrane

provides a great replacement for costly IEMs, rendering this allorganic ARFB battery chemistry economical. However, during flow-cell testing, significant capacity decay of  $\sim 25\%$  over 95 cycles at a current density of  $40~\mathrm{mA~cm}^{-2}$  restricts the practicality of this technology for long life cycle applications

(Figure 71c). It is imperative to point out that such a rapid capacity decay is attributed to the occurrence of a side reaction possibly induced by oxygen. Despite the insufficient cyclic stability and low volumetric energy density of  $10.8\mathrm{WhL}^{-1}$  of the

![](images/301bf557e3856d153e6e7a22ab059d7df0d79ee2efb72775d26e35446928ef86.jpg)

aforementioned RFB technology, the practice of using polymer-based redox couples has bright future prospects.

Likewise, homogeneously dispersed, microsized polymer particulates have been designed to serve as redox-active species for an all-polymer particulate slurry RFB (Figure 71d).529 In this RFB, the used catholyte and anolyte are  $1.0\mathrm{mol}$  of polyhydroquinone (PHQ) and naphthalene-1,4,5,8-tetracarboxylic acid dianhydride-ethylenediamine copolymer (PII) particulate, both in  $2\mathrm{M}$  sulfuric acid solution. The average sizes of polymer particulates have been determined to be 1.195  $\mu \mathrm{m}$  for PHQ and  $2.675\mu \mathrm{m}$  for PI1 (Figure 71e). These redox-active species not only exceed the solubility limit of conventional active materials but also promise the widespread applicability of insoluble redox-active materials and dialysis membranes in RFBs. As shown in Figure 71f, the long-term reversible cycling of the all-polymer particulate slurry RFB (APPSB) at  $20\mathrm{mAcm}^{-2}$  shows an initial discharge capacity of  $4.95\mathrm{AhL}^{-1}$  with CE approaching  $100\%$  and EE  $\sim 60\%$ . The discharge capacity retention after 300 cycles is  $70\%$  of the initial capacity, which is equivalent to a capacity decay of  $0.1\%$  per cycle.

The summary of key parameters of the most recently developed aqueous organic RFBs is presented in Table 9. Although numerous ground-breaking studies reveal good electrochemical performance, the development of aqueous

organic RFBs is still in its infancy. The energy density and cyclic stability of organic RFBs are yet to be improved to compare with the performance of VRFBs (Figure 72a).513 Notwithstanding the aforementioned concerns, the incorporation of organic redox materials greatly diversifies the befitting material selection range and their thematic redox mechanisms for RFB furtherance. For this purpose, a hierarchical computational scheme has been proven effective for screening organic redox materials with optimal redox potential, solubility, and stability from abundant molecules based on successive property evaluations obtained from high-throughput computations (Figure 72b).530 Such computational high-throughput screening incorporates real investigations for accelerating discovery of optimal organic redox species for RFB applications. Such an approach gives fundamental insight at the molecular level, which is imperative to improve performance of organic active materials. Recently, several in situ nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) techniques have been reported (Figure 72c)531 for investigating electrolyte decomposition and battery self-discharge mechanisms in real time. Such NMR methods are known to present great potential in understanding a wide range of redox kinetics in RFB systems.

7.2.3.2. Nonaqueous Electrolytes. In 1988, Matsuda et al.[534] pioneered the first RFB based on a nonaqueous supporting

electrolyte with an open-circuit potential (OCP) of  $2.6\mathrm{V}$ . With innumerable research studies already existing on aqueous electrolytes, the transition toward nonaqueous electrolytes has yielded higher cell voltages (greater than  $2\mathrm{V}$ ) within the domain of RFBs. Furthermore, it has substantially expanded the library of material selections to motivate the exploration of novel RFB

chemistries. $^{535}$  This has given rise to the possibility of developing a new generation of RFBs with high equilibrium potentials, unprecedented energy densities, higher EEs, and low cost. Moreover, the packaging cost of nonaqueous RFBs is likely to be reduced as fewer components and units are required for synthesizing these cells while delivering electrochemical

performance comparable to that of their ARFB counterparts. These advantages overall enhance the reliability of nonaqueous RFB systems. As far as the current research trend on nonaqueous RFBs is concerned, it focuses principally on the development of redox active materials, such as metal-ligand complexes and organic molecules.[536] However, the research on nonaqueous RFBs is in its preliminary developmental stages. In addition, this battery technology is facing dire challenges in terms of its limitation to scale-up and to reach the energy densities of its aqueous counterparts owing to extremely low solubility of the active redox species in nonaqueous electrolytes. In addition to these concerns, the ionic conductivities of nonaqueous electrolytes are intrinsically low, especially for the highly concentrated redox materials. Also, thorough research on developing suitable membranes with low area resistance for nonaqueous RFBs is still in order. Important parameters of the most recent nonaqueous RFB systems have been summarized in Table 10. It is evident that most of these chemistries employ active materials with low

concentration, generally less than  $0.5\mathrm{M}$ , which explains their low energy densities (less than  $30\mathrm{WhL^{-1}}$ ) even at high cell voltages.[53] Even though some of these RFBs can achieve high solubilities (e.g.,  $0.8\mathrm{M}$  Fc1N112-TFSI in  $1.2\mathrm{M}$  LiTFSI and  $2\mathrm{M}$  TEMPO in  $1.0\mathrm{M}$ $\mathrm{LiPF_6}$ ), these cells yield very short cycle life (less than 20 cycles) at ultralow current density below  $2\mathrm{mA}$ $\mathrm{cm}^{-2}$ . Moreover, compared with aqueous electrolytes, nonaqueous electrolytes are expensive and flammable, which are key limitations when it comes to their practical applications in LSES.

7.2.3.3. Semisolid Electrolytes. As a mitigation strategy to solve the conundrum of low solubility of redox species in nonaqueous RFBs, the concept of semisolid electrolyte (Figure 73a,b) has been pioneered by Chiang and co-workers.[541] In the electrolyte, the insoluble active materials are mixed with conducting carbon and are dispersed in the supporting electrolyte to form a suspension. RFBs with semisolid electrolyte (also known as semisolid RFBs) permit electrolyte concentration beyond the solubility limit and yield much higher

![](images/108ca05321530f74aaf7ca2d6208f20fc5c3829adcd30c3f8ceb45f7c4d540d2.jpg)

![](images/1d6ba139c7ef5b68865b41ec21d6cecf8dbf1602ccdc1da004c32874e079ed57.jpg)

![](images/adb731472cb4144663fca3ebbeb53841d0e35b4874d17d5894879b92f17d9af8.jpg)

![](images/fcb6bdcd618f04dbfb7c4fc68ee178e7c29fa0621b465ffc32dac8e2056f18e3.jpg)

![](images/37dbc461fc8d13c146bf70ad51b991a76ec912a80d71ebc7dfb135727a9b7fef.jpg)  
Figure 74. (a) Assembly of a redox-targeting-based RFB. (b) Scheme compiling the chemical reactions between the redox mediators and materials. (c) CV of the redox mediators and Li storage materials (LFP and  $\mathrm{TiO}_2$ ). Reproduced with permission from ref 550. Copyright 2015 The American Association for the Advancement of Science. (d) Schematic of a symmetric flow battery consisting of an electrochemical cell and two energy storage tanks. PB granules are loaded in the cathodic tank for energy storage. The inset shows the conversions of PB/PW and PB/BG upon charging/ discharging. (e) Electrochemical performance of  $\mathrm{Zn} / [\mathrm{Fe}(\mathrm{CN})_6]^{3 - }$ PB hybrid flow cell at  $20\mathrm{mAcm^{-2}}$  . (f) CE, VE, and EE of  $\mathrm{Zn} / [\mathrm{Fe}(\mathrm{CN})_6]^{3 - }$ PB hybrid flow cell at  $20\mathrm{mAcm^{-2}}$  . (g) Volumetric capacity and chemical cost of  $[\mathrm{Fe(CN)}_6]^{4 - / 3 - }$ PB system and its comparison with other reported electrolytes. Reproduced with permission from ref 551. Copyright 2019 Elsevier.

![](images/5116d66a983386e7d83f1e01e725bbe86cfd235788b669ea8136a59b3304c51e.jpg)

![](images/152523d07271676047e70e0f628b76ebfbe629f098163c574fbd410013e3b748.jpg)

volumetric capacity compared to traditional RFBs. During electrochemical operation, uniform charge transfers between the active material particles and the current collector, which is ascribed to the percolation nanoscale conductor network in the electrode suspension. As compared to traditional electrolyte in RFBs, a semisolid suspension in semisolid RFBs poses a lower risk of cross-contamination, enabling the use of a microporous membrane rather than an expensive IEM. Originally, a semisolid lithium-ion flow cell employed LCO (20 vol %, 10.2 M) and 1.5% Ketjen black catholyte and  $\mathrm{Li_4Ti_5O_{12}}$  (10 vol %, 2.3 M) and 2% Ketjen black anolyte. The charge capacity of this state-of-the-art RFB cell approached the theoretical capacity of  $\mathrm{Li_4Ti_5O_{12}}$  (170 mAh g $^{-1}$ ), and the CE was up to 73% and 80% in the first and second cycle, respectively.[541] Since the inception of the

semisolid RFB concept, various active materials, for example, LCO,  $\mathrm{LiNi}_{0.5}\mathrm{Mn}_{1.5}\mathrm{O}_4$ ,  $\mathrm{LiNi}_{1/3}\mathrm{Co}_{1/3}\mathrm{Mn}_{1/3}\mathrm{O}_2$ , LFP,  $\mathrm{Li}_4\mathrm{Ti}_5\mathrm{O}_{12}$ , silicon, and graphite, have been investigated.[371,542-544] However, the improved concentrations of semisolid suspensions are inevitably accompanied by high viscosities, which in turn give rise to high driving pressures and flow resistance. In this regard, in 2015, Chen et al. designed a sulfur-impregnated carbon composite for a semisolid RFB cell.[545] Unlike mechanically mixed sulfur-carbon composite suspension, sulfur infiltrates into carbon via heat treatment for improving electrical contact and decreased viscosity (Figure 73c). Figure 73d demonstrates, for a Li-flow cell with the  $20\mathrm{vol}\%$  S and  $26\mathrm{vol}\%$  Ketjen black catholyte operating at a current density of  $4\mathrm{mA~cm}^{-2}$ , the catholyte's volumetric capacity within the range of  $110 - 155\mathrm{Ah}$

![](images/18d81df475b7ece8e13afa874dcf66300e5da392bcdedc1a48dfc2e5f4844fec.jpg)  
a

![](images/81c152efc7a591da6fe6b38fb41bc85e0c579c8e991d82d0022e0fb8c440d083.jpg)  
b

![](images/0cfbaa113cc75b20475f7c8c6ec528696f12adf42cf9db9a6542e6d76f4b19e5.jpg)  
C

![](images/3685bd724f16c6162379fe7d089e436e5baab2f8f42fe275415e5ba4a4ddf642.jpg)  
d

![](images/1817dcfc0f6ba31542a3a2e2faa5af3ced2f088f5f1cfe54f22752cdba8ec214.jpg)
