![](images/d1db785027a9ecd4bedb35c63093e3f44e04f9f9d4a7b914142dbbbe2a2e9f11.jpg)  
f  
Figure 75. Typical examples of novel RFBs. (a) Integrated SFB using III-V tandem cell photoelectrode and 4-OH-TEMPO/MVCl $_2$  redox couples. (b) Estimation method and pathways to boost the SOEE of SFB device. (c) Device cycling behavior shows cell potential and photocurrent density delivered by the photoelectrode. (d) SFB displayed a stable SOEE around  $14\%$  for ten cycles. (e) Schematic of cross section of the SFB, showing two single junction silicon photoelectrodes and two CF inert electrodes, which could be connected in three different modes. Reproduced with permission from ref 556. Copyright 2018 Elsevier. (f) Representative device cycling behavior showing cell potential between cathode and anode (red curves), as well as the photocurrent density delivered by the photoelectrodes connected in series (blue curves). (g) Representative potential-capacity profiles during galvanostatic discharging process at the 1st, 50th, and 100th cycles. (h) Discharging capacity utilization rate and SOEE, showing a stable cycling performance over  $200\mathrm{h}$  (100 cycles). Reproduced with permission from ref 557. Copyright 2019 Wiley-VCH.

![](images/a6d871292c53d05b2449913c6037395553475a0a3565f5dcae15b8cabdfa95ce.jpg)  
9

![](images/31854e0e64fbff36a94e26f86e2b7fcb03e08295d4b60f402ac9a111a586f217.jpg)  
h

$\mathrm{L}^{-1}$ . Similarly, later in 2016, a novel concept of a multiple redox semisolid-liquid (MRSSL) flow battery was put forward for achieving higher energy density than previously reported semisolid RFBs.[371] In principle, the MRSSL RFB takes advantage of both highly soluble active material in the liquid phase (LiI) and high-capacity active material in the solid phase (S/C composite), by forming a biphasic MRSSL catholyte (Figure 73e).[371] During the electrochemical cycling, extraordinary volumetric capacity  $(550\mathrm{Ah}\mathrm{L}^{-1})$  was achieved for the catholyte, thanks to the highly concentrated and synergistic multiple redox reactions of LiI and sulfur. Regardless of the choice of highly concentrated electrolytes, most of the reported semisolid RFBs operate at a low current density (below to  $5\mathrm{mA}$ $\mathrm{cm}^{-2}$ ). Furthermore, the evidence of their long-term cycling stability during reversible cycling is still unclear and requires further scrutiny. Other issues that need immediate attention include the overall poor cell performance of semisolid RFBs owing to the highly viscous suspensions and the demanding extra pumping burden. Last but not least, unlike the structural

design of traditional RFBs, which comprises multiple reaction stacks and complex flow fields, ample efforts are needed to propose an optimized and scalable engineering design for these semisolid RFB systems.

7.2.3.4. Redox-Targeting-Based Electrolytes. Another fresh approach to boost the capacity of redox electrolyte is through redox-targeting-based electrolytes, consisting of redox targeted mediators and active materials.[546] In this regard, the redox reaction involves exchanging of charge carriers between redox mediators and active materials, whereby power is produced by the electrochemical reactions of the redox mediators in RFBs. In addition, the energy is deposited in solid active materials from the redox-mediated chemical reactions.[547,548] In other words, the redox targeted mediators transfer charge between the electrode and the active material upon repetitive cycling. Compared with semisolid RFBs or traditional LIBs, the concept of the redox mediator offers competitive advantages in terms of eliminating the necessity of using ion conducting additives and binders for insulating active materials. In addition, the viscosity

and complexity of such electrolytes are comparable to those of traditional RFBs. Among many studies conducted in this regard, Wang et al. has reported the first RFB with a redox-targeting-based electrolyte (RTFB) using LFP as the  $\mathrm{Li^{+}}$  storage material in the cathodic tank and lithium metal as the anode.[549]  $1,1^{\prime}$ -Dibromoferrocene ( $\mathrm{FcBr}_2$ ,  $3.55\mathrm{V}$  vs.  $\mathrm{Li} / \mathrm{Li}^{+}$ ) and ferrocene ( $\mathrm{Fc}$ ,  $3.25\mathrm{V}$  vs.  $\mathrm{Li} / \mathrm{Li}^{+}$ ) were selected as the two redox-shuttle molecules in the catholyte so as to undergo chemical delithiation and lithiation of LFP molecules. Later, a novel RTFB chemistry was introduced, which comprised a LFP cathode paired with  $\mathrm{FcBr}_2 / \mathrm{Fc}$  and a  $\mathrm{TiO}_2$  anode paired with cobaltocene  $[\mathrm{Co}(\mathrm{Cp})_2]$  and bis(pentamethylcyclopentadienyl)cobalt  $[\mathrm{Co}(\mathrm{Cp}^*)_2]$  (Figure 74a-c).[550] Considering  $50\%$  porosity of the materials in the tank, the theoretical energy density of this RTFB can reach up to  $500\mathrm{WhL^{-1}}$ , which is ten times higher than that reported for conventional VRFBs. In general, the rate of the characteristic redox targeting reaction in RTFBs directly affects the battery power, as well as the consumption of active materials. At present, considerably low current densities have been reported for RTFBs. Therefore, it is absolutely necessary to design appropriate molecules and active materials for exhibiting rapid redox reaction kinetics. The bright side is that the redox-targeted strategy-just like nonaqueous RFBs-is also applicable in ARFB systems. As a consequence of positive advancement toward the development of organic redox species, a high-capacity ferrocyanide/ferricyanide  $\left[\mathrm{Fe(CN)}_6\right]^{4 - / 3 - }$  based electrolyte system has been designed for ARFBs by means of redox targeting reactions with Prussian blue  $\left(\mathrm{Fe}_4[\mathrm{Fe}(\mathrm{CN})_6]_3,\mathrm{PB}\right)$ .[551] Upon employing  $0.8\mathrm{M}\left[\mathrm{Fe}(\mathrm{CN})_6\right]^{3 - }$  neutral solution as catholyte, the RFB cell delivers a volumetric capacity of 56.3 Ah  $\mathrm{L}^{-1}$  in the presence of PB, which is much higher than that without PB (Figure 74d). Additionally, the average CE and VE of the  $\mathrm{Zn} / \left[\mathrm{Fe}(\mathrm{CN})_6\right]^{3 - } - \mathrm{PB}$  flow cell are  $99.7\%$  and  $87.7\%$  at  $20\mathrm{mAcm}^{-2}$ , respectively, upon cycling for  $500\mathrm{h}$  (Figure 74e). In short, the aqueous systems show much lower expenses, higher ionic conductivity, and more convenient cell assembly procedures as compared with their nonaqueous equivalents. The incorporation of PB into  $\left[\mathrm{Fe}(\mathrm{CN})_6\right]^{4 - / 3 - }$ -based electrolyte via redox targeting reactions imparts distinct advantages over traditional electrolytes for ARFBs (Figure 74f).

7.2.4. Novel RFBs. Integrating both solar energy conversion and energy-storage functions into one device has been proven to be effective in giving rise to more compact, efficient, and cost-effective solar energy storage, as well as consumption, especially for off-grid applications. In this regard, solar RFBs with flowing liquid-phase redox species exhibit relatively fast reaction kinetics, thereby providing high solar-to-output electricity efficiency (SOEE). For instance, a photoelectrode[552] or dual photoelectrode[553-555] produce holes and electrons for facilitating oxidation or reduction reactions of the redox couples in order to convert intermittent solar energy into chemical energy. Despite the promising research involving solar flow battery (SFB) technology, most such devices suffer from several common issues, including low SOEE, high cost, and short lifetime.[555] Recently, a novel design for a highly efficient SFB has been proposed, which involves the monolithic integration of highly efficient, high-photovoltage tandem III-V SFBs with high-voltage 4-hydroxy-2,2,6,6-tetramethylpiperidin-1-oxyl (4-OH-TEMPO)/MV RFBs, for delivering a remarkable SOEE of  $14.1\%$  (Figure 75a).[556] Further, it has been proven that it is possible to achieve the highest SOEE by matching the voltage of RFBs with the maximum power point of the photoelectrode (Figure 75b). The characteristic cycling behavior of the

aforementioned SFB and its SOEE for ten cycles are illustrated in Figure 75c,d. The concerns related to this SFB technology include its unclear cycling performance upon reversible cycling and high manufacturing cost of III-V tandem junction SFBs, hindering its practical applications. Most recently, in 2019, a neutral pH aqueous electrolyte-based SFB with robust BTMAP functionalized Vi and Fc redox couples and inexpensive stable silicon-based photoelectrodes has been investigated (Figure 75e). Unlike the previously mentioned monolithically integrated SFB, this SFB device exhibits continuously stable cycling performance over  $200\mathrm{h}$  along with an improved SOEE of only  $5.4\%$  (Figure 75f-h).

# 7.3. Summary and Future Perspective for RFBs

Achieving high energy density, low cost, and long cycling life is urgently needed for the large-scale applications of RFBs. In this section, the most prominent studies focusing on the optimization of electrodes, membranes, redox species, and various promising novel RFB have been summarized and discussed with their respective advantages and limitations. Regardless of extensive work, the capital cost target of RFBs is still far from what has been set by the US DoE \((\\) 100\mathrm{kWh}^{-1}\) ) for GSES, therefore demanding groundbreaking development of novel RFB chemistries. The limitations of metal ion redox species and Nafion membranes encourage researchers to reorient themselves toward harnessing the full potential of nonaqueous or all-organic RFB technologies and porous membranes. Organic ARFBs have been considered as one of the most promising candidates for giving rise to next-generation RFBs due to the low cost and high safety. In addition, lithium-based RFBs with semisolid or redox targeting concepts are becoming a hot topic for combining the inherent advantages of LIBs and RFBs. In view of the LSES applications, SFBs are promising for exhibiting unique advantages and are therefore to be used for commercial applications in the future. In addition, the flow fields, stacks, and engineering considerations are equally important to improve the performance and economic aspects of the RFB technologies and have been analyzed in detail in the literature.[558] In short, the pursuit of green and LSES technologies is driving future research on exploring novel and potentially more powerful next-generation RFB systems. Finding excellent materials for enhancing the overall performance and lowering the cost of RFBs is essential for revitalizing the LSES needs.

# 8. METAL-AIR BATTERIES

# 8.1. Introduction

The currently known commercial LIBs, in spite of their extensive utilization in addressing our current energy needs, have reached their performance limits given their inability to further improve their energy and power densities for LSES applications, such as powering automobiles and grid stations.[42,559] Therefore, this inadequacy has recently been addressed by the revival of electrochemical MAB systems with threshold energy density of 500 Wh  $\mathrm{kg}^{-1}$ .[560,561] Historically, the idea of MABs was primarily patented by Maiche dating back to 1878,[562] later commercialized by Heise in 1932,[563] and further advanced after 1960 with the advent of aqueous Al-air, Fe-air, and Mg-air batteries.[564-566] However, it was not until two decades ago that nonaqueous metal-air energy storage systems were pioneered first with the advent of the Li-air battery by Abraham and Jiang[567] and, afterward, with Na-air and K-air cells by Hartmann et al.[568] and Ren and Wu,[569] respectively.

![](images/66a6adc809213354b4b49211cd999a068b0c90a815cfd05d615743476c19782e.jpg)  
Volumetric energy density (kWh L-1)

![](images/3fe244031454f6042eb32c29b730c6b629bc796130823fae800e275bfb5359b6.jpg)  
Theoretical energy density (kWh kg-1)

![](images/a94f2ea81fa865a3e43ec4755feb6ea87e70b5fc5f39639aa97b31bf988db106.jpg)  
Figure 76. Theoretical and practical energy densities of aqueous MAB systems. $^{572,573}$  
Figure 77. Brief timeline showing chronological progress in Zn-air battery technology over the years.[562,563,578,579,584-593]

Typically, the working principle of rechargeable MABs is governed by the oxidation and reduction of metallic anode and oxygen cathode. Since MABs import design features from both fuel cells and conventional batteries such as metal-ion cells, they require a liquid electrolyte between the electrodes split by a separator.[570] The electrochemical reactions occurring at the electrodes and electrolytes, however, differ depending upon the types of metallic anodes, as has been reported.[571] For instance,  $\mathrm{Zn}$ ,  $\mathrm{Mg}$ ,  $\mathrm{Fe}$ , and  $\mathrm{Al}$  are the metals whose surfaces, upon undergoing passive treatments by means of corresponding hydroxides or oxides, are rendered thermodynamically compatible for the aqueous electrolytes to some degree.[561,570] That is why such metals have been widely studied for aqueous MABs (Figure 76). The electrochemical reactions for aqueous MABs are governed by the following equations:[561]

Metal anode:

$$
\mathrm {M} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {M} ^ {n +} + n \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \tag {32}
$$

Air cathode:

$$
\mathrm {O} _ {2} + 2 \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} + 4 \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \leftrightarrow 4 \mathrm {O H} ^ {-} \tag {33}
$$

Overall reaction:

$$
4 \mathrm {M} + n \mathrm {O} _ {2} + 2 n \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} \leftrightarrow 4 \mathrm {M} ^ {n +} + 4 n \mathrm {O H} ^ {-} \tag {34}
$$

Here, M stands for metal, such as Zn, Al, Mg, or Fe, and  $n$  denotes the metal ion charge transfer number. It is important to mention that the above electrochemical reactions represent the discharging process from left to right and can be reversed upon recharging of the MAB. In other words, during recharging, the metal is plated back on the anode and oxygen is released at the

![](images/aa2608bd0d9ebb8cc9302aaf69dc403976a8a91036ae86a2a9b70fe7f195bf52.jpg)  
Figure 78. Schematic configuration of Zn-air battery with the design of Zn anodes, cathode catalysts, and gas diffusion electrode structure. Reproduced with permission from ref 562. Copyright 2019 Royal Society of Chemistry.

cathode. In contrast to the aqueous MAB family, metals such as alkali metals of Li, K, or Na are too reactive in aqueous electrolytes. Even though the reactivity of these metals is possibly hampered by ionic conductive films, for instance, glass ceramic, the fabrication of their corresponding aqueous alkali MAB systems is too intricate and expensive to render it practical.[561] Therefore, Li-, K-, and Na-air battery systems better work in aprotic or nonaqueous electrolytes, giving rise to the highly acknowledged nonaqueous MABs. The electrochemical reactions of such battery systems are as follows:[561,570]

Anode:

$$
\mathrm {M} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {M} ^ {+} + \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \tag {35}
$$

Cathode:

$$
x \mathrm {M} ^ {+} + \mathrm {O} _ {2} + x \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {M} _ {x} \mathrm {O} _ {2} \quad (\text {w h e r e} x = 1 \text {o r} 2) \tag {36}
$$

Overall reaction:

$$
x \mathrm {M} + \mathrm {O} _ {2} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {M} _ {x} \mathrm {O} _ {2} \tag {37}
$$

It is obvious that the difference of chemical reactions between aqueous and nonaqueous MAB systems lies in the oxygen reduction reaction (ORR) and oxygen evolution reaction (OER). Apropos nonaqueous metal-air systems, ORR comprises reduction of molecular oxygen on the surface of cathode electrocatalyst to generate  $\mathrm{O}_2^{2-}$  or  $\mathrm{O}_2^-$ , which then combines with metal cation to form  $\mathrm{O}_2^{2-}$  or  $\mathrm{O}_2^-$  or the mixture of both as discharge products.[561,571] Apart from the principal difference of redox reactions, another factor that differentiates such batteries from their aqueous counterparts is the higher sensitivity of lithium, potassium, and sodium to  $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$  and  $\mathrm{CO}_2$  in the atmospheric air. Therefore, MABs with these metallic anodes are generally studied in pure  $\mathrm{O}_2$  environments, and thereby rather termed as  $\mathrm{Li}-\mathrm{O}_2$ ,  $\mathrm{K}-\mathrm{O}_2$ , and  $\mathrm{Na}-\mathrm{O}_2$  batteries.

The aqueous MABs gain significant advantages over the nonaqueous counterparts in terms of technology maturity, practical implementation, and mass production for GSES. Considering that there are a number of excellent review articles on the nonaqueous MABs,[574-577] we focus on discussing the aqueous MABs and their potential for GSES applications in this review. The following subsections delineate the basic operational chemistry of Zn-air batteries as the leading example of aqueous metal-air batteries.

# 8.2. Zinc-Air Batteries

Zn-air, Al-air and Mg-air batteries are known as stable aqueous MAB systems, among which Zn-air batteries have long been studied.[562,578] Figure 77 shows a brief chronological account of progress concerning Zn-air batteries. The first Zn-air battery was invented in 1878, soon after the patent introducing the concept of MABs in 1848. From the commercialization of primary Zn-air batteries in 1932 to the industrialization of secondary Zn-air systems around 2012, little scientific advancement was made with the paramount breakthrough being rechargeable Zn-air energy devices. However, it is in the past decade that the progress on these systems has gained phenomenal momentum, from pioneering the flexible Zn-air battery in 2014 for wearable electronics to harnessing the natural-based electrocatalytic materials, such as silk- and wood-derived ones, for enhancing their electrochemical performance.[579,580] Nonetheless, severe issues such as the formation of zinc dendrites and EEs as low as  $60\%$  due to the lack of newly proposed bifunctional catalysts, hindered their early progress in the twentieth century.[581] As compared to Al-air and Mg-air batteries, Zn-air electrochemical energy storage systems are comparatively more stable, thanks to their charging capabilities in aqueous electrolytes. The Zn-air battery has caught much attention due to its high theoretical energy density of  $\sim 1300\mathrm{Whkg^{-1}}$ .[582] Therefore, along with  $\mathrm{Li - O_2}$  cells, Zn-air batteries have been predicted to offer very promising prospects. The scientists also speculate that advanced future Zn-air battery systems may surpass LIBs in their performance for serving in LSES applications.[583]

The basic structural schematic of a typical Zn-air battery, as illustrated in Figure 78, is composed of a Zn anode, a porous air cathode with suitable catalysts, and aqueous electrolytes. The Zn anode can be designed to have various forms including foam, fiber, sponge, coating, and alloying, while the air cathode has flexibility in the catalyst design, such as metal-free carbon, M-N-C, perovskite, and spinel structures. The gas diffusion electrode (GDE), consisting of a catalyst nanoparticle layer coating on nanoporous carbon with a gas diffusion polymer (typically PTFE) as the current collector, plays an important role in the electrocatalytic reactions of the air cathode in OER/ ORR (Figure 78). The electrode reactions that take place in the Zn-air cell can be expressed in the following equations:

![](images/01572ed237a2dfde8c93d88e5391320d4e2993ddb03da23da37e5487efda9f47.jpg)

![](images/31619664c449219d95f106e1f8f7b52d20a545128389b4331b56f22907b47e08.jpg)

![](images/f7bad1b90f178393c910876b3ec083486fef52da3ad1d91c005db37f14c7846d.jpg)

![](images/fdb8136701015b9e9ed30874990993a5e27768d4ff67f0f44f498b3eb94921e4.jpg)

![](images/49783d376cf5e532165aefd1adebd0b1f6973fe8fbdfed02db4e1b3931098bbf.jpg)

![](images/c1097baa346aa2fcfa3c8c3fd8d06e11920a3a08b0d568bb2ff6188137d23817.jpg)

![](images/84cbc378b83844ed9e2f7b9e7785663e79fbccdf27b829ab3deedaee3136cb6f.jpg)

![](images/8182a3dda27236b8d0ab0838c3b03a6eea069684b4b91e739deefec81c40647f.jpg)

![](images/6b08328f0815dd754fb3eb640abef097a3e07274bb9c969524fb0ec90d2fe94b.jpg)

![](images/1a4d4dd94c72e749c8a474b40c93b64c26c5f0154225262b8e04198abe317f51.jpg)  
Figure 79. Structural configurations and electrochemical performances of metal-based air cathode electrocatalysts. (a) Schematic of OER/ORR activity of nanoscale  $(1\mathrm{nm})$ $\mathrm{CoO}_x$  layer on metallic substrate made of Co/N-RGO. (b) Polarization and power density curves of secondary Zn-air batteries showing superior performance of ultrathin  $\mathrm{CoO}_x@\mathrm{Co}/\mathrm{N}$ -doped GO as compared to  $\mathrm{Pt/C+RuO_2}$ . Reproduced with permission from ref 603. Copyright 2019 Wiley-VCH. (c) Crystal structure of typical spinel oxide. (d) Representation of octahedral coordination with regards to d-orbital. (e) ORR activity and (f) OER activity of different spinels in terms of  $\mathbf{e}_{\mathrm{g}}$  occupancy of active element at octahedral site. Reproduced with permission from ref 613. Copyright 2017 Wiley-VCH. (g) Crystal structure of  $\mathrm{ABO}_3$  perovskite oxide and activation sites. Reproduced with permission from ref 614. Copyright 2017 The American Association for the Advancement of Science. (h) Diagram of CCBC and ORR/OER activity in MABs. (i) Charge/

![](images/cf9cb83239273d698625b953a640a3ec2e330950f511ededd18a878a7b09784d.jpg)

![](images/ab8ca2a26ed202028a35cca4e0094c0349247b9dd55bbe511be267574b69e83e.jpg)

Figure 79. continued

discharge polarization curves showing superior performance of CCBC as compared to  $\mathrm{Pt / C}$  and conventional perovskite LaNiO $_3$ . OER and ORR activity of single metal/C and double metal/C composites as electrocatalysts in Zn-air batteries. Reproduced with permission from ref 609. Copyright 2012 American Chemical Society. (j) Structure and OER/ORR performance of S,N-Fe/N/C-CNT composite in Zn-air cells. (k) Overall LSV polarization curves showing excellent ORR and OER performance of S,N-Fe/N/C-CNT in Zn-air battery with 0.1 M KOH solution as compared to other cathode catalysts. (l) Discharge-polarization and power density plots of Zn-air battery based on S,N-Fe/N/C-CNT and Pt/C electrocatalysts at a constant charge/discharge current density of  $5\mathrm{mAcm}^{-2}$ . Reproduced with permission from ref 611. Copyright 2017 Wiley-VCH.

Air cathode:

$$
\mathrm {O} _ {2} + 4 \mathrm {e} ^ {-} + 2 \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} \leftrightarrow 4 \mathrm {O H} ^ {-} \tag {38}
$$

Zn anode:

$$
\mathrm {Z n} + 4 \mathrm {O H} ^ {-} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {Z n} (\mathrm {O H}) _ {4} ^ {2 -} + 2 \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \tag {39}
$$

$$
\mathrm {Z n} (\mathrm {O H}) _ {4} ^ {2 -} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {Z n O} + \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} + 2 \mathrm {O H} ^ {-} \tag {40}
$$

Overall anode:

$$
\mathrm {Z n} + 2 \mathrm {O H} ^ {-} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {Z n O} + \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} + 2 \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \tag {41}
$$

Overall:

$$
2 \mathrm {Z n} + \mathrm {O} _ {2} \leftrightarrow 2 \mathrm {Z n O} \tag {42}
$$

During discharge, the atmospheric oxygen diffusing into the cathode is reduced to form hydroxide ions through ORR, which migrate to the Zn anode to give away zincate ions  $(\mathrm{Zn(OH)}_4^{2-})$ . The  $\mathrm{Zn(OH)}_4^{2-}$  further breaks down to insoluble zinc oxide as the discharge product rendering liquid electrolyte supersaturated. The oxidation of anodic Zn also releases electrons by traveling through the external circuit to the air electrode during discharge. It is important to mention that the movement of  $\mathrm{Zn(OH)}_4^{2-}$  from the metallic terminal to the air electrode creates an increase in polarization and decrease in cyclic efficiency. This problem, however, is solved by means of a separator between the electrodes, the function of which is allowing the passage of hydroxide ions while blocking  $\mathrm{Zn(OH)}_4^{2-}$ . For the recharging process, the above electrochemical reactions are reversible, eventually leading to the deposition of Zn back at the metallic anode and oxygen being released at the electrode-electrolyte interface following the OER.

# 8.2.1. Air Cathode Electrocatalysts in Zn-Air Batteries.

It is important to emphasize that Zn-air batteries are still in the early developmental stages, primarily because of the sluggish OER/ORR kinetics, high overpotentials at the air cathode, and relatively low round trip efficiency.[595] Another part of their practical limitation is the cycling life, which is less than a quarter of that for LIBs. Therefore, it is urgent to develop highly efficient and stable catalysts that exhibit exceptional electrocatalytic activity in terms of stupendous electron conductivity, stability, and selectivity. Moreover, economical and environmental aspects are equally fundamental. In this regard, noble metals (Pt, Ag, Ru, Ir, and so on) have been extensively investigated as electrocatalysts for performing catalytic activities in Zn-air batteries. Nevertheless, challenges such as low stability, scarcity, and high costs still remain, hindering their practical entrance into the commercial markets.[596] Hence, the key considerations in designing and fabricating electrocatalysts include low costs, energy consumption, and carbon footprint. Herein, we briefly demonstrate each of the types and subtypes of electrocatalysts along with pertinent evidence from the up-to-date literature.

8.2.1.1. Metal-Based Electrocatalysts. Transition metal and pure carbon-based electrocatalysts have been widely invest

tigated as replacements for noble metal-based catalysts, principally owing to their various valence numbers, electrocatalytic activity, low-cost, stability, and environmental friendliness. Metal-based air-electrode electrocatalysts are broadly categorized into two genres: metallic compounds (metal oxides, etc.) and metal-C-N (M-C-N) materials.

Metallic compounds of oxides, sulfides, and hydroxides are counted as the most promising candidates for low-cost transition metal (Fe, Co, Mn, Cu, and Ni) compounds. Among oxides, single metal oxides,[597,598] spinel oxides,[599,600] and perovskite oxides[581,601] are prominent OER/ORR bifunctional catalytic materials. Regarding single metal oxides, Meng et al.[602] investigated  $\mathrm{MnO}_2$  based nanostructures, including  $\alpha$ -,  $\beta$ -,  $\delta$ -, and amorphous  $\mathrm{MnO}_2$ , in secondary Zn-air batteries and recorded their electrocatalytic performance in the order of  $\alpha$ - $\mathrm{MnO}_2 >$  amorphous  $\mathrm{MnO}_2 > \beta$ - $\mathrm{MnO}_2 > \delta$ - $\mathrm{MnO}_2$ . The  $\mathrm{CoO}_x$  family has also been under investigation for its high electrocatalytic OER/ORR performance and variable tendency to transfer electrons. To elucidate, Xie and co-workers[603] prepared a nanoscale ( $\sim 1$  nm)  $\mathrm{CoO}_x$  layered structure on Co/N codoped RGO (named Co/N-RGO), as shown in Figure 79a, to reveal that the integration of  $\mathrm{CoO}_x$  and Co/N-RGO augments the electron transfer rate while sparing active sites for enhancing conductivity. Further, quantitative evidence in the form of positive  $E_{1/2}$  of  $0.896\mathrm{~V}$  for ORR demonstrated its overall catalytic activity, yielding high power density up to  $120\mathrm{~mW}$ $\mathrm{cm}^{-2}$  when employed as an air cathode catalyst in the Zn-air battery as is evident in the power density curve (Figure 79b). Doping single metal oxides with another metal has also been proved to be a useful method for augmenting the catalytic activity of oxygen catalysts. For instance, the two-dimensional (2D) structure of NiO-NFs when doped with porous Co exhibited superior electronic state at the Fermi level and, therefore, is effective in increasing electronic conductivity and OER/ORR performance of pristine NiO-NFs.[604] In addition, the transition metal sulfides, which constitute a subcategory of chalcogenides, have also been greatly studied. The transition metal sulfides are known for their ability to host  $\mathrm{Zn}^{2+}$  owing to their variety of crystal structures and large interlayer spacing to store zinc ions. In this regard, vanadium sulfides ( $\mathrm{VS}_2$  nanosheets,  $\mathrm{VS}_4$ , and  $\mathrm{VS}_4@\mathrm{RGO}$ )[231,605] and molybdenum sulfides ( $\mathrm{Mo}_6\mathrm{S}_8$  and  $\mathrm{MoS}_2$ )[606,607] have been reported to exhibit remarkable ORR and OER performances as compared to individual sulfides,  $\mathrm{NiS}_2$  and  $\mathrm{CoS}_2$ .

The spinel oxides (single  $\mathrm{A}_3\mathrm{O}_4$  or binary  $\mathrm{A}_x\mathrm{B}_{3 - x}\mathrm{O}_4$ ) are acknowledged due to their multiple valence states of cations, providing donor-acceptor sites for facilitating OER and ORR kinetics through reversible oxygen adsorption. According to Wei et al.,[613] there are tetrahedral and octahedral sites in spinel oxide structure (Figure 79c). The octahedral sites play a pivotal role in the catalysis of spinel oxide because the  $\mathbf{e_g}$  orbital, directly facing the oxygen atom, overlaps with an O 2p orbital for octahedral coordination (Figure 79d). The ORR and OER activities of different spinel oxides as a function of  $\mathbf{e_g}$  occupancy of active

![](images/2743ad8dd129e27c78714dad34891741bc4a58d29242e835be89cb5f179f9add.jpg)

(d) Charge/discharge cycling performance of Zn-air battery based on NPMC-1000  $(0.5\mathrm{mgcm}^{-2}$  for ORR and  $1.5\mathrm{mgcm}^{-2}$  for OER) at a current density of  $2\mathrm{mAcm}^{-2}$ . element at octahedral sites are shown in Figure 79e,f. In these illustrations, the black circle represents cubic  $\mathrm{MnCo_2O_4}$  spinels with Mn as the active element. The red and orange circles represent the Mn of tetragonal spinels  $(\mathrm{Mn}_2\mathrm{Co}_1\mathrm{O}_4,$ $\mathrm{Mn}_{2.5}\mathrm{Co}_{0.5}\mathrm{O}_4$  ,and  $\mathrm{Mn}_3\mathrm{O}_4)$  and ferrite, respectively. The green "up" triangle characterizes the Mn of cubic  $\mathrm{Li_xMn_2O_4}$  spinels. In addition, the blue diamond relates to the Co of cubic spinels  $(\mathrm{Co}_3\mathrm{O}_4,$ $\mathrm{ZnCo_2O_4},$  and  $\mathrm{CoFe_2O_4})$  , while the pink square signifies the Ni of cubic spinels  $(\mathrm{NiCo_2O_4}$  and  $\mathrm{NiFe_2O_4})$  . It is clear that the spinel structures with Co and Ni as active element tend to positively stray from the expected trend (dotted volcano line) because of the strength of the metal-oxygen covalent bond at octahedral sites.

Perovskite oxides  $\left(\mathrm{ABO}_3\right)$  have  $\mathrm{BO}_6$  octahedral sites with  $\mathrm{A}^+$  cations in their structural framework, as shown in Figure 79g. The synthesis of perovskite oxides anchored on N-doped carbon materials has been an extensive practice for designing optimized bifunctional catalysts. Chen et al.[609] introduced a core-corona bifunctional catalyst (CCBC) (Figure 79h) composed of exceedingly OER-active  $\mathrm{LaNiO}_3$  supported on highly ORR-active N-doped CNTs. The DFT analysis for degradation testing has shown that the CCBC outperforms Pt/C catalyst by 3-13-fold. The charge/discharge polarization curves (Figure 79i) also exhibit superior performance of the CCBC as compared to the

typical  $\mathrm{Pt} / \mathrm{C}$  and  $\mathrm{LiNiO_3}$ . Furthermore, P-3G, which is composed of cation-ordered perovskite  $(\mathrm{PrBa}_{0.5}\mathrm{Sr}_{0.5})_{0.95}\mathrm{Co}_{1.5}\mathrm{Fe}_{0.5}\mathrm{O}_{5 + \delta}$  and 3D porous N-doped graphene, has also been examined as an active OER/ORR catalyst.[610]

Metal-nitrogen-doped carbon (M-N-C) based materials consisting of  $\mathrm{M - N_x}$  moieties, in particular  $\mathrm{Fe / Co - N_x - C}$  moieties, anchored on a C matrix have emerged as a promising family of bifunctional cathode catalysts in Zn-air battery systems $^{596}$  due to their low-cost and intrinsic catalytic performance, which is further ascribed to the interaction between metal and nitrogen inside the C matrix for regulating the local electronic configuration. Such interactions result in decreased adsorption energy of oxygenated intermediate species, eventually leading to exceptional bifunctional OER/ ORR activity. Among numerous recent studies, Xie et al. $^{611}$  synthesized single atom  $\mathrm{Fe - N_x}$  anchored on N and S containing ordered C layers (named S,N-Fe/N/C-CNT), as shown in Figure 79j. The resulting configuration revealed that the rich atomic dispersion of  $\mathrm{Fe - N_x}$  sites and their ordered structures facilitated quicker electron transfer and augmented the exposure of active sites. The S,N-Fe/N/C-CNT demonstrates excellent ORR and OER activities in terms of  $\Delta E$  (0.75 V) (Figure 79k) and a high power density of  $102.7~\mathrm{mW~cm}^{-2}$  (Figure 79l). In

addition to single metal  $\mathrm{M - N - C}$  catalysts, bimetallic 2D structured  $\mathrm{FeCo - N_x - CN}$  showed reversible ORR/OER with a low  $\Delta E$  (0.78 V), high power density of  $150\mathrm{mWcm}^{-2}$ , and cyclability up to  $40\mathrm{h}$  at  $5\mathrm{mA cm}^{-2}$  and  $10\mathrm{mA cm}^{-2}$ . [612] Overall, such a design of electrocatalysts is speculated to offer bright prospects for the development of advanced electrode materials for novel energy storage systems.

8.2.1.2. Metal-Free Electrocatalysts for Zn-Air Batteries. The pristine carbon materials are without any doubt the most extensively studied materials with regards to optimal design of air electrodes in MABs. Owing to their high specific surface area

and conductivity, such materials can serve to boost the electron transfer rate and mass diffusion of discharge/charge species in ORR/OER reactions.[615] Among them, CNTs, CB, RGO, and graphitic carbon nitrides are noteworthy. The doping of heteroatoms (N, S, P, or B) is considered as an extremely conducive strategy to customize the charge/spin redistribution in the carbon matrix for enhancing its catalytic activity. Generally, monoatom doping is highly effective in generating numerous activation sites in the carbon framework and lowering reduction energy.[616] In this regard, the redox catalytic activity of B-doped carbon nanomaterials holds precedence over their N-

doped counterparts due to their lower doping concentration. This preference is ascribed to the B atoms, due to their lower electronegativity than carbon, introducing charge carrier species and encouraging efficient chemisorption of oxygen atoms.[596]

Adding two heteroatoms in the carbon skeleton can also induce synergistic effects to aid electrocatalytic functions for Zn-air batteries. In this regard, studies have reported doping of carbon foams with some typical dual heteroatoms such N-B, N-P, and N-S. Recently, Dai and co-workers[617] have reported the ORR and OER catalytic activities of 3D carbon foams with N and P as co-dopants (NPMC). Figure 80a shows the preparation process of the NPMC, which involves the polymerization of aniline monomers followed by the pyrolysis of polyaniline (PANi) aerogel. As a result, the surface area of the NPMC catalysts was recorded to be as large as  $\sim 1663\mathrm{m}^2\mathrm{g}^{-1}$  (Figure 80b). In addition, the polarization curves (Figure 80c) of the Zn-air battery based on the NPMC catalyst showed excellent charge (OER) and discharge (ORR) performance, and NPMC-1000 exhibited stability in battery cycling with no voltage change even after 600 charge/discharge cycles ( $\sim 100\mathrm{h}$ ), as shown in Figure 80d. The suitable electronic structures, the OER/ORR activities, and the corresponding overpotentials were studied by carrying out first-principles calculations using density functional theory (DFT). It was found that, for OER, the most active site for N and P dopants is at the edge of graphene and the overall reaction was ascending at potential of  $0\mathrm{V}$ . However, when the battery potential increased to the threshold of 0.797 (equivalent to an overpotential of  $0.395\mathrm{V}$ ), the reaction directs downward along with the spontaneous occurrence of OER, as shown in Figure 80e. Similarly, the most active N-doped site is at different locations near the edge of graphene, the ORR pathway of which along with Gibbs free energy change is illustrated in Figure 80f. Furthermore, a similar study conducted by Dai et al.[590] has also revealed that a Zn-air battery system based on a N,P co-doped C sphere catalyst exhibited desirable electrochemical bifunctional OER/ORR catalytic activity, approaching that of commercial catalysts.

Apart from the single- and dual-doped carbon electrocatalysts, multidoping of elements in carbon materials has been exclusively investigated.[618] The studies include the synthesis of ternary (N, F, and P) doped carbon catalyst by using electrospinning and heat treatment methods,[619] N,P,S-doped carbon nanospheres by employing secondary pyrolysis and template etching,[620] and N,F,S-tridoped carbon fibers.[621] It is important to mention that although the N,P,S-doped carbon nanospheres exhibited ORR catalytic activity comparable to that of  $\mathrm{Pt / C}$ , their ORR activity was found to be poor. In addition, the catalytic benefits of tridoped carbon fibers are relatively poor as compared to the conventional  $\mathrm{Pt / C}$  and  $\mathrm{RuO_2}$  catalysts.[596]

To summarize, the metal-free carbon materials overall have shown excellent ORR activity along with other key considerations pertaining to cost, selectivity, stability, and so on. Nevertheless, such catalyst materials demonstrated a concrete downside of insufficient OER activity because of the induced defects in the carbon substrate due to the doping of heteroatoms.

8.2.1.3. Metal-Organic Framework Based Electrocatalysts. Metal-organic frameworks (MOFs) are known catalyst materials in secondary Zn-air batteries owing to their highly ordered crystalline structure, customized pore size, ultrahigh surface area, and flexibility to structural design. Such materials are made of metallic ions or agglomerates integrated with organic ligands, including zeolite imidazole frameworks (ZIFs).

In contrast to the traditional noble-metal catalysts, MOFs can offer the following potential advantages: (i) convenience of doping of highly dispersed heteroatoms; (ii) control over size, morphology, and crystalline structure of pristine MOFs; (iii) induced electrical conductivity through conductive ligands; and (iv) supermolecular properties. In general, MOF-based oxygen catalysts can be categorized into pristine MOFs and their derivatives.

Concerning pristine MOFs, 2D MOFs have emerged recently as a hot topic owing to their inherent porosity, considerable conductivity, and chemical stability.[622] Hitherto, most of the 2D MOFs have been synthesized from the coordination of metal ions, such as  $\mathrm{Cu}^{2+}$ ,  $\mathrm{Ni}^{2+}$ , or  $\mathrm{Co}^{2+}$ , with ligands derived from benzene or triphenylene with phenol  $(-OH)$ , imine  $(-NH_2)$ , or thiophenol  $(-SH)$  functional groups. For instance, Zhu et al.[623] pioneered a simple 2D oxide sacrifice approach to synthesize ultrathin FeCo MOF-74 nanosheets (named FeCo-MNS), which exhibited superior OER activity with low onset potential value of  $1.528\mathrm{V}$ . Similarly, Zhang et al.[624] proposed a new class of 2D MOF containing  $\pi$ -conjugated  $[\mathrm{Co}_3(\mathrm{HHTP})_2]_n$  nanosheets, which demonstrated ultrahigh mass activity (64.63 A mg $^{-1}$ , 1.7 V vs. RHE) when applied in OER, proving their bright prospects in Zn-air batteries.

In addition to 2D MOFs, 3D bifunctional electrocatalysts also attracted attention for their employability in aqueous  $\mathrm{Zn}$ -air batteries. Shinde et al.[625] synthesized a double-linked hexaiminobenzene MOF (Mn/Fe-HIB-MOF) and proved its enhanced bifunctional utility toward OER/ORR. Figure 81a-c show the sequential synthesis scheme of single-atom HIB-MOF, structural design of dual-atom HIB-MOF, and its SEM-recorded spherical morphology, respectively. Moreover, the electrochemical characterization shows that the zinc-air batteries based on Mn/Fe-HIB-MOF demonstrate superior battery performance, as evident from  $\Delta E = 0.627\mathrm{V}$ ,  $E_{1/2} = +0.883\mathrm{V}$  (ORR), low  $E_{10\mathrm{mA}\cdot \mathrm{cm}^{-2}} = 1.61\mathrm{V}$  (OER), power density measurement (Figure 81d) and excellent cycle life over 1000 h at  $10\mathrm{mA}\mathrm{cm}^{-2}$  (Figure 81e). Furthermore, the customization of 2D MOFs with other MOF nanoparticles for enhancing electrical conductivity and catalytic performance has also been practiced in  $\mathrm{Zn}$ -air systems.[626] Although conductive MOFs have made their name in their catalytic effectiveness for bifunctionality, these materials have some major downsides in terms of high costs of ligands and complexity of their fabrication. Therefore, the pristine MOFs are discouraged as catalysts in  $\mathrm{Zn}$ -air batteries. In contrast, the development of MOFs as self-sacrificing templates for building porous carbon enclosed in metal-metal compounds, is becoming a hot topic.

MOF derivatives based on porous metal to metal compound-based carbon materials have emerged as promising candidates to yield admirable bifunctional (ORR/OER) electrocatalytic activities. This new category of MOF derivatives may constitute either metal-nitrogen based carbon materials  $(\mathrm{M - N - C})$  or carbon-based metallic compounds. The  $\mathrm{M - N - C}$  materials (where M stands for Fe, Ni, Co, etc.) are made of species in the form of  $\mathrm{MN}_x$  inserted in a carbon matrix and are known for their increased ORR and OER activities. In addition to the single-atom based  $\mathrm{M - N - C}$  materials, transition metals also proved themselves to be good candidates for MOF-derived  $\mathrm{M - N - C}$  materials. The evidence has been reported for the competitive performance of structurally ordered rod-like Co nanoparticles inserted in N-doped C rings,[627] 3D sandwiched N-doped-C/Co nanoparticle/N-doped-C,[628]  $\mathrm{Co - N}_x / \mathrm{C}$  nanorod arrays made

![](images/17df04ef2add10cb3397dd91701c878fcbc09535dac6370102dc9a1b0a50e7bc.jpg)

![](images/ac1aaeeeaa3c0599035328b0e00b1b971a1c720a8a7553101202fd1362e89067.jpg)

![](images/f30c15a95cb6b71c52058aac5d3f3183184baa9fab411774c85edd610bd7bf2e.jpg)

![](images/fa83ab7c7b9504ec0d138e9673afe7ec580d334586adba89d0af7f9645cab7ea.jpg)

![](images/0fe7bd8abac7561682c4e8478fede787b9f93269fce6869f0ad8d893fbd49218.jpg)

![](images/ce2b501b3c52333e8bbe613983df04a5fe1128211897dc50e562190112d8567f.jpg)  
Figure 82. Advancement of electrolytes in Zn-air batteries. (a) Schematic of 3-phase reaction zone in solid-state and liquid electrolytes in Zn-air batteries. Reproduced with permission from ref 633. Copyright 2016 Wiley-VCH. (b) Preparation of PANa hydrogen electrolyte from monomer AA, neutralizer NaOH, initiator ammonium persulfate,  $\mathrm{Zn(CH_3COO)_2}$ , and KOH. (c) Cycling performance and corresponding CE of Zn-air battery at  $96\mathrm{C}$  rate showing superior performance of PANa as compared to PVA electrolyte. Reproduced with permission from ref 640. Copyright 2018 WileyVCH. (d) Illustration of ion selectivity penetrating across polymerized IL membrane electrolyte in Zn-air battery. Reproduced with permission from ref 644. Copyright 2016 American Chemical Society. (e) Schematic of  $\mathrm{Zn^{2+}}$  solvation sheath in WIS solutions. Reproduced with permission from ref 119. Copyright 2015 The American Association for the Advancement of Science. (f) Molecular dynamics simulated solvation structure of  $\mathrm{Zn^{2+}}$  in HCZE. (g) Cycling performance of Zn-air battery based on HCZE electrolyte at a current density of  $50\mathrm{mA g^{-1}}$  and a constant capacity of  $1000\mathrm{mAh g^{-1}}$ . Reproduced with permission from ref 642. Copyright 2018 Springer Nature.

![](images/2418e7f31ead2809753b969d4969fe319b8c97c1a8d360c18ea4b245f29a9f84.jpg)

by the 2-step pyrolysis of 3D ZIFs, $^{629}$  and the pyrolysis product of 2D  $\mathrm{Zn / Co}$  ZIF. $^{630}$

Recently, Huang and co-workers $^{631}$  adopted a bottom-up method to synthesize non-transition metal based MOF-derived hollow N-doped carbon spheres (HNCSs), the preparatory process for which is shown in Figure 81f. The starting MOF undergoes pyrolysis and a subsequent solvothermal process to obtain ZnO-ZIF-8 core-shell microparticles, which are then carbonized at  $1000^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  to obtain HNCSs. The SEM micrograph (Figure 81g) confirms the hollow structure of the HNCS particles, as evident from the contrast distribution over their curved surfaces. Upon electrochemical testing, the HNCS electrocatalyst delivers remarkable ORR activity  $(5.34\mathrm{mAcm}^{-2}$  current density), long-term stability (up to  $96.5\%$  capacity retention after  $10\mathrm{h}$ ), and superior reaction selectivity as compared to the known Pt/C electrocatalyst in alkaline medium. Similarly, Zheng et al. $^{632}$  introduced a novel Co-MOF,O-doped carbon electrocatalyst based on Zn,Co-doped glucosamine and ZIF-8 MOF for enhancing the performance of Zn-air batteries. Upon employment in Zn-air cells, the electrocatalyst shows a higher OCP up to  $1.38\mathrm{V}$  as compared to that of the Pt/C based air cathode  $(1.32\mathrm{V})$ , as shown in

Figure 81h. The power density is recorded to be  $\sim 144\mathrm{mW}$ $\mathrm{cm}^{-2}$ . In addition to the excellent specific capacity (671.6 mAh  $\mathrm{g_{Zn}}^{-1}$ ) at  $10\mathrm{mAcm}^{-2}$ , the battery shows remarkable cycling performance. As evident in Figure 81i, the final charge and discharge potentials are 1.81 and  $1.23\mathrm{V}$ , respectively, after 510 cycles, which are close to the initial charge and discharge potentials of 1.76 and  $1.30\mathrm{V}$ , surpassing the cycling performance of the  $\mathrm{Pt / C + RuO_2}$  based Zn-air batteries.

To summarize, in order to qualify as suitable electrocatalysts for ORR in  $\mathrm{Zn}$ -air batteries, it is pivotal for them to create more active sites for the nucleation of discharge product while keeping the enhanced rate of ORR/OER activity. It has been established that among all the selections, carbon-based materials are used to date in almost all the electrocatalysts, out of which metal-based catalysts stand out as competitive replacements as compared to the conventional noble or precious metal catalysts in  $\mathrm{Zn}$ -air batteries. In contrast, the precious metal catalysts are favored the least against all their counterparts owing to their overwhelming cost and no extraordinary performance.

8.2.2. Electrolyte Advancement in Zn-Air Batteries. Alkaline electrolytes, such as KOH, NaOH, and LiOH, are mostly employed in Zn-air batteries, exhibiting high metallic

corrosion resistance, low viscosity, good ionic conductivity, and oxygen electrocatalytic activity. $^{633}$  Among these, KOH is the most extensively studied owing to high solubility of  $\mathrm{Zn}$  salts in it, fast electrochemical kinetics, and ionic conductivity of  $\mathrm{K}^{+}$ . $^{634}$  In general, the use of alkaline solutions has been met with major challenges, which include the dissolution of zinc giving rise to undesired intermediate species (such as  $\mathrm{Zn(OH)}_4^{2-}$ ), anodic corrosion, and dendrite growth. In order to suppress the formation of  $\mathrm{Zn(OH)}_4^{2-}$  leading to  $\mathrm{Zn}$  dendrites, the addition of room temperature ionic liquids (RTILs), such as 1-ethyl-3-methylimidazolium dicyanamide, in alkaline solutions has been proven effective. $^{635}$  As for  $\mathrm{Zn}$  passivation acting as a diffusion barrier and hindering the diffusion of hydroxide ions, increasing the concentration (i.e., pH) of alkaline electrolytes and adding additives are among the primary mitigation strategies. $^{634}$

Realizing the limitation of alkaline solutions, the focus has gradually shifted toward aqueous neutral electrolytes for curbing the problems of electrolyte carbonation and dendrite formation. In this regard, Goh and co-workers[636] have reported the efficacy of  $\mathrm{ZnCl}_2$  and  $\mathrm{NH}_4\mathrm{Cl}$  as electrolytes in Zn-air batteries, yielding long cycle life, minimal dendrite growth, and nonexistent carbonate formation.

The problems mentioned pertaining to aqueous electrolytes led to studies pivoting toward nonaqueous electrolytes in the sphere of zinc-air batteries.[637] Concerning this, organic solvents can limit the shortcomings related to hydrogen evolution, dendrite growth, drying out of electrolyte due to water evaporation, and carbonation. In addition, these organic solvents have the ability to operate under higher temperature ranges and provide wide electrochemical windows. However, limited attention has been devoted to the applicability of organic solvents in metal-air (or Zn-air) batteries. Furthermore, solid-polymer electrolytes or thin film electrolytes represent another genre of nonaqueous electrolytes having massive potential to be employed in Zn-air batteries.[638] Their history pertaining to zinc-air batteries can be traced back to the 1990s as reported in ref 575. Figure 82a shows the schematic configuration of Zn-air batteries with solid-state polymer and liquid electrolytes. The polymer electrolytes have been acknowledged for their energy enhancement, high operational temperature ranges, good mechanical strength and toughness, fabrication convenience, low convection (ensuring low electrode corrosion and increased battery life), and low battery leakage. For instance, the ionic conductivities of poly(acrylic acid) (PAA), poly(vinyl alcohol) (PVA), and poly(4-vinylbenzenesulfonate-co-[3-(methacryloylamino) propyl] trimethylammonium chloride) (PAM) have recently been investigated pertaining to aqueous Zn-air batteries.[639] It has been revealed that when Zn-air batteries based on these polymers are tested for their charge/discharge cycles  $(0.5\mathrm{mAcm}^{-2})$ , PAA shows superior cycling stability. In addition, the efficiencies of KOH  $(6\mathrm{M})$ , PVA  $(6\mathrm{M})$ , PAA  $(6\mathrm{M})$ , and PAM have been recorded to be  $72\%$ ,  $70\%$ ,  $79\%$ , and  $66\%$ , respectively, proving the superior performance of PAA in Zn-air batteries. However, a major problem with the solid-state polymer electrolytes is their low ionic conductivities and lower solubility of Zn salts. Therefore, efforts are currently being devoted to rectifying this issue with novel methods, for example, by introducing co-polymers, coating electrodes with solid electrolyte, or in situ solidification of electrolytes inside the electrode structure, giving rise to hybrid solid-state batteries.

Gel-polymer electrolytes, which are made of a 3D polymeric skeleton containing a liquid electrolyte, expanding throughout its volume to facilitate rapid ion transport as in liquid

electrolytes, constitute another important alternative. The major advantage of these electrolytes is their high flexibility and the prevention of water loss. However, the gel-polymer electrolyte based MABs suffer from inadequate stability of the polymer frameworks, leading to the quick loss of electrolytic solvents resulting in short battery cycle life. Huang et al.[640] have recently reported a gel-polymer electrolyte based on sodium polyacrylate hydrogel (PANa) for flexible Zn-air battery systems (Figure 82b), and the electrochemical results showed relatively better cycling stability at  $2\mathrm{mA}\mathrm{cm}^{-2}$  for  $160\mathrm{h}$  as compared to the traditional PVA electrolyte. In addition, the aqueous Zn battery based on the PANa electrolyte has demonstrated  $65\%$  charge capacity retention even after 16000 charge/discharge cycles of the battery (Figure 82c), which is superior to the battery based on PVA.

Furthermore, nonaqueous RTILs, having melting temperatures equal to or below  $100^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , have gained substantial attention. In contrast to the aqueous electrolytes, nonaqueous RTILs are preferred owing to their low volatility, nonflammability, thermal stability, ionic composition, and inherent high degree of customization, allowing only desirable ions to pass through them (Figure 82d). Among these, the ones with imidazolium and pyrrolidinium as cations and dicyanamide and trifluoromethanesulfonylimidide as anions have been investigated.[641] The loss of water in aqueous electrolytes has also been addressed with WIS electrolytes, giving rise to intrinsically safer ZABs.[577] The WIS electrolytes hold precedence over their counterparts due to the stronger bonding of water molecules imparted by the ultrahigh concentration of salt. An extensively investigated example of such a solution is LiTFSI, in which water molecules take part in the solvation of metal ions, and the dearth of water molecules as compared to TFSI salt makes them difficult to decompose. According to Suo et al.,[119] upon increasing the LiTFSI concentration beyond  $20m$ , an average of two  $\mathrm{TFSI}^{-}$ are reserved for each  $\mathrm{Li^{+}}$  solvation sheath (Figure 82e). Similarly, Wang et al.[642] has introduced a highly concentrated  $\mathrm{Zn}$ -ion electrolyte (HCZE), which is composed of  $1m\mathrm{Zn(TFSI)}_2$  and  $20m$  LiTFSI (where  $m$  is molality ( $\mathrm{mol}\mathrm{kg}^{-1}$ )). The HCZE electrolyte not only ensures water retention in open surroundings but also promotes the stability and reversibility of the  $\mathrm{Zn}$  anode. Figure 82f illustrates the solvation structures of  $\mathrm{Zn}^{2+}$  in HCZE with  $1m\mathrm{Zn(TFSI)}_2$  and various concentrations (5, 10, and  $20m$ ) of LiTFSI. In addition, it can be proven from electrochemical testing that the  $\mathrm{Zn}$ -air battery based on the WIS electrolytes yields exceptional energy density ( $300\mathrm{Whkg}^{-1}$ ) for 200 cycles (Figure 82g). Furthermore, a low-cost  $\mathrm{ZnCl}_2$  based WIS electrolyte for dendrite-free reversibility of  $\mathrm{Zn}$  anode has also been reported.[643] With continual efforts toward the exploration of electrolytes for optimal performance in  $\mathrm{Zn}$ -air battery systems, considerable attention has been awarded to viable designs of electrode materials and catalysts in order to maximize the overall battery performance for GSES applications.

8.2.3. Architecture and Electrochemistry of Zn Anode. In contrast to the number of studies on electrocatalysts, only a handful of them have pivoted their attention to addressing the challenges faced by Zn electrodes. The problems offered by the Zn electrode have been reported to be comparably serious to those of the vastly investigated air electrode in Zn-air batteries. This is primarily because the latter exhibits much longer life cycle along with better degradation as compared to the former.[645] This justifies the urgent need to reorient attention

a

d

![](images/46ca0506fe489991e549684c8484f9f19c08ccfef78f2a82d9505da22573edb3.jpg)

C

![](images/f16339817e54074bc3e02321982c1c0da2c37a1d3588b59ad039171a21e4a24a.jpg)

![](images/88d364253ea4c918eb91ec3fe79902eb4f13c479ab16fc8f1e2a1b75967908b8.jpg)

Figure 83. Recent technologies in  $\mathrm{Zn}$  -air battery systems. (a) XRD pattern of air cathode after cycling in  $\mathrm{Zn(OTF)_2}$  electrolyte. (b) Schematic representation of  $2\mathrm{e}^{-}$  transfer mechanism in the vicinity of internal Helmholtz layer and outer Helmholtz layer in the presence of  $\mathrm{Zn(OTF)_2}$  electrolyte. Lower image shows the process involving desolvation and  $\mathrm{O}_2^-$  -containing pair formation. (c) Cycling performance of  $\mathrm{Zn}$  -air battery at a current density of  $0.1\mathrm{mA cm}^{-2}$ . Reproduced with permission from ref 593. Copyright 2021 The American Association for the Advancement of Science. (d) Schematic of symmetric all-solid-state  $\mathrm{Zn}$  -air cell based on CPS(101)||CBCs||patterned zinc||CBCs||CPS(101) configuration. (e) Diagram illustrating S and P active sites, participating in the trifunctional catalytic activity of CPS(101) on its surface. (f) Comparison of charge/ discharge voltage profiles of CPS(101),  $\mathrm{Pt / C + RuO_2}$  and commercial A201. Reproduced with permission from ref 658. Copyright 2021 Springer Nature. (g) Image showing SEI for conducting  $\mathrm{Zn^{2 + }}$  characterized by tiny nodular particles trapped in polymer framework. (i) Cycling performance of  $\mathrm{Zn||O_2}$  battery in  $4m\mathrm{Zn(OTF)}_2 + 0.5m$ $\mathrm{Me}_3\mathrm{EtNOTF}$  electrolyte showing SEI layer formation.
