![](images/0878895992817752ad3fb93a368cf9dfeabc8833a4d0a7dd6ad4f1fd4964bd84.jpg)  
h

![](images/7248b08dccce6cf6afa74330e6a1a908fd8733e5594bafa2ef81fc78dcb62f42.jpg)  
i

to addressing the dire problems pertaining to the Zn anode in Zn-air batteries.

The major problem of the Zn anode is its passivation, which results from the limited usage of Zn metal, especially in aqueous alkaline media, due to the limited transfer of soluble species to the anode and dissolved zinc into the electrolyte.[574] Such low consumption of anodic Zn accounts for the difference between actual energy density (220–300 Wh kg $^{-1}$ ) and theoretical energy density (1300 Wh kg $^{-1}$ ) of Zn-air batteries. The following are the key reactions occurring at the Zn electrode:[571,646]

$$
\mathrm {Z n} _ {\left(\mathrm {s}\right)} + 4 \mathrm {O H} ^ {-} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {Z n} (\mathrm {O H}) _ {4} ^ {2 -} _ {\left(\mathrm {a q}\right)} + 2 \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \tag {43}
$$

$$
\mathrm {Z n} (\mathrm {O H}) _ {4} ^ {2 -} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {Z n O} _ {\left(\mathrm {s}\right)} + 2 \mathrm {O H} ^ {-} + \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} \tag {44}
$$

Within the context of these reactions, passivation refers to the deposition of  $\mathrm{ZnO}$  particles, after supersaturation in the aqueous electrolyte, onto the  $\mathrm{Zn}$  electrode surface, forming an insulating layer and inhibiting further  $\mathrm{Zn}$  anodic reactions during the battery discharge.[647] Consequently, the discharge process ceases altogether, thereby curbing the  $\mathrm{Zn}$  electrode consumption and lowering the discharge capacity. In addition, the limited reversibility of the  $\mathrm{Zn}$  anode is directly related to the reduced rechargeability of  $\mathrm{Zn}-$  air batteries. According to Liu et al.,[648] high current density yields a compact  $\mathrm{ZnO}$  layer which results in the passivation of electrode. In contrast, lower values of current density lead to the formation of a porous  $\mathrm{ZnO}$  layer, which

instead of passivation allows enough mass transfer of hydroxide ions for anodizing purposes. Several mitigation strategies have been investigated for inhibiting the  $\mathrm{ZnO}$  passivation. The most commonly known solution comprises of altering the structural configuration of the  $\mathrm{Zn}$  anode to increase porosity, which provides high surface area for facilitating the accumulation and diffusion of discharge products.[646] In this regard, pulse electroplating has been employed for the development of  $\mathrm{Zn}$  anode with rational porosity and specific surface area for inhibiting the  $\mathrm{ZnO}$  passivation layer.[649] However, this method offers some potential downsides in terms of increased electrode resistance, dendrite growth, and corrosion rate, as well as decreased CE, which are further addressed with high concentration  $\mathrm{Zn}$ -ion electrolyte (HCZE), as reported.[642] Another simple strategy for suppressing the  $\mathrm{ZnO}$  passivation involves augmenting the concentration of aqueous solvents, so as to destabilize the loose  $\mathrm{ZnO}$  layer by increasing its solubility under high pH conditions.[650] However, this solubility of the  $\mathrm{ZnO}$  layer comes at the expense of  $\mathrm{Zn}$  corrosion.[646] Studies have also proved the efficacy of adding suitable surfactant, thanks to its amphiphilic nature, into the electrolyte to prevent deposition of the passive  $\mathrm{ZnO}$  layer.[650,651]

The formation of Zn dendrites owing to the uneven distribution of active species onto the anode surface is another key challenge and is greatly associated with the performance of Zn-air batteries.[652] Upon cell recharging, the Zn anode undergoes morphological alteration into a symmetric leaf-like structure, depending upon the current density. It is important to point out that this problem is responsible for creating further issues, such as short circuiting and shortened life of Zn-air battery.[653] To address this issue, a well-established and straightforward mitigation strategy is to use electrically insulating material as a separator, which is stable in aqueous medium and porous enough to allow substantial ionic conductivity. Specifically, a separator allows the migration of  $\mathrm{Zn}^{2+}$  species from the Zn anode to the oxygen cathode while blocking  $\mathrm{Zn(OH)}_4^{2-}$ . In this regard, polymeric separators, such as polyethylene, polyolefin, PVA, hydrated cellulose membrane, PAN, and polypropylene, are among the widely accepted choices.[654,655] Since separators alone do not necessarily stop dendrite formation, they are used in combination with the addition of inorganic or organic additives.[646] These additives serve the purpose of changing the electrodeposition behavior of the zinc anode by influencing its electrochemical features, like grain size, anticorrosion properties, and chemical composition. Hitherto, organic additives (EDTA, Tween 20 (polysorbate 20), and tartaric acid),[656] inorganic additives (CdO,  $\mathrm{Pb}_3\mathrm{O}_4$ , PbO,  $\mathrm{Bi}_2\mathrm{O}_3$ ,  $\mathrm{In}_2\mathrm{O}_3$ , and SnO),[657] and RTILs have been investigated as effective dendrite inhibitors.

8.2.4. Emerging Technologies for Zn-Air Batteries. The Zn-air energy storage systems have made substantial contributions to revolutionizing the next-generation energy storage devices. These are at present the most competitive alternatives for flexible and portable electronics, automobile, and LSES applications. Cutting-edge scientific studies on Zn-air technology have been conducted recently to further enhance the performance of such systems through novel chemistries and material designs. For instance, an attempt to improve the sluggish  $4\mathrm{e}^{-}\mathrm{O}_2$  chemistry and irreversibility in Zn-air batteries has been made by Sun and co-workers.[593] In their study, an unprecedented  $\mathrm{Zn - O_2 / ZnO_2}$  chemistry, facilitating  $2\mathrm{e}^{-}\mathrm{O}_2$  transfer in non-alkaline electrolyte, has been introduced by coating a  $\mathrm{Zn^{2 + }}$  ion-rich internal Helmholtz layer onto the air

cathode by means of hydrophobic trifluoromethanesulfonate anion species. In the presence of  $1\mathrm{mol}\mathrm{kg}^{-1}\mathrm{Zn(OTF)}_2$  as electrolyte and ambient air as cathode, fibers of  $\mathrm{ZnO}_2$  are formed as discharge products, which upon charging are completely diminished, revealing the excellent reversibility of  $\mathrm{ZnO}_2$  in  $\mathrm{Zn(OTF)}_2$  electrolyte (Figure 83a). With regards to the mechanism governing the formation of  $\mathrm{ZnO}_2$  during battery discharge, the molecular dynamics simulations attribute it to the strongly hydrophobic nature of trifluoromethanesulfonate anions, creating  $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ -poor and  $\mathrm{Zn}^{2+}$ -rich settings in the internal Helmholtz layer of the air cathode (Figure 83b). Such interfacial arrangement of most  $\mathrm{Zn}^{2+}$  (within  $6\AA$  from the cathode) and  $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$  molecules (away from the cathode) facilitates ORR with  $2\mathrm{e}^-$  transfer, while suppressing the  $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ -related reactions involving  $4\mathrm{e}^-$  transfer. In addition, the galvanostatic voltage profiles of  $\mathrm{Zn}$ -air cells at various current densities further confirm the excellent reversibility of  $\mathrm{ZnO}_2$  and ORR. At high current density, however, the appearance of high overpotential still calls for the use of powerful electrocatalysts for maintaining the EE of  $\mathrm{Zn}-\mathrm{O}_2$  batteries through  $2\mathrm{e}^-$  transfer. In addition, the cell exhibits exceptional cycling performance of approximately  $1600\mathrm{h}$  with charge and discharge cycles of  $10\mathrm{h}$  each while operating in ambient air, as demonstrated in Figure 83c.

Recently, a novel all-solid-state Zn-air pouch cell (ZPC), which has extended the performance limit of previously reported Zn-air batteries, was introduced by Shinde et al.[658] Such state-of-the-art battery yields high energy density  $(460\mathrm{Whkg_{cell}}^{-1})$  and long cycle life (6000 cycles at current density of  $25\mathrm{mA}$ $\mathrm{cm}^{-2}$ ). As is evident in Figure 83d, the structural components of the ZPC are (101)-faceted copper phosphosulfide [CPS(101)], patterned zinc metal, and antifreezing chitosan biocellulosics (CBC) acting as cathode, anode, and superionic electrolyte, respectively. These elements are arranged in a multilayered symmetric configuration [CPS(101)||CBC||patterned zinc||CBC||CPS(101)], which facilitates the formation of a vigorous solid electrolyte interface (SEI), which is ascribed to the interactions of negative species along the CBC,  $\mathrm{Zn^{2+}}$  ions, and the crystallographic direction of the CPS. The CPS(101) based cathode has been shown to exhibit superior trifunctional ORR/OER/HER catalytic activity (in alkaline  $0.1\mathrm{M}$  KOH solution) and outstanding reversible OER/ORR kinetics  $(\Delta V_{\mathrm{OER / ORR}} = 0.59\mathrm{V})$  as compared to  $\mathrm{Pt / C}$  and  $\mathrm{RuO_2}$ . The trifunctional redox activity of CPS(101) has been further revealed by DFT calculations, according to which the separating distance between S and P serves as an oxide receptor site for S to aid OER/ORR and as a proton receptor site for P to support HER, as illustrated in Figure 83e. When ZPCs based on CPS(101) and CBC were electrochemically tested, the longer charge/discharge plateaus, with minimal overpotential at high current density, as compared to commercial  $\mathrm{Pt / C + RuO_2}$  and A201 electrolytes, showed the excellent compatibility between CBC and CPS(101), because of less interfacial resistance over the battery cycling (Figure 83f). Moreover, high cell-level volumetric and specific energy densities of approximately  $1609 \pm 35\mathrm{WhL^{-1}}$  and  $523 \pm 15\mathrm{Whkg_{cell}}^{-1}$ , respectively, were recorded for such ZPCs. Moreover, their operating life was measured to be  $>2000$  and 350 charge/discharge cycles against  $20\%$  and  $70\%$  DoD, respectively. Above all, their ability to operate under extreme temperatures  $(-20$  to  $80^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$  continuously for 1500 cycles and at  $-20^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$  for 500 bending cycles  $(0^{\circ},90^{\circ},180^{\circ}$ , and  $0^{\circ}$ ) with maximal initial capacity retention implies the practical impact of this all-solid-state flexible ZPC design.

![](images/f9eed88e5db09ec3a1bace66feb42d1fdfa52fd7c5409cd4bd9bbdd48376b5b6.jpg)  
a

![](images/8e720e295bad020a76fa0676843aff66df756fcf4a3912139843a6f07d5be6c9.jpg)  
b

![](images/a1d6186ece305a16dd08442baf418ab3fd4413b1a614b78fd9f69cd4a80e83fb.jpg)  
C  
Figure 84. Applications of Zn-air batteries. (a) Comparison of Zinc8's fuel tank based Zn-air battery design (left) and conventional LIB stacks (right) (Courtesy of Zinc8, www.zinc8energy.com). (b) Capacity of the NantEnergy's Zn-air based battery pack for storing energy from solar PV panels. (c) Microgrid storage applications of Zn-air battery systems for a variety of utilizations.

The typical irreversibility of the Zn anode, originating from the dendrite growth and HER side reaction during Zn plating and stripping, in aqueous Zn cells is commonly considered to be the primary factor limiting the in situ formation of a SEI. To facilitate SEI formation for suppressing side reactions on Zn metal, Cao and co-workers[659] have recently pioneered the use of aqueous  $\mathrm{Zn(OTF)_2}$  electrolyte in conjunction with trimethylethylammonium trifluoromethanesulfonate (Me3EtNOTF) as an additive. This arrangement triggered the formation of a hydrophobic SEI based on fluorinated species (Figure 83g,h), which was able to avoid HER and curb Zn dendrite formation in different Zn cell chemistries, including a  $\mathrm{Zn - O_2}$  battery. Upon electrochemical cycling, the  $\mathrm{Zn - O_2}$  cell based on  $\mathrm{Zn(OTF)_2}$  electrolyte, Me3EtNOTF additive, the limited Zn anode, and CB coated carbon paper as air cathode yielded a life span of 300 cycles (Figure 83i) under a capacity up to  $1000\mathrm{mAhg^{-1}}$  (cathode based) and impressive energy density ( $325\mathrm{Whkg^{-1}}$ ) (cathode and anode based). In addition, the presence of  $\mathrm{Me}_3\mathrm{EtNOTF}$  in  $4m$  electrolyte ensured the reasonable reversibility of the Zn anode over its cycling, desirable acceleration of ORR, and lower overpotentials compared to pristine  $4m\mathrm{Zn(OTF)_2}$  electrolyte. However, the use of efficient bifunctional electrocatalysts is still advised for enhancing the round-trip efficiency of the  $\mathrm{Zn - O_2}$  battery.

8.2.5. Applications of Zn-Air Batteries. The primary Zn-air battery systems have been known to have replaced lead-acid and other alkaline and mercury batteries currently in use for small scale applications, such as hearing aids, military field electronics, and cell phones.[641] In contrast, the focus has now been shifted toward harnessing the long-lasting and high-energy

density applications of secondary Zn-air battery systems, for instance, for electrical vehicles (EVs). Pertaining to EVs, these batteries provide the best energy density to cost and energy density to weight ratios recorded to date. Another imperative benefit of Zn-air battery technology is the lowest safety concern owing to the use of aqueous or solid state electrolytes. Many firms have come forward in the last few decades and have upgraded the Zn-air battery technology. For example, Electric Fuel, a worldwide leading manufacturer of portable electronics, has achieved specific energy up to 200 Wh kg $^{-1}$  in a Zn-air battery, which is capable of powering a passenger bus continuously for 8 h in one charge with zero emissions. $^{562}$  Most recently, this technology has emerged to serve military applications owing to its ability to achieve energy capacity of 400 Wh kg $^{-1}$  and to operate under extreme temperatures. $^{660}$  Another German firm named Dreisbach Electromotive Inc. utilized Zn-air batteries to electrically power Chrysler minivans, which are equipped with 60 kWh Zn-air cells coupled with 10 kWh Ni-Cd cells to yield mileage of 322 km at speed of 64 km h $^{-1}$ .

In addition to EVs, ZPC assembly design has also been prominently used in the Internet of Things. In this regard, ZAF Energy Systems Inc. has patented a ZPC for tracking applications through terrestrial cellular networks, and such a battery design can offer shelf life of more than 100 days of continuous operation along with environment friendly landfill disposability.[661] Similarly, numerous studies have been dedicated to Zn-air batteries for their application in flexible and wearable electronics.[580,603] Notable are investigations conducted by Wang et al.[580] and Fu et al.,[662] utilizing naturally

inspired silk- and hair-derived air cathode electrocatalysts, respectively, for the fabrication of flexible Zn-air batteries. Such advancements have paved the way to more sustainable Zn-air batteries, generating less carbon footprint.

With regards to the LSES applications of Zn-air systems, substantial advancements have been made by a number of energy firms in providing reliable and cost-effective energy storage solutions to industrial and nonindustrial stakeholders. NantEnergy, for instance, is an active player in the energy marketplace known for providing reliable and cost-effective solid-state Zn-air battery based microgrid (both grid-tied and off-grid) solutions consolidated with renewable energy technologies, such as solar PV (Figure 84b). Besides its battery storage system claiming extended life of more than a decade, all of its key components are entirely recyclable. Such possibilities have paved the way to cheaper (less than  $\$100\mathrm{kWh}^{-1}$ ), cleaner (no reliance on fossil fuels), safer (no thermal runaway or chemical gassing), and more controllable (remote monitoring and data collection) energy storage prospects. It is important to point out that the electrode raw materials for the Zn-air battery cost  $85\%$  less than those for the Li-ion battery, which is attributed to the abundance of Zn metal in the Earth's crust. This huge price difference thus accounts for the overall lower manufacturing cost of Zn-air batteries, by  $\sim95\%$ , than that of LIB systems.

Similarly, a Canadian energy enterprise named Zinc8 has patented a Zn-air battery design that does not need battery stacking, as in the case of LIBs, for its scalability. On the contrary, to increase the capacity of the Zn-air battery, only the size of the tank (containing KOH electrolyte) for storing Zn fuel particles is increased (Figure 84a). This simple yet efficient design has been claimed to be conducive in bringing down its energy capacity expenditure to as low as \( \\( {45}\mathrm{{kWh}}{}^{-1} \) ,not to mention that this design is promising for yielding zero capacity fade over its extensive operating lifetime (approximately \( {2000}\mathrm{\;h} \) with \( 8\mathrm{\;h} \) of storage per cycle). Therefore, all such attributes strengthen the reliability of zinc-air cells from the microgrid energy storage perspective for domestic, commercial, and industrial utility (Figure 84c). Most recently, in 2020, the New York Power Authority in collaboration with Zinc8 is on its way to commercializing this novel and clean technology with a mission to decarbonize its power sector in the next decade. Likewise, the world renowned Eos energy storage systems, along with Con Edison and National Grid (British Power Company), has also been in the race for developing Zn-air batteries for LSES purposes.

It is only a matter of time until Zn-air batteries penetrate into energy marketplaces and would serve as the holy grail of energy storage owing to their inexpensive mass production. Although the LIB is currently the most employed technology, the reserves of Li are rapidly declining and its price is soaring. Therefore, Zn-air technology has been speculated to fill in as one of the most feasible, economical, and environmentally friendly GSES solutions for balancing the demand and supply of power grid electricity and for evading high peak time costs.

# 8.3. Other Aqueous MABs

8.3.1. Al-Air Batteries. As compared to the theoretical energy density of  $1.3\mathrm{kWhkg}^{-1}$  of Zn-air batteries, Al-air systems are known to demonstrate excellent theoretical energy density up to  $8.1\mathrm{kWhkg}^{-1}$ , which is lower than that of Li-air batteries  $(11.4\mathrm{kWhkg}^{-1})$ .663 Al-air batteries are also acknowledged for their cost-effectiveness, abundance of Al

metal, light weight, nontoxicity, recyclability, and lower electrochemical equivalence (2.98 Ah g $^{-1}$ ) as compared to other metals, such as Li (3.86 Ah g $^{-1}$ ). Moreover, the maximum OCP of Al metal,  $-1.87$  V vs. SHE at pH of 14.6, is not subject to alteration even in the absence of parasitic reactions.[64] The electrochemical reactions that occur in Al-air batteries are described in the following:[64]

Anode:

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathrm {A l} (\mathrm {O H}) _ {3 (\mathrm {s})} + 3 \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \rightarrow \mathrm {A l} _ {(\mathrm {s})} + 3 \mathrm {O H} ^ {-} _ {(\mathrm {a q})} \\ E ^ {\circ} = - 2. 3 1 \mathrm {V} \tag {45} \\ \end{array}
$$

Cathode:

$$
\mathrm {O} _ {2 (\mathrm {g})} + 2 \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} _ {(1)} + 4 \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \rightarrow 4 \mathrm {O H} _ {\text {(a q)}} ^ {-} \quad E ^ {\circ} = 0. 4 \mathrm {V} \tag {46}
$$

Overall:

$$
\begin{array}{l} 4 \mathrm {A l} _ {\left(\mathrm {s}\right)} + 3 \mathrm {O} _ {2 (\mathrm {g})} + 6 \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} _ {\left(\mathrm {l}\right)} \rightarrow 4 \mathrm {A l} (\mathrm {O H}) _ {3 (\mathrm {s})} \\ E ^ {\circ} = 2. 7 1 \mathrm {V} \tag {47} \\ \end{array}
$$

It is evident that the theoretical voltage of Al-air batteries is  $2.71\mathrm{V}$ . However, Al-air batteries typically operate within the range of  $1 - 2\mathrm{V}$ , which is ascribed to (i) the oxide layer, which covers the Al-anode surface and augments the internal resistance, delaying the steady-state voltage and resulting in the supersaturation of electrolyte with  $\mathrm{Al(OH)_3}$  precipitates, and (ii) parasitic corrosion reaction on the Al-anode, hampering the consumption of Al metal and forming  $\mathrm{H}_2$ .[64] In addition to corrosive reactions in aqueous solutions both in open circuit and in discharge state, other problems pertaining to Al-air batteries include accumulation of  $\mathrm{Al(OH)_3}$  and  $\mathrm{Al_2O_3}$  at both anode and cathode, complex intercalation chemistry of  $\mathrm{Al}^{3+}$ , resulting in irreversibility of hydrated alumina, abrupt decline in charge capacity, and poor shelf life. Numerous studies[65,66] have been carried out to avoid the charge capacity loss in the Al-air batteries and enhance the CE.

Regarding cathode catalyst materials, the precious metals, such as Pt-based electrocatalysts, are highly active ORR candidates owing to their advantages related to the formation of  $\mathrm{OH}^-$  ions in the aqueous solutions. These materials also offer potential drawbacks such as high costs and poor durability. In addition to Pt, other metals such as Pd, Au, and Ag have also been reported for their good ORR catalytic activity.[667] Particularly, the Ag-based electrocatalysts are almost 50 times cheaper than the Pt-based electrocatalysts. It has been found that only  $5 - 15\%$  concentration of Ag-based catalysts is sufficient for catalytic activity at the air cathode.[668] Therefore, the Ag based materials serve as effective ORR catalysts owing to their high electrical conductivity  $(6.3\times 10^{7}\mathrm{S}\mathrm{m}^{-1})$  and superb electrochemical stability.[669] The redox activity of Ag-based electrocatalysts in Al-air batteries can be further enhanced by alloying elements,[670] coupling them with supporting materials,[671] and morphology control.[672]

Among nonprecious metals,  $\mathrm{M - N - C}$  based electrocatalysts, similar to the Zn-air battery systems, have proved to be efficient candidates for outperforming the Pt-based catalysts in Al-air batteries. Among numerous members of the M-N-C family, macrocyclic  $\mathrm{M - N}_x$  based complexes are acknowledged for embodying immense potential to emerge as the next-generation of ORR catalysts. Such materials yield exceptionally high ORR with negligible overpotentials.[673] However, in order to preserve

![](images/af2b318f9d0c5c139de07792e8f3cc3f35fe92865b4841e6bc4ca94c8d2b397f.jpg)

![](images/97fadb293c66cb0d2b9a3ae6eeb0b87e4c990be390e58eacb2c636dbc42eb6bb.jpg)

![](images/e63b4373693b2b7057f03e73b99f36430267ac34ef5ef3184f9089192fbd92a5.jpg)

![](images/940c7c9c90b7c7aca0866acbb76db604a4c38fa3c6b1f306eb48c66ecb1ee438.jpg)

![](images/8fd7565af565af1fa5b7bff5e10ac59971280cdb5a2229e0bee620f6c1052522.jpg)

![](images/9e74ff4bf8cf6f12d8ac59e113a9085256581e52b22e795ddc9046592cd09da9.jpg)

![](images/51f4c9fa0f81b5b1ec2893d5f989544e58dc1313f2a6bda38fb13b2f9447989f.jpg)

![](images/b6bb47c509d73e6fd2ff0af7092bbda049e6923c782bf6d0dbbc00c7ddffa91a.jpg)  
Figure 85. Electrochemical performance of Al-air and Mg-air batteries using different cathode electrocatalysts and electrolytes. (a) Preparation process of P-CD/G nanocomposites by means of hydrothermal annealing process. (b) Demonstration of ORR on the P-CD/G based nanocomposite electrocatalysts in Al-air battery. (c) TEM micrograph of P-CD/G nanocomposite. (d) CV curves showing OER/ORR performance of P-CD/G based electrocatalyst in  $\mathrm{O}_2$ -saturated and  $\mathrm{N}_2$ -saturated  $0.1\mathrm{M}$  KOH solution. (e) Polarization and power density curve showing Al-air battery performance employing P-CD/G catalyst. Reproduced with permission from ref 675. Copyright 2019 Wiley-VCH. (f) Schematic representation of the preparation process of  $\mathrm{Fe - N_x}$  anchored on OM-NCNF- $\mathrm{FeN}_{x}$ . (g) ORR mechanistic diagram of OM-NCNF- $\mathrm{FeN}_{x}$  in KOH and Dulbecco's phosphate-buffered saline (DPBS) electrolytes. (h) TEM micrograph of the interconnected porous structure of OM-NCNF- $\mathrm{FeN}_{x}$  nanofibers. (i) ORR polarization curve showing superior catalytic performance of the OM-NCNF- $\mathrm{FeN}_{x}$  as compared to its counterparts in  $0.1\mathrm{M}$  KOH electrolyte and (j) discharge and power density curve of  $\mathrm{Mg}$ -air battery with different NCNF- and Pt/C-based air cathodes in DPBS buffer solution. Reprinted from ref 702. Copyright 2018 Wiley-VCH.

![](images/53a8d5ab9eda065ff81f16a5de90b8b8cc3e350ddb650303e54d55e08195ba88.jpg)

![](images/8d2771b78aa5290a41a09e46c4e2cd69031fd42f411d74f326b1842a49c56c9c.jpg)

the structural integrity of  $\mathrm{M - N_x}$  sites at high temperatures, Jin et al. [674] proposed a  $\mathrm{SiO_2}$ -protective-layer-assisted method to synthesize  $\mathrm{Fe - N_x / C}$  electrocatalyst and tested its ORR performance in alkaline media (0.1 M KOH), showing its comparable ORR performance to that of  $\mathrm{Pt / C}$ . Among others, most recently, Lai et al. [675] have reported an electrocatalyst based on P-doped carbon dots rooted in graphene (P-CD/G), prepared by hydrothermal and annealing methods (Figure 85a), for enhanced ORR performance (Figure 85b) exclusively in a flexible solid-state Al-air battery. It has been revealed that the annealing temperature is very critical for gauging maximum control of P as dopant in P-CD/G (Figure 85c). This catalyst containing 2.31 atomic percent of P, when annealed at  $900^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , exhibits improved ORR activity as compared to those annealed at 800 and  $1000^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . As is evident from Figure 85d, both current density and ORR potential for P-CD/G improve significantly as compared to its as-treated GO counterpart, attributed to the numerous ORR active sites generated by the addition of P as dopant and the formation of C dots. Moreover, the same electrocatalyst yields a power density up to  $157.3\mathrm{mWcm}^{-2}$ , which is comparable to or slightly better than that recorded for a

similar  $\mathrm{Pt / C}$  based Al-air battery  $(151.5\mathrm{mWcm}^{-2})$  , as shown in Figure 85e.

With regards to the advancements in Al anode, pure Al corrodes rapidly in aqueous electrolytes and yields hydrogen through a rigorous chemical reaction. The HER at the anode-electrolyte interface causes severe self-discharge problems during Al-air battery operation.[676] Many studies have been conducted to address such issues by investigating various Al-alloys and introducing additives into the electrolytes for inhibiting corrosion. With regards to the former approach, elements like Ga, Ti, Sn, and In have been considered viable alternatives for synthesizing different compositions of Al-alloys for aqueous Al-air batteries.[677,678] In particular, Zn has been widely used for considerably curbing the anodic corrosion by increasing the HER potential and for augmenting the electrochemical performance.[677] Moreover, other alloys such as Al-1Mg-0.1Ga-0.1Sn (wt %), Al-1Mg-1Zn-0.1Ga-0.1Sn (wt %),[679] and Al-0.15Bi-0.15Pb-0.035Ga (wt %)[678] have also been studied for their efficacy in suppressing HER and increasing the overall performance of the Al-air battery. With regards to the alkaline electrolytes, some inorganic additives, such as ZnO,  $\mathrm{In(OH)_3}$  and  $\mathrm{Na_2SnO_3}$  have been widely studied for preventing the

corrosion of Al anodes. $^{680}$  Further, hybrid structures like carboxymethyl cellulose/ZnO, L-cysteine/ZnO, and cetyltrimethylammonium bromide/ZnO have also been proven to be efficient. $^{681}$  Plant extracts have also been investigated and are promising choices as environmentally friendly additives for reducing the corrosive degradation of Al and suppressing the side reactions between Al anode and the electrolytes. $^{682}$

In addition to the issues related to corrosion, electrolyte leakage and performance losses during battery cycling constitute the intrinsic challenges in Al-air battery systems. Although aqueous acidic and neutral electrolytes have been studied for Al-air batteries, various molar concentrations of  $\mathrm{NaOH}$  and KOH are among the most commonly used strategies in alkaline electrolytes.[683-685] These hydroxides are harnessed widely owing to their high conductivity, active ORR, nontoxicity, and low overpotential. In addition to the aqueous electrolytes, ILs provide bright prospects in minimizing the corrosion of Al anodes in Al-air batteries. Aprotic ILs have also been reported to fabricate secondary Al-air batteries.[686] For instance, a study conducted by Gelman et al.[687] scrutinized a hydrophilic 1-ethyl-3-methylimidazolium oligo-fluoro-hydrogenate based IL, owing to its high ionic conductivity  $(100\mathrm{mScm}^{-1})$  and stability in air and water environments, in Al-air batteries. Upon electrochemical discharging, the energy density  $(2300\mathrm{Whkg}^{-1})$  of the Al-air cell based on this electrolyte and Al4N7 anode has been recorded to be superior as compared to similar cells studied previously. Gels and solid electrolytes are known corrosion inhibitors. In this regard, PVA, PAA, and the variants of these two have been studied. For instance, Wu et al.[688] synthesized an alkaline PAA/PVA based polymer electrolyte to apply in an Al-air battery. Zhang et al.[689] used a similar strategy to alter the acrylic acid based alkaline gel electrolyte to obtain the composition of  $9\mathrm{M}$  KOH  $+6\%$  PAAgel  $+\mathrm{ZnO} + \mathrm{K}_2\mathrm{S}_2\mathrm{O}_8,$  and upon electrochemical testing in Al-air batteries, it achieved power density and high current density of  $91.13\mathrm{mWcm}^{-2}$  and  $120\mathrm{mA cm}^{-2}$ , respectively. Furthermore, semisolid state electrolytes, such as KOH  $+\mathrm{PVA} + \mathrm{PEO} + \mathrm{ZnO} + \mathrm{Na}_2\mathrm{SnO}_3,$  have also been reported to manufacture flexible Al-air batteries.[690]

8.3.2. Mg-Air Batteries. Mg-air batteries are considered to be an efficient alternative to Li-based MABs owing to their environmental friendliness, cost-effectiveness, and safety. Further, the nontoxicity of Mg anodes is greatly acknowledged for biocompatible and biodegradable power applications.[686] Unlike other MAB systems, Mg-air cells have not been studied as much. Regardless, such batteries are known to have theoretical energy density up to  $6.8\mathrm{kWhkg}^{-1}$ . Following are the reactions occurring in a typical Mg-air battery:

Anode:

$$
\mathrm {M g} (\mathrm {O H}) _ {2} + 2 \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \rightarrow \mathrm {M g} + 2 \mathrm {O H} ^ {-} \quad E ^ {\circ} = - 2. 6 9 \mathrm {V} \tag {48}
$$

Cathode:

$$
\mathrm {O} _ {2} + 2 \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} + 4 \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \rightarrow 4 \mathrm {O H} ^ {-} \quad E ^ {\circ} = 0. 4 \mathrm {V} \tag {49}
$$

Overall:

$$
2 \mathrm {M g} + \mathrm {O} _ {2} + 2 \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} \rightarrow 2 \mathrm {M g} (\mathrm {O H}) _ {2} \quad E ^ {\circ} = 3. 0 9 \mathrm {V} \tag {50}
$$

From these reactions, the theoretical voltage of  $\mathrm{Mg}$  -air battery is  $\sim 3.1\mathrm{V}$ . During the battery discharge,  $\mathrm{Mg}$  is oxidized to form  $\mathrm{Mg}^{2 + }$  at the anode, whereas  $\mathrm{O}_2$  passes at the cathode to

form  $\mathrm{OH}^-$ . In spite of the high voltage and energy density of  $\mathrm{Mg}$ -air cells, there are some limitations hampering their widespread applications. Such issues include (i)  $\mathrm{Mg}$  anode self-corrosion in alkaline electrolytes, detrimentally affecting the cell's CE, (ii) high polarization due to the accumulation of discharge product  $(\mathrm{Mg(OH)}_2)$  on the anode surface, and (iii) thermodynamic barrier of converting  $\mathrm{Mg}$  oxides back into  $\mathrm{Mg}$ , thereby limiting its rechargeability.[68] These issues have been addressed with several mitigation strategies, including alloying the  $\mathrm{Mg}$  anode, nanostructured anode materials, corrosion resistant inhibitors in electrolytes, and electrocatalysts over cathode reactions.

Alloying pristine Mg anodes with metallic elements (Al, Mn, Zn, Sn, Ce, Y, and In) has proven effective for reducing the corrosive propensity of the Mg anodes in Mg-air batteries.[691] Some compositions of Mg alloys also facilitate the effective utilization of anode, by bringing about structural transitions in the discharge product, thereby enhancing the overall efficiency of the battery. For instance, Zheng et al.[692] employed a low-cost orthogonal structured Mg-Al-Sn-Mn anode and revealed its superior discharge capability  $(-1.602\mathrm{V}$  vs. SCE) with minimal corrosion resistance. Similarly, Wang et al.[693] reported a Mg-Al-Pb-Ce-Y alloying composition that exhibited improved anodic performance in a Mg-air battery in  $3.5\mathrm{wt}\%$  NaCl solution. In addition to the Mg-Al based alloys, recently, Mg-Li based alloys are also under investigation for their efficient anodic performances in Mg-air batteries, attributed to their high faradaic capacity, specific energy, and negative standard potential. To demonstrate, Mg-14Li-1Al-0.1Ce has shown superior electrochemical performance in terms of specific capacity of  $2076\mathrm{mAhg}^{-1}$  at an operating voltage of  $1.272\mathrm{V}$ , as compared to the pristine Mg and AZ31 anodes in Mg-air batteries.[694] Although Mg alloys have been widely investigated, their utilization leads to lower operating potentials as compared to the theoretical values. Therefore, much of the current effort has been pivoted to altering the chemical composition, microstructure and morphology of the Mg anode. For example, highly pure Mg  $(99.99\%)$ , which is free from impurities (Fe, Co, or Ni) normally present in commercial Mg, shows better electrochemical performance as the anode than even some of the Mg alloys investigated to date.[695] In a similar fashion, Zhao et al.[696] studied the impact of surface texture on the AZ31 alloy-based anode by employing rolling and cross-sectional surfaces. Upon electrochemical testing, the results revealed that the resulting cross-sectional surface is more corrosion resistant and electrochemically efficient as compared to its former counterpart.

In addition to the extensive scrutiny of  $\mathrm{Mg}$  based anode material, the electrochemical performance of a  $\mathrm{Mg}$ -air battery is equally dependent on the air cathode. The catalytic layer in air cathode drives the ORR during battery operation, and an ideal electrocatalyst is ought to exhibit rapid ORR kinetics, so as to increase the CE by lowering the overpotential of the MAB. Among many studied ORR catalytic alternatives for  $\mathrm{Mg}$ -air batteries, Pt-based precious metals have been highly investigated due to their superior catalytic activity. For instance,  $2 - 4\mathrm{nm}$  sized Pt particles anchored on carbon have been established as state-of-the-art ORR electrocatalysts.[697] However, their high cost and low abundance present major concerns; therefore, the emphasis has been laid upon exploring cost-effective Pt-based catalysts and non-precious metal based electrocatalysts. In relation to the cheap Pt-based catalyst, alloying Pt with transition metal (Fe, Co, or Ni) provides an important gateway

to economically enhance ORR catalytic activity. The ORR performance of Pt alloys is strongly determined by the low adsorption or binding energy of  $\mathrm{OH^{*}}$  and  $\mathrm{O^{*}}$  species onto their surface.[691] Based on this mechanism,  $\mathrm{Pt}_3\mathrm{Y}$ ,[698]  $\mathrm{PtCo} / \mathrm{C}^{691}$  and intermetallic  $\mathrm{Pt}_3\mathrm{Co}^{698}$  nanoparticles have been studied to reveal tremendous ORR kinetics. In addition to the Pt-based electrocatalysts, other noble metals (Pd, Cu, or Ag) and their alloys have attracted considerable attention for their enhanced ORR performance in aqueous  $\mathrm{Mg}$ -air batteries.[574]

Carbon- and transition metal-based electrocatalysts have also achieved well-deserved consideration in relation to  $\mathrm{Mg}$  -air batteries. Pristine carbon forms are not known as active ORR materials, but heteroatom dopants (P or N) can significantly improve the ORR catalytic activity of carbon structures by imparting high surface areas. Hitherto, numerous variants of mesoporous carbons,[699] CNTs,[700] and 2D graphene[701] have been proven to perform exceptionally well as air cathode electrocatalysts. Recently, Cheng et al.[702] have synthesized atomic  $\mathrm{Fe - N_x}$  (M-N-C family) supported on open mesoporous N-doped CNFs (denoted by OM-NCNF-  $\mathrm{FeN}_x$ ) for enhancing the ORR activity for flexible and biocompatible  $\mathrm{Mg}$  -air electrochemical systems. The schematic illustrations of orderly synthesis of OM-NCNF-  $\mathrm{FeN}_x$ , its TEM micrograph, and corresponding ORR activity in alkaline solution are shown in Figure 85f-h, respectively. It is evident from the TEM micrograph that the OM-NCNF-  $\mathrm{FeN}_x$  embodies plenty of irregular yet interconnected open mesopores, which contribute to the enhanced catalytic activity of the material. Furthermore, it can be seen from Figure 85i that the OM-NCNF-  $\mathrm{FeN}_x$  experiences superior ORR dynamics, as evident from its onset potential  $(E_0)$  of  $0.905\mathrm{V}$  and half-wave potential  $(E_{1 / 2})$  of 0.836 V. Moreover, with the OCP of  $\sim 1.77\mathrm{V}$  for the OM-NCNF-  $\mathrm{FeN}_x$  based  $\mathrm{Mg}$  -air battery, the highest current density and the power density have been recorded to be  $19.2\mathrm{mAcm}^{-2}$  and  $5\mathrm{mWcm}^{-2}$ , respectively (Figure 85j).

The selection of electrolyte in the  $\mathrm{Mg}$ -air battery is imperative and can be judged by changing its  $\mathsf{pH}$  value and concentration and adding suitable additives or inhibitors. To prevent the corrosion of  $\mathrm{Mg}$  anode in alkaline solutions, some researchers have proved the effectiveness of ILs and solid-state electrolytes. With regards to the former, Forsyth and coworkers $^{703}$  have confirmed supreme corrosion resistance of  $\mathrm{Mg}$  in ILs, such as  $\left[\mathrm{P}_{6,6,6,14}\right]\left[\mathrm{TFSI}\right]$  and  $\left[\mathrm{P}_{6,6,6,14}\right]\left[\mathrm{DPP}\right]$ ; however these electrolytes are not compatible for  $\mathrm{Mg}$ -air batteries due to the passivation of films deposited on to the surface of the  $\mathrm{Mg}$  anode. This limitation has led to further investigation of phosphonium chloride (denoted by  $\left[\mathrm{P}_{6,6,6,14}\right]\left[\mathrm{Cl}\right]$ ) in water to achieve an amorphous gel electrolyte. Such an electrolyte not only exhibits sufficient ionic conductivity to yield a current density of  $0.05\mathrm{mAcm}^{-2}$  but also provides anode passivation at OCV of the  $\mathrm{Mg}$ -air battery. $^{704}$  Concerning solid-state  $\mathrm{Mg}$ -air batteries, an important study conducted by Jia et al. $^{705}$  has pioneered synthesizing an IL-biopolymer electrolyte named chitosan-choline nitrate thin film electrolyte for miniaturized biocompatible  $\mathrm{Mg}$ -air batteries. The electrochemical testing of the fabricated solid-state battery yielded an OCV of  $1.80\mathrm{V}$  and output power of  $118\mu\mathrm{W}$ , which is enough to power implantable biomedical devices, such as biomitors or cardiac pacemakers.

# 8.4. Summary and Future Perspectives for MABs

This section manifests itself as tip of the iceberg of the aqueous MABs. Nevertheless, it reflects the most recent and significant advancements related to electrolyte designs and cathodic and

anodic modifications by utilizing different electrochemical materials. Specifically, among different air cathode catalysts in  $\mathrm{Zn}$ -air batteries, the bifunctional transition metal oxides anchored on various carbon forms have yielded competitive electrochemical performances (up to  $\sim 11000\mathrm{mAhg^{-1}}$ ), regardless of their economical aspects and practicality for commercial applications. The big picture along with the design configuration of novel flexible zinc-air batteries is summarized, along with key considerations in further pursuit of related advancements. It can be seen that although ample advancements have been made pertaining to harnessing the full potential of aqueous MAB systems, there is not an iota of doubt that there is still long way to go for such battery systems to realize their summit. The lithium-air (nonaqueous) batteries show much higher energy densities than their zinc-air counterparts, yet the latter are likely to offer bright prospects owing to the low cost and abundance of  $\mathrm{Zn}$ , a concern much more important than high efficiency and energy densities for LSES applications.[576] That is probably why primary zinc-air batteries were commercialized long time ago (since 1932) in medical and telecommunications, while the commercial bulk production of secondary zinc-air batteries initiated in 2012 with a limited energy density ( $\sim 35$  Wh kg $^{-1}$  as of 2017).

In LSES applications, the initial capital, operating expenditures, and maintenance costs of Zn-air batteries are equally vital considerations in addition to high energy density, efficiency, and battery cycles. As the metal-air technology is progressing, studies are being carried out from the perspective of its recyclability and environmental friendliness. For instance, Zn-air batteries manufactured and installed by NantEnergy have resulted in reducing 10000 tons of  $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$  that would be produced by the otherwise consumption of 4 million liters of diesel. In addition, with the abundance of natural reserves of zinc, hence its low cost, almost  $100\%$  recyclability of the wasted zinc battery has rendered this technology further cost-effective and practical from the grid-storage standpoint. The aqueous electrolytes are of massive importance in aqueous MABs due to their inherent advantages such as high-power output, immense ionic conductivity, and utmost safety. The recent advent of RTILs is also promising because of not only being able to eliminate Zn anode dendrite formation but also maintaining substantial ionic conductivity to enable smooth battery cycling operations. Furthermore, the single cell assembly of aqueous batteries needs further optimization. Although the closer spacing between the cathodes in aqueous MAB assembly is the key to maximum power density/volume ratio, the oxygen availability and transmission is hindered, resulting in anoxia, which may further lead to oxygen transfer polarization. Therefore, the assembly and stacks of metal-air aqueous batteries lack sufficient clarity in design. Referring to the designs of the developed assembly of battery stacks, such as fuel cells, one of the effective alternatives is to benchmark optimal design of the aqueous MABs for large-scale stationary applications.

It is evident that most of the research studies have revolved around the exploration of electrocatalysts for enhancing the OER/ORR redox activities in MAB systems. However, the low rechargeability has been mostly overlooked, which is fundamental in securing the future of secondary MABs. Therefore, special attention must be paid to ensure high RTE and CE, for reversibility improvement and side reaction suppression, to render them more energy conservative and economical. The agreement on this aspect of MABs can serve as a major leap toward the advancement of MAB systems. One mitigation

![](images/458b075e51b35678e58f37878086452e419631268a118b2ef14304dd5fe0b6a9.jpg)  
Figure 86. Schematic of HGBs with different bifunctional catalysts and cathode materials. Reproduced with permission from refs 711 (Copyright 2019 Elsevier), ref 713 (Copyright 2019 Elsevier), ref 716 (Copyright 2017 American Chemical Society), ref 56 (Copyright 2018 National Academy of Sciences), ref 718 (Copyright 2020 American Chemical Society), ref 720 (Copyright 2018 American Chemical Society), and ref 723 (Copyright 2018 Elsevier).

strategy to solve the reversibility conundrum is to integrate the scrutiny of cathode electrocatalysts and anode architecture altogether, rather than investigating individual electrodes and their accompanying reactions. In other words, the consolidated understanding of simultaneous electrochemistry of anode and cathode reactions is as important as the investigation of new cathodes, anodes, electrolytes, additives, separators, and overall cell designs. Given the reported advantages of aqueous metal-air systems, significant attention is still needed in order to hamper the detrimental repercussions (passivation and dendrite formations) of aqueous solvents. Similarly, there is dire need to work around the safety concerns of nonaqueous MAB systems to prevent risky short-circuiting or thermal runaways. It is only after the consensus on the handful of the most widely accepted designs of MABs that these systems can become powerful and reliable enough to serve in large scale vehicular and GSES applications with long and stable cycles.

# 9. HYDROGEN GAS BATTERIES

Hydrogen gas has shown great potential as one of the lightest and cleanest sources of chemical energy for replacing traditional fossil fuel on its merits of renewability, abundance, and high energy density.[706] In the twentieth century, great efforts have been devoted toward the development of highly efficient and regenerative hydrogen-oxygen fuel cells to power EVs and space power systems.[707] However, such fuel cells offer two severe problems, hindering their further development for LSES applications. These include relatively short lifetime predominantly because of engineering issues and the instability of the oxygen electrodes, in which even the best catalysts (for instance, Pt) are insufficient in relation to electrochemical reduction.[707]

In this regard, the earliest concept of a  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  battery presents a brilliant alternative to deal with the stability problems of the oxygen regenerating electrodes by replacing them with a stable solid electrode of nickel hydroxide  $(\mathrm{Ni(OH)_2})^{708}$ . In the 1970s, a  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  battery was developed and employed for aerospace applications owing to its high reliability, long-term stability, and long cycle life.[707] Unfortunately, the original  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  battery could not be widely used in most commercial applications, mainly due to the use of expensive Pt catalysts and the complex cell manufacturing. Regardless, the robustness of  $\mathrm{H_2}$  gas electrodes has triggered rigorous efforts to explore cost-effective

battery systems with good electrochemical performance for LSES applications. Recently, Cui and co-workers redesigned  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  and  $\mathrm{Mn - H_2}$  gas battery systems by matching the  $\mathrm{H_2}$  gas anode with  $\mathrm{Ni(OH)_2 / NiOOH}$  and  $\mathrm{Mn^{2 + } / MnO_2}$  cathodes, respectively, showing superior electrochemical performance and low cost for GSES applications.[56,709] Afterward, a series of rechargeable HGBs have been proposed and developed. Therefore, the renaissance of HGBs has enlightening prospects in LSES applications. In the following sections, we discuss the recent advancements and future prospects of HGBs predominantly for LSES applications.

# 9.1. The Working Principle of HGBs

Figure 86 shows a schematic illustration of a typical HGB along with its key components (anode, cathode, electrolyte, and separator). Concerning the anode, highly active catalysts on the gas diffusion layer (GDL) provide pathways for the active redox reactions: HER and hydrogen oxidation reaction (HOR). In order to comply with reversibility during the charge/discharge of HGBs, the anode catalysts are required to show bifunctional characteristics for both HER and HOR redox reactions. In this regard, a number of electrocatalysts have been studied as excellent candidates for enhancing the electrochemical reactions of the anode, which are fundamentally divided into three categories: noble metal, non-noble metal, and carbon based materials.[710-716] During charging, the  $\mathrm{H}_{2}$  gas is released from the anode, while, conversely, the hydrogen gas is oxidized on the anode during discharging. Owing to high reversibility and low overpotential of bifunctional catalysts during HER and HOR, the HGBs show ultrafast and long-term stable electrochemical performance.[717] In relation to the cathode, the materials that have received considerable attention include metallic oxides and hydroxides, lithium intercalation compounds, sodium intercalation compounds, organic molecules, and liquid redox molecules, to name a few.[56,709,718-723] All of these cathode materials give rise to unique kinds of battery systems involving their own characteristic electrochemical reactions occurring on the cathode. The separators are designed to insulate the cathode and anode while providing long-term stability of the electrolyte in different circumstances.

![](images/3843eebaa52c2311866b64206c05d650800ea9cc6016e563c8a0c900c245b1af.jpg)

Copyright 1982 American Institute of Aeronautics and Astronautics. (c) Discharge curve of traditional  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  battery under a discharge current of  $20\mathrm{A}$ . Reproduced with permission from ref 737. Copyright 1988 Elsevier. (d) Long cycling of traditional  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  battery for typical EOCV and EODV on a standard  $55\mathrm{min}$  charge and  $35\mathrm{min}$  discharge. Reproduced with permission from ref 731. Copyright 1996 American Institute of Aeronautics and Astronautics.

# 9.2. The Development of HGBs

9.2.1. Traditional  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  Battery. In the early 1970s, the  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  battery was developed as a spin-off of the hydrogen-oxygen regenerative fuel cells and applied for powering EVs.[724] Meanwhile, a number of practical storage cells acquired extensive support from the National Aeronautics and Space Administration (NASA).[725,726] The traditional  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  battery was fabricated at COMSAT laboratories for aerospace applications.[724] In addition, the NTS-2 spacecraft was launched in 1977, the first satellite of its kind to utilize the  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  technology for its excellent performance.[724] Figure 87a shows a digital photograph of the traditional  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  battery, which was manufactured and employed by NASA for highly reliable space missions for over 30 years.[727] In the practical  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  battery assembly, many  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  single cells are electrically connected in a stack by repeating units to build up a high capacity, as shown in Figure 87b. The separators, derived from fuel cell technology, connect the hydrophilic surface of the Pt electrocatalyst-based  $\mathrm{H_2}$  gas anode with a back-to-back Ni cathode pair. Further, the hydrophobic side of the  $\mathrm{H_2}$  gas electrode is in contact with a plastic screen that allows  $\mathrm{H_2}$  gas to flow freely to the catalytic sites on the anode. With regards to the electrode materials for the traditional  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  batteries, the cathode materials and Pt-based  $\mathrm{H_2}$  gas anode are adopted from aerospace Ni-Cd batteries and hydrogen-oxygen fuel cells, respectively.[728]

The traditional  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  battery, with the  $\mathrm{O}_2$  being stored in the  $\mathrm{Ni(OH)}_2$  cathode and  $\mathrm{H}_{2}$  gas being stored in a high-pressure gas vessel, shows long life and high reliability without periodic refueling and servicing, the typical needs of fuel cells.[729] The redox reactions of the  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  battery during cycles can be shown as follows:

Cathode:

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathrm {N i O O H} + \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} + \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {N i} (\mathrm {O H}) _ {2} + \mathrm {O H} ^ {-} \\ E ^ {\circ} = 0. 4 9 \mathrm {V} \tag {51} \\ \end{array}
$$

Anode:

$$
2 \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} + 2 \mathrm {e} ^ {-} \leftrightarrow \mathrm {H} _ {2} + 2 \mathrm {O H} ^ {-} \quad E ^ {\circ} = - 0. 8 3 \mathrm {V} \tag {52}
$$

Overall:

$$
2 \mathrm {N i O O H} + \mathrm {H} _ {2} \leftrightarrow 2 \mathrm {N i} (\mathrm {O H}) _ {2} \quad E ^ {\circ} = 1. 3 2 \mathrm {V} \tag {53}
$$

Upon overcharging, the  $\mathrm{O}_2$  gas produced on the cathode recombines with  $\mathrm{H}_2$  gas to form water, thereby avoiding a battery hazard in case of overcharge.[728] Another advantage of the  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  batteries is low self-discharge due to its extremely slow reaction kinetics between  $\mathrm{H}_2$  gas anode and Ni-based cathode materials. Moreover, the high power density for supporting fast charge/discharge, high reliability, and long cycling lifetime are other supplementary yet fundamental

![](images/f569448b8e54a096a5baac86eccb1c2e800c488d6035068c220ce1dc8ceb0d08.jpg)  
a

![](images/aa3b716978dcc0530f1bfe2e3a1bc92d98a7f008d014606a0e429d425a945030.jpg)

![](images/19e818bb5ee691762884b1de47953d9106f2419dea05e7ea7e0004236a37326f.jpg)

![](images/a6597ce526e2134dca15808fdbf97c9e14a0acce34b3dc9ad91f35611f058417.jpg)

![](images/1f800341471e0341ebdcc4f86aad69d64bc3e36ff6a04199026cb09606245227.jpg)

![](images/78a15710af3b75878fcbf2b86d1cc67b35e81d1d8332f23c209bde8a16d4f49f.jpg)

![](images/451c9ca8874cadff478b0d3d591068c32ca3766ca1f787c6b9fb4e033132fb27.jpg)

![](images/fc3f953c9ac1fa206dd22232208dbe7560c5085887df069faa6c62625e0e07b2.jpg)

![](images/9d8ff44ce3d418f878e84aa1458f12c4c32bbce26c2fcc977e479ac7a806e8c5.jpg)  
Figure 88. Low-cost  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  battery based on 3D NiMoCo catalyst. (a) Schematic illustration of the low-cost  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  cylindrical battery and electrode configuration. (b) Low magnification SEM (scale bar  $= 100\mu \mathrm{m}$ ) and high magnification SEM (inset, scale bar  $= 1\mu \mathrm{m}$ ) and (c) TEM of the 3D NiMoCo catalyst. (d) HER and (e) HOR polarization curves of the NiMoCo and Pt/C electrodes. (f) Stability of HER at a current density of  $20\mathrm{mA}$ $\mathrm{cm}^{-2}$  and (g) HOR at a constant potential of  $0.3\mathrm{V}$  of the NiMoCo and Pt/C electrodes. (h) Rate capability with the inset of galvanostatic charge/ discharge curves at different currents and (i) cycle stability of the low-cost  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  battery at a constant current of  $100\mathrm{mA}$  ( $\sim 5.56\mathrm{mA cm}^{-2}$ ). Reproduced with permission from ref 56. Copyright 2018 National Academy of Sciences.

![](images/730a6267a1926cd4d7b524a0376c78880fb91cc67ce147aa9836fc193b7ab83f.jpg)

advantages of such battery systems. $^{707,708,730}$  Figure 87c shows the discharge curve of the traditional  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  cell, which has a distinct discharge voltage plateau of  $\sim 1.25\mathrm{V}$ , indicating the electrochemical reaction of  $\mathrm{Ni(OH)_2}$  material with respect to the  $\mathrm{H}_{2}$  gas. $^{731}$  The lower plateau of  $\sim 0.8\mathrm{V}$  is ascribed to the accumulation of  $\mathrm{O}_2$  gas within the cell. In addition, the single  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  cell shows a high capacity of  $\sim 120\mathrm{Ah}$ , which is sought in high energy demand aerospace applications. As shown in Figure 87d, the end-of-charge voltage (EOCV) and end-of-discharge voltage (EODV) exhibit no obvious degradation even after about 48000 cycles, which demonstrates excellent cycling stability for these systems. $^{731}$  After extensive development and maturation in optimizing the electrolyte and modifying the state-of-the-art battery designs, the  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  batteries have reached further improvement in lifetime, attracting much deserved attention in launching satellites. $^{732-736}$

9.2.2. Low-Cost Ni–H₂ Battery. Pt-based electrocatalysts are most extensively employed for enhancing the redox activity of the H₂ gas anode in the traditional Ni–H₂ batteries. However, the high-cost Pt catalysts have hindered their further develop-

ment for practical LSES applications. $^{737}$  Alternatively, in contrast to the traditional  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  battery, Cui and co-workers have employed an earth-abundant 3D NiMoCo electrocatalyst in a high pressure  $\mathrm{H}_{2}$  gas anode for enhancing the HER/HOR activity and storage capacity of a  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  battery. $^{56}$  Figure 88a shows the schematic illustration of the low-cost NiMoCo-based cylindrical  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  cell along with its energy storage mechanism. Evident are the rolling  $\mathrm{Ni(OH)}_2$  as cathode, NiMoCo-catalyzed  $\mathrm{H}_{2}$  as anode,  $30\%$  KOH solution as electrolyte, and a polymer separator assembled into a steel vessel, enclosing high-pressure  $\mathrm{H}_{2}$  gas. $^{738}$  It is important to point out that the 3D NiMoCo catalyst is interconnected by many nanoparticles coated onto Ni foam, providing advanced redox electrocatalytic activity for the  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  battery (Figure 88b,c). To evaluate the activity of the catalysts, HER and HOR have been tested by a three-electrode configuration and two-electrode setup under  $\mathrm{H}_{2}$ -rich atmosphere, as shown in Figure 88d,e, respectively. It can be seen that the anodes based on Pt/C and NiMoCo exhibit significantly higher HER and HOR activity than that based on the nickel foam. In addition, the NiMoCo catalyst requires an over

![](images/5e2f8d76ec50c38e1f505c0f623fbc9b5d09bdfa3ab8c215514f787d65d18738.jpg)

![](images/e32c4b21cbcb25c8267e824b9d6a81137225dd35f9044e6e4e6619ffcb5404b8.jpg)

![](images/fc867a19d8d84db9382677e3f47cbc4abbdd8b86ddfef3ced894240682d0efc9.jpg)

![](images/810b4ea66f098f0c2f5eff3ef373c8bbf1200b05b02bdc76dd78b15bb57f9ee7.jpg)

![](images/9b9bf109f42f50e942b7e0c48495b1fa0bca75dfbfdb6203aa9539722ebc08c8.jpg)

![](images/6f8ac60afa74cfb2dfc4a07108fe3a97d72263dd0845e0735b7e8c319e6b8bfb.jpg)  
Figure 89.  $\mathrm{Mn - H_2}$  battery with cathode  $\mathrm{Mn^{2 + } / MnO_2}$  chemistry. (a) Schematic illustration of the  $\mathrm{Mn - H_2}$  battery in the charge and discharge modes. SEM images and EDX spectrum of (b) pristine CF, (c) CF after it was charged at  $1.6\mathrm{V}$  to  $1\mathrm{mAh}$ , and (d) CF after it was discharged at  $10\mathrm{mAcm}^{-2}$  to  $0.5\mathrm{V}$ . (e) Discharge curves at different current densities. (f) Rate capacities of  $\mathrm{Mn - H_2}$  battery. (g) Setup of the Swagelok-type  $\mathrm{Mn - H_2}$  cell. (h) Long-term stability tests of  $\mathrm{Mn - H_2}$  cell. (i) Schematic and digital photograph of the membrane-free cylindrical  $\mathrm{Mn - H_2}$  cell. (j) Cycling stability of the cylindrical cell with a charge capacity of  $15\mathrm{mAh}$ . Reproduced with permission from ref 709. Copyright 2018 Springer Nature.

![](images/d55bd1abd9a8380adf14bf89f85a758f7f1a465c9739d4dd0081c05fe5af9f28.jpg)

potential of  $\sim 80\mathrm{mV}$  to drive hydrogen generation at a current density of  $10\mathrm{mAcm}^{-2}$ , which is comparable to that of the Pt/C catalyst ( $\sim 71\mathrm{mV}$ ). In addition to the excellent HER performance, the NiMoCo catalyst shows outstanding long-term stability with negligible degradation of HER over  $1500\mathrm{h}$  at a current density of  $20\mathrm{mA cm}^{-2}$ , thereby outperforming the Pt/C catalyst with gradual activity degradation over  $800\mathrm{h}$  (Figure 88f). Figure 88e shows that the Pt/C and NiMoCo catalysts have negligible HOR activities under argon atmosphere and typical polarization curves with large current densities in  $\mathrm{H}_2$  gas, justifying their  $\mathrm{H}_2$  gas oxidation activities and the importance of  $\mathrm{H}_2$  in the  $\mathrm{Ni}-\mathrm{H}_2$  battery. The NiMoCo catalyst upon testing was found to have an onset potential of  $0\mathrm{V}$  toward HOR, similar to that of the Pt/C electrode, indicating its excellent HOR activity.

This further shows its superior cycling stability with minimal degradation over  $1000\mathrm{h}$ , as compared to that of  $\mathrm{Pt / C}$  (Figure 88g). Therefore, the outstanding HER/HOR activities of the NiMoCo catalyst render it an exceptional bifunctional  $\mathrm{H}_{2}$  gas catalyst for the low-cost  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  systems. Figure 88h,i show the electrochemical performance of the low-cost  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  cylindrical cell. The galvanostatic charge and discharge curves at a constant current of  $50\mathrm{mA}$  present distinct plateaus at about 1.55 and 1.25 V, respectively (Figure 88h). Furthermore, the cylindrical cell can achieve high discharge capacity of  $640\mathrm{mAh}$  (areal capacity of  $35.5\mathrm{mAhcm}^{-2}$ ), high CE of  $\sim 98.5\%$  and remarkable rate capability. Also, the  $\mathrm{Ni - H_2}$  cell exhibits a relatively high energy density of  $\sim 140\mathrm{Whkg^{-1}}$  and excellent cycling performance for 600 cycles at a current of  $100\mathrm{mA}$ , as shown in Figure 88i. The
